The Syllogism
intermediate
8 min
Division of Syllogisms and the Naming of Iqtirani
Author clarifies division basis and critiques naming of iqtirani.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the division of syllogisms into iqtirani and istithna'i is based on form rather than matter, and critique the standard etymology of the term 'iqtirani'.

Core Idea
The form of the conclusion determines whether a syllogism is conjunctive or conditional—not the matter.

This passage from the commentary tackles a core classification issue: what distinguishes a conjunctive (iqtirani) from a conditional (istithna'i) syllogism? The author insists it is a matter of form, not content. He then probes the etymology of 'iqtirani' and finds it problematic because the middle term is not a 'limit' of the conclusion. Understanding this helps you appreciate how careful logicians are with technical terms and classification criteria.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the division criterion
The passage says the primary division is based on whether the syllogism contains the 'form' (hay'a) of the conclusion. Matter (subject-predicate) is always present, so it cannot be a distinguishing factor. This means that every syllogism has matter, but only some have the conclusion's form embedded in the premises.
In istithna'i, the conclusion is explicitly stated as a condition or exception; in iqtirani, it is implicit.
2
Examine the etymology of 'iqtirani'
The term 'iqtirani' is said to come either from 'conjunction of the limits (hudud) of the desired conclusion' or from containing the conjunction particle 'waw' (and). Note that 'hadd' means edge or limit, referring to the terms of a proposition.
The base text uses the first explanation.
3
Identify the problem with the first explanation
The middle term (hadd al-awsat) is not a limit of the conclusion, because the conclusion has only two terms. So calling the middle term a 'limit of the desired conclusion' is inaccurate. The commentator calls this 'laxity' (musamaha).
This shows how precision in terminology is critical in logic.
4
Evaluate the proposed solutions
Two solutions are offered: (1) Rephrase to say 'limits of the syllogism' (hudud al-qiyas) or use the dual number to refer only to the two terms that appear in the conclusion. (2) Use the rhetorical figure istikhdam, where 'limits' first means the conclusion's terms and then the syllogism's terms in a single expression.
Istikhdam is a subtle device that allows a word to carry two meanings in one context.
5
Understand the beginning of conditional divisions
The passage then starts to divide conditional syllogisms (sharti) into five types based on whether the premises are purely conditional or mixed with categorical. The enumeration is cut off, but it sets the stage for a comprehensive classification.
This division is important for analyzing complex arguments in Islamic theology and jurisprudence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۱۸) حيث ثبت هنا ان انقسام القياس الى الاستثنائى والاقترانى انما هو باعتبار اشتماله على الهيئة و عدمه و ذكر المادة لاعبرة به هنا لوجوب اشتمال القياس على المادة سواء كان استثنائياً ام اقترانياً. (میرزا محمد علی)
ثم انها سمي (۱۹) قوله لاقتران حدود المطلوب فيه : او لاشتماله على كلمة الجمع اعنى: الواو. الحدود بذلك ، لانها واقعة في طرف القضية و «الحد» في اللغة الطرف. (عبد الرحيم)
(۲۰) لا يخفى ما فيه من المسامحة فان الاوسط ليس بجد للمطلوب فالاولى ان يقول اولاً : لاقتران الحدود بالقطع عن الاضافة و يريد بها حدود القياس أو يأتي بصيغة التثنية ويترك ذكر الاوسط هنا. ويمكن ان يقال: المراد بالحدود اولاً حد المطلوب وبالضمير الراجع اليه ثانياً حدود القياس على طريقة الاستخدام. (18) Since it is established here that the division of the syllogism into conditional (istithna'i) and conjunctive (iqtirani) is only by virtue of its containing the form or not, and mention of the matter is of no account here because the syllogism must contain matter whether it is istithna'i or iqtirani. (Mirza Muhammad Ali) Then it is named (19) His statement: 'For the conjunction of the limits of what is sought (al-matlub) in it' or 'for its containing the word of conjunction, i.e., the waw (and).' The limits are so called because they lie at the edge of the proposition, and 'hadd' in language means 'edge'. (Abd al-Rahim) (20) It is not hidden that there is some laxity in it, for the middle term is not a limit of what is sought. So it is better that he first say: 'for the conjunction of the limits' (bi-l-qat' 'an al-idafa) and intend by it the limits of the syllogism, or he comes with the dual form and leaves mention of the middle term here. It can be said: what is meant by 'limits' first is the limit of the desired (al-matlub) and by the pronoun referring back to it second is the limits of the syllogism, by way of usage (istikhdam). (Mirza Muhammad Ali) (21) His statement: 'And otherwise it is conditional (sharti)' and its divisions are five: because it either consists of purely conditional (premises) or not. On the first, it either consists of two connectives (muttasilatan), or two disjunctives (munfasilatan), or two different ones. On the second, it either consists of a categorical and
Source pages p. 343

Commentary
This lesson collects several connected notes about the classification of syllogisms. Note (18) clarifies that the primary division between istithna'i and iqtirani is based on whether the syllogism includes the form (hay'a) of the conclusion or not. The matter (subject-predicate) is always present, so it is not a distinguishing factor. Note (19) explains the naming of 'iqtirani' (conjunctive): it is either from the conjunction of the terms of the desired conclusion, or because it contains the conjunction 'waw' (and). The term 'hadd' (limit) is used because it lies at the edge of a proposition. Note (20) criticizes this explanation: the middle term is not a 'limit' of the conclusion, so the definition is imprecise. The commentator suggests better phrasing: either use 'limits' for the syllogism's terms, or use the dual number. Alternatively, one can use the figure of speech known as 'istikhdam' (using a term in two senses) where 'limits' first refers to the conclusion's terms and then to the syllogism's terms. Note (21) begins the division of conditional (sharti) syllogisms into five types, based on whether the premises are all purely conditional or mixed with categorical. The enumeration is cut off at 'categorical and' on this page. The lesson thus provides the basis for distinguishing iqtirani from istithna'i and the beginning of a detailed classification of conditional syllogisms.

Key Terms
الاقتراني
الشرطي
الاستخدام
الحدود

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the key criterion for dividing syllogisms into istithna'i and iqtirani?
A) The presence or absence of matter.
B) The presence or absence of form (hay'a).
C) The number of premises.
D) The type of conclusion.

Key Takeaway
The division of syllogisms into iqtirani and istithna'i is a division of form, not content, and precise terminology is essential in logical classification.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Classify the following syllogism as iqtirani or istithna'i and justify your answer using the criterion from the passage: 'If the imam is just, his testimony is accepted; the imam is just; therefore his testimony is accepted.' (Answer: istithna'i because it has a conditional premise and the conclusion is explicitly stated as the consequent.)
?
Critique the etymology of 'iqtirani' as given in the base text: 'for the conjunction of the limits of what is sought.' What problem does the commentator identify, and how does he propose to fix it? Write your answer in 2-3 sentences.

Three Rational Divisions Under 'And Otherwise' in Istithna'i Qiyas
Lists three rational divisions under 'and otherwise'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the three rational divisions of how the 'other statement' appears in an istithna'i qiyas and identify which one is correct according to the author.

Core Idea
In Istithna'i qiyas, only the matter of the conclusion is mentioned, not its form.

When the author says 'and otherwise' (wa illā) in the definition of istithna'i qiyas, he implies that the conclusion (called 'the other statement') may relate to the qiyas in different ways. Our commentator explains that exactly three rational divisions arise: the conclusion's matter alone, its form alone, or neither. Which one actually occurs? Let's find out.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three divisions
The passage lists: first, the other statement is mentioned by its matter (material) not its form; second, by its form not its matter; third, by neither matter nor form.
These are the only logical possibilities given that the conclusion is a composite of matter and form.
2
Understand the basis
The conclusion is a composite; its negation can occur by negating one part or both. The first division corresponds to the negation of form only.
The author implies that in a valid istithna'i, only the matter (the terms) must be present in the premises, not the full conditional structure.
3
Recognize the correct division
The author states that only the first division (matter without form) is correct. The others are impossible.
This is established by subsequent arguments.
4
Note the reasons for the refutations
Form cannot exist without matter (like an accident requires a substratum). A qiyas cannot lack both because the conclusion must be generated from something in the qiyas.
These reasons are developed in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لما كان الشقوق العقلية المندرجة تحت قوله (والا) ثلاثة اقسام الأول ما ذكره المحشى و هو ما يكون القول الآخر مذكوراً بمادته لا هيئته والثاني عكس ذلك اعنى ان يكون مذكوراً بهيئته دون المادة والثالث انتفاء الأمرين :اعنى ان لا يكون مذكوراً بمادته و لا بهيئته و ذلك لانه قد اخذ في الاستثنائى مجموع الامرين معاً و انتفاء المركب تارة يصدق بانتفاء أحد الاجزاء و اخرى بانتفاء جميعها وكان الصحيح منها هو القسم الأول فقط، تصدى الى بيان عدم صحة الوجهين الاخيرين بعد بالوجه الاول بالذكر فقال: «اذلا يعقل...». حاصله انه لا يمكن وجود الهيئة بدون المادة، ضرورة استحالة وجود العارض بدون المعروض و كذا لا يمكن عدم اشتمال القياس على شيء من المادة والهيئة لان النتيجة هو القول المتولد من القياس فاذا لم يكن لها اسم ولا رسم في القياس فكيف يتولد منه ؟ ثم ربما يتوهم هنا: ان تعريف الاستثنائى غير مطرد و تعريف الاقتراني غير منعكس و ذلك لان قولنا : ، كل ج، ب و كل ب ب فكل ج ب يصدق عليه : ان القول الآخر مذكور فيه بمادته وهيئته مع أنه من اقسام الاقتراني. والجواب ماسبق اليه الاشارة قبيل هذا من انه ليس بقياس مطلقا، لان العلم بالنتيجة لابد ان يكون حاصلاً قبل العلم بالقياس وهنا حاصل قبله وايضاً قد تقدم آنفاً من ان المراد من كون المادة مذكورة، أن يكون طرفا النتيجة مذكورين لانفس النتيجة و هنا المذكور فى القياس نفس النتيجة دون مادتها فلا يكون داخلاً تحت واحد من القسمين فافهم. (ميرزا محمد علی)
When the rational divisions under his statement 'and otherwise' were three: first, what the commentator mentioned, which is that the other statement is mentioned by its matter (material) not its form; second, the opposite of that, i.e., that it is mentioned by its form without the matter; third, the negation of both, i.e., that it is mentioned neither by its matter nor by its form. That is because in the istithna'i qiyas both together are taken, and the negation of a composite sometimes occurs by negation of one part and sometimes by negation of all. The correct among them is only the first division. He proceeded to explain the invalidity of the last two after the first by mentioning, saying: 'for it is not conceivable...' Its summary is that form cannot exist without matter, as it is impossible for an accident to exist without a substratum, and similarly, it is impossible for a qiyas to lack both matter and form, because the conclusion is the statement generated from the qiyas; if it has neither name nor trace in the qiyas, how can it be generated from it? Then perhaps it is mistakenly thought here that the definition of istithna'i is not comprehensive (inverse) and the definition of iqtirani is not exclusive (converse), because our statement 'Every J is B and every B is F, so every J is F' applies to it that the other statement is mentioned in it by its matter and form, while it is from the divisions of iqtirani. The answer is what was indicated prior to this: that it is not a qiyas at all, because knowledge of the conclusion must be obtained before knowledge of the qiyas, and here it is obtained before it. Also, it has been stated recently that by 'matter being mentioned' is meant that the two terms of the conclusion are mentioned, not the conclusion itself, and here what is mentioned in the qiyas is the conclusion itself, not its matter, so it does not fall under either of the two divisions. So understand.
Source pages p. 343

Commentary
This passage refutes two of the three rational divisions of the istithna'i qiyas. The author first lists the three divisions based on how the 'other statement' (the conclusion's form or matter) appears. The first is when the matter (material terms) is mentioned but not the form (logical structure). The second is the opposite (form without matter). The third is neither. The author argues that only the first is correct because form cannot exist without matter (as an accident requires a substratum), and a qiyas cannot lack both because the conclusion must be generated from it; if it has no trace, it cannot generate. Then an objection is raised: an example like 'Every J is B, every B is F, so every J is F' might be considered istithna'i because the conclusion is present in the qiyas (as the other statement). The answer is that this example is not a qiyas at all because the conclusion is already known beforehand, so it fails the condition that the qiyas should produce new knowledge. Also, the matter mentioned must be the terms of the conclusion, not the conclusion itself. Therefore, such an example does not fall under the division. For instance, in fiqh: 'Every intoxicant is haram, every nabidh is intoxicant, so every nabidh is haram' – here the conclusion 'nabidh is haram' is not explicitly stated in the premises; only the terms appear.

Key Terms
هيئة
مادة
قول آخر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How many rational divisions under 'and otherwise' does the author list for the appearance of the other statement in istithna'i qiyas?
A) Two
B) Three
C) Four
D) One

Key Takeaway
The three divisions are matter alone, form alone, or neither; only matter alone is correct.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the following istithna'i qiyas: 'If zakat is obligatory, then charity on wealth is required. Zakat is obligatory. Therefore, charity on wealth is required.' Identify the matter (terms) that appear in the premises and the form that does not appear.
?
In a Barbara categorical syllogism: 'Every A is B, every B is C, so every A is C,' does the conclusion appear by matter, form, or both? According to the division, would this fall under the first, second, or third division? Why?

The Syllogism
advanced
8 min
Refutation of the Impossible Divisions and Answer to an Objection
Refutes two divisions and answers objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the second and third divisions of istithna'i qiyas are impossible and respond to the objection that some categorical syllogisms would incorrectly be classified as istithna'i.

Core Idea
Form cannot exist without matter, and a qiyas without both cannot generate a conclusion.

Having listed the three divisions, the author proceeds to refute the second and third. He argues that the second (form without matter) is impossible because logical form is an accident that requires matter as a substratum. The third (neither) is impossible because a qiyas that contains no trace of the conclusion cannot produce it. Then he tackles an objection: a categorical syllogism like 'Every J is B, every B is F, so every J is F' seems to have the conclusion fully present (both matter and form) in the premises, potentially blurring the definition. The answer clarifies a crucial condition.

Guided Path
1
Understand the impossibility of form without matter
The author argues that form (hay'a) is an accident ('ārid) that cannot exist without a substratum (ma'rūḍ), which is the matter. So the second division is impossible.
This is a metaphysical principle: accidents depend on substances.
2
Understand the impossibility of lacking both
If a qiyas contains neither the matter nor the form of the conclusion, then the conclusion has no trace in the qiyas, so it cannot be generated from it. The conclusion must be somehow present to be produced.
This is a logical principle: ex nihilo nihil fit.
3
Identify the objection
An objector might think that a categorical syllogism like 'Every J is B, every B is F, so every J is F' has the conclusion mentioned by both matter and form in the premises (since the premises contain the terms and the form is the syllogistic structure), yet it is not istithna'i.
This would make the definition of istithna'i not exclusive (in'ikās) and the definition of iqtirani not comprehensive (intirād).
4
Analyze the author's answer
The author gives two responses. First, such a syllogism is not a qiyas at all because the conclusion is already known before the qiyas (it is trivial). Second, 'matter being mentioned' means the terms of the conclusion are mentioned, not the conclusion itself. In the categorical syllogism, the conclusion itself is explicitly stated, so it is not a case of matter being mentioned.
Thus it does not fall under the first division either; it is excluded entirely.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(ميرزا محمد علی)

The Syllogism
beginner
5 min
Basis of Dividing Qiyas into Istithna'i and Iqtirani
Author clarifies that the division is based on inclusion of istithna', not on the matter.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between istithna'i and iqtirani qiyas based on the presence or absence of an exception tool.

Core Idea
The division hinges solely on the exception tool, not on the matter.

This passage corrects a common misconception about the division of qiyas. Many students think that the presence of matter (content) determines the type, but the author emphasizes that both types always include matter. The only distinguishing feature is whether the syllogism contains a tool of exception (like 'except' or 'but'). Understanding this criterion is essential for correctly classifying and analyzing syllogisms in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the common misunderstanding
The passage indicates that some people mistakenly think the division is based on whether the matter is mentioned. Recognize that this is the confusion the author addresses.
The matter refers to the terms and propositions forming the content of the syllogism.
2
Extract the author's criterion
The author states that the division is 'only by consideration of its inclusion of [the exception tool] and its absence'. So the criterion is the presence or absence of an exception tool like 'illā or lakin.
Exception tools are words that signal an exceptional condition.
3
Apply the criterion to examples
Take a conditional syllogism: 'If it is dawn, then prayer time has entered; it is dawn, so prayer time has entered.' It contains 'if...then' which is a conditional, but also the istithna' tool is the affirmation of the antecedent. In Arabic, such syllogisms often use 'فإن' or 'لكن'. Compare with a categorical syllogism: 'Every intoxicant is haram; nabidh is an intoxicant; so nabidh is haram.' It has no exception tool.
Note that both examples have matter (terms and premises).
4
Explain why matter is irrelevant
The author notes that matter is necessary for both types: 'it is necessary that the qiyas includes matter whether it is istithna'i or iqtirani.' Therefore, it cannot be a distinguishing factor.
This reinforces that the only valid basis is the exception tool.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حيث ثبت هنا ان انقسام القياس الى الاستثنائى والاقترانى انما هو باعتبار اشتماله على و عدمه و ذكر المادة لاعبرة به هنا لوجوب اشتمال القياس على المادة سواء كان استثنائياً ام اقترانياً. (میرزا محمد علی)
Where it is established here that the division of qiyas into istithna'i and iqtirani is only by consideration of its inclusion of the exception tool and its absence, and mentioning the matter is not considered here because it is necessary that the qiyas includes matter whether it is istithna'i or iqtirani.
Source pages p. 343

Commentary
This brief passage corrects a potential misunderstanding. The division of qiyas into istithna'i (conditional) and iqtirani (conjunctive) is not based on whether the material (matter) is mentioned or not. Rather, it is based solely on whether the qiyas includes a tool of exception (like 'except' or 'but'). Both types necessarily have matter (terms and content). For example, the first-figure syllogism 'Every human is an animal, every rational is human, so every rational is animal' is iqtirani and has matter. The conditional 'If it is day, then it is light; but it is day, therefore it is light' is istithna'i and also has matter. The distinction is the presence of the exception tool in the latter.

Key Terms
استثنائي
اقتراني

B) The number of premises.
C) The quality (affirmative/negative) of the conclusion.
D) The quantity (universal/particular) of the premises.

Key Takeaway
The sole criterion for dividing qiyas into istithna'i and iqtirani is the presence or absence of an exception tool, not the matter.

Why the Conjunctive Syllogism is Called Iqtirani
Author discusses why the conjunctive qiyas is called iqtirani, with two different opinions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the precise reason for naming the conjunctive syllogism 'iqtirani' and explain why the original phrasing was imprecise and how it was corrected.

Core Idea
The name 'iqtirani' refers to the conjunction of all terms in the syllogism, not just the conclusion's terms.

In this passage, two commentators debate the exact reason why the conjunctive syllogism (qiyas iqtirani) is given that name. The original wording mentioned 'the limits of the desired conclusion,' but a critic points out that the middle term is not part of the conclusion. This lesson walks through the problem and the elegant solutions proposed.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two opinions
The first note gives two possible reasons: (1) because the terms of the desired conclusion are conjoined in it, or (2) because it contains the conjunction 'waw' (and). Understand each.
2
Analyze the critique
The second note objects to the first reason: the middle term is not a limit of the conclusion, so saying 'limits of the desired conclusion' is imprecise. Recognize why this is a logical issue—the middle term is essential to the syllogism but not part of the conclusion.
In a first-figure syllogism, the middle term connects the subject and predicate of the conclusion, but it does not appear in the conclusion itself.
3
Understand the proposed corrections
The second note offers three better formulations: (a) say 'conjunction of the limits' absolutely, meaning the limits of the entire qiyas (including the middle term); (b) use the dual form (limits of the two premises or the limits of the conclusion's subject and predicate) and omit the middle; or (c) interpret the original phrase via 'istikhdam' (using a word in two senses) so that 'limits' first refers to the conclusion's terms and second to the qiyas's terms.
The istikhdam explanation is a rhetorical device that allows the same word to mean different things in the same context.
4
Apply to an example
Take an Islamic syllogism: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden; every nabidh is an intoxicant; therefore every nabidh is forbidden.' The limits (ḥudūd) are 'intoxicant' (middle), 'forbidden' (major), and 'nabidh' (minor). The name iqtirani refers to the conjunction of these three terms in the premises.
This example shows that the middle term is indeed a limit of the qiyas, even if not of the conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله لاقتران حدود المطلوب فيه : او لاشتماله على كلمة الجمع اعنى: الواو. الحدود بذلك ، لانها واقعة في طرف القضية و «الحد» في اللغة الطرف. (عبد الرحيم) لا يخفى ما فيه من المسامحة فان الاوسط ليس بجد للمطلوب فالاولى ان يقول اولاً : لاقتران الحدود» بالقطع عن الاضافة و يريد بها حدود القياس أو يأتي بصيغة التثنية ويترك ذكر الاوسط هنا. ويمكن ان يقال: المراد بالحدود اولاً حد المطلوب وبالضمير الراجع اليه ثانياً حدود القياس على طريقة الاستخدام. (ميرزا محمد علی)
His statement: 'because of the conjunction of the limits (terms) of the desired [conclusion] in it' or 'because it includes the word of conjunction, i.e., the waw.' The limits are so called because they are located at the ends of the proposition, and 'limit' in language means edge/extremity. (Abd al-Rahim) It is not hidden that there is some laxity in this, for the middle term is not a limit of the desired conclusion. So it is better to say first: 'because of the conjunction of the limits' without any addition, meaning the limits of the qiyas, or to use the dual form and leave out the middle term here. It is possible to say: By 'limits' first is meant the limit of the desired [conclusion], and by the pronoun referring back to it secondly is meant the limits of the qiyas, by way of usage (tawr al-istikhdam). (Mirza Muhammad Ali)
Source pages p. 343

Commentary
This passage consists of two marginal notes on the reason for the name 'iqtirani'. The first note (by Abd al-Rahim) mentions two possible reasons: (1) because the terms (limits) of the desired conclusion are conjoined in it, or (2) because it contains the word of conjunction 'waw' (and). He clarifies that 'limit' (hadd) means extremity, as terms are at the ends of propositions. The second note (by Mirza Muhammad Ali) critiques the first: it is imprecise to say 'conjunction of the limits of the desired [conclusion]' because the middle term is not a limit of the conclusion. He suggests better formulations: either say 'conjunction of the limits' absolutely (meaning the limits of the qiyas, i.e., the two terms of the conclusion plus the middle term), or use the dual form to refer to the subject and predicate of the conclusion, leaving out the middle. Alternatively, one can interpret the phrase using 'usage' (istikhdam) where the first mention of 'limits' refers to one thing (the conclusion's limits) and the second to another (the qiyas's limits). For example, in a first-figure syllogism: 'Every A is B, every B is C, so every A is C' – the limits of the conclusion are A and C, but the qiyas includes B as well; the name iqtirani refers to the conjunction of all three limits.

Key Terms
اقتراني (iqtirānī)
حد (ḥadd)
استخدام (istikhdām)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the problem with saying 'because of the conjunction of the limits of the desired [conclusion]' as the naming reason for iqtirani?
A) The middle term is not a limit of the conclusion.
B) The conclusion has no limits.
C) The name iqtirani does not mean conjunction.
D) The waw is not a conjunction.

Key Takeaway
The conjunctive syllogism is named iqtirani because it conjoins all three terms (subject, predicate, and middle) in its premises, not just the conclusion's terms.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Consider the following syllogism: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden; every nabidh is an intoxicant; therefore every nabidh is forbidden.' List all three terms (ḥudūd) of this qiyas. Then explain why the name iqtirani is appropriate, referring to the conjunction of these terms.

Five Types of Conditional Qiyas
Enumeration of five conditional qiyas types

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to enumerate and distinguish the five types of conditional qiyas based on the composition of premises.

Core Idea
Conditional qiyas splits into five types: three pure-conditional and two mixed with categorical.

The author defines conditional qiyas (qiyas sharti) as a syllogism whose premises are conditional propositions. He then divides it into five types based on the composition of the two premises. Understanding these divisions is essential for recognizing valid argument forms in Islamic logic, especially when dealing with conditional reasoning in kalam and fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two main categories
The author first distinguishes between a conditional qiyas composed entirely of conditional premises (pure) and one that mixes a categorical premise with a conditional premise.
Pure: both premises are shartiyya; mixed: one is hamliyya and one is shartiyya.
2
Subdivide the pure conditional category
Within pure conditional, there are three possibilities: both premises are conjunctive conditionals (muttasila), both are disjunctive conditionals (munfasila), or one of each (different).
These correspond to types (1), (2), and (3).
3
Subdivide the mixed category
When one premise is categorical and the other is conditional, the conditional can be either conjunctive or disjunctive, giving two types.
Types (4) and (5).
4
Count the total divisions
Adding the subdivisions: 3 (pure) + 2 (mixed) = 5 total divisions.
5
Associate each type with a basic example
For type (1): 'If A then B, if B then C, so if A then C.' For type (2): 'Either A or B, not A, so B.' For type (3): 'If A then B, either B or C, so ...' For type (4): 'All A are B, if B then C, so if A then C.' For type (5): 'All A are B, either B or C, so ...'
Examples are illustrative; the passage promises details later.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: «والا فشرطى» و اقسامه خمسة، لانه اما ان يتركب من الشرطيات الصرفة ام لا و على الأول فاما ان يتركب من متصلتين او منفصلتين او مختلفتين وعلى الثانى فاما ان يتركب من حملية و متصلة او من حملية و منفصلة فهذه خمسة اقسام تأتي تفصيلاً مع امثلتها بعيد هذا انشاء الله تعالى (میرزا محمد علی)
His statement: 'and otherwise it is conditional' and its divisions are five. For it is either composed of purely conditional [premises] or not. On the first, it is either composed of two conjunctive conditionals, or two disjunctive conditionals, or different ones. On the second, it is either composed of a categorical and a conjunctive conditional, or a categorical and a disjunctive conditional. So these are five divisions, whose details with examples will come later, God willing. (Mirza Muhammad Ali)
Source pages p. 343

Commentary
This passage divides the conditional qiyas (qiyas sharti) into five types. The division is based on the composition of the two premises. If both premises are conditional (shartiyya), then there are three subcases: both are conjunctive (muttasila), both are disjunctive (munfasila), or one of each (different). If one premise is categorical (hamliyya) and the other conditional, then there are two subcases: the conditional is either conjunctive or disjunctive. Thus we have five divisions: (1) two conjunctive conditionals, (2) two disjunctive conditionals, (3) one conjunctive and one disjunctive, (4) categorical and conjunctive conditional, (5) categorical and disjunctive conditional. The author promises detailed examples later. For instance, (1) 'If A then B, if B then C, so if A then C.' (2) 'Either A is B or C is D, but not both; if not A, then C is D.'

Key Terms
شرطي
حملي
متصل
منفصل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Conditional qiyas is divided into five types based on whether both premises are conditional (three types) or one is categorical (two types).

Why a Qiyas Is Called Conditional
Author explains why the conditional qiyas is named 'conditional' and gives two interpretations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and explain the two main interpretations for why a qiyas is called 'conditional' (shartiyya) based on the commentaries.

Core Idea
A qiyas is named after its most prominent part, not necessarily its majority.

This passage explores a subtle naming issue in logic: why is a certain type of qiyas called 'conditional' when it may contain only one conditional premise? The commentators offer two broad interpretations: naming after the greatest part, and naming based on part-whole relations within the broader category of conditional syllogisms. Understanding this will sharpen your appreciation of how definitions and classifications work in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being explained
Find in the Arabic what exactly is being called 'conditional' (shartiyya). The passage refers to a specific qiyas, likely one whose major premise is conditional.
2
Extract the first interpretation
Shaykh Abd al-Rahim states it is named after its 'greatest part' (a'zam ajza'ih). This means the conditional premise is the most important or predominant component, so the whole qiyas is named after it.
3
List the three possibilities from the second note
Mirza Muhammad Ali offers three interpretations: (1) it is called conditional because it is a lesser part (instance) of the broader category of conditional qiyas; (2) because it is one single division among many; (3) because it is a part that comes first in the conditional structure. He also hints that the first note's interpretation might be included.
These are not contradictory but highlight different aspects of the naming.
4
Compare the two notes
The first note focuses on predominance; the second expands to include part-whole relationships. The second note's third possibility ('part that precedes') is similar to the first note's idea that the conditional premise is the greatest part, as it often comes first.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تسميته حينئذ شرطية، باعتبار اعظم اجزائه. (شيخ عبد الرحيم) اى لكونه اقل اجزاء منه كما هو ظاهر. ويحتمل ان يكون الجهة فيه كونه قسماً واحداً بخلاف الشرطي فانه اقسام متعددة كما مر او كونه جزء من الشرطى مقدماً عليه، و يحتمل شمول العبارة للوجه الاول ايضاً فتأمل (ميرزا محمد على)
Its naming then as conditional (shartiyya) is due to the consideration of its greatest part. (Shaykh Abd al-Rahim) i.e., because it is the lesser part of it (the conditional), as is apparent. And it is possible that the aspect is that it is one single division, unlike the conditional which is many divisions as mentioned, or that it is a part of the conditional, preceding it. And it is possible that the expression covers the first aspect as well, so ponder. (Mirza Muhammad Ali)
Source pages p. 344

Commentary
This pair of notes discusses why a particular qiyas (perhaps one of the five divisions) is called 'conditional' (shartiyya). The first note (by Abd al-Rahim) says it is named after its greatest part, i.e., because the majority or most important premise is conditional. The second note (by Mirza Muhammad Ali) offers three interpretations: (1) it is called conditional because it is the lesser part (i.e., a specific instance) of the broader category of conditional qiyas; (2) because it is a single division among many divisions of the conditional; (3) or because it is a part of the conditional that comes first (as a component). The author suggests that the expression might also include the first interpretation. For example, in qiyas 'If A then B, but A, so B', the major premise is conditional, and the whole is named after it.

The Universal Affirmative: Noblest but Not Always
Author comments that the universal affirmative is the noblest of demands and may be equal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a universal affirmative conclusion in the first figure is superior and when it may be equal to other propositions.

Core Idea
The universal affirmative is usually the noblest conclusion, but it may sometimes be equal.

This short note from Abd al-Rahim's hashiya alerts us to a subtle point: the universal affirmative is praised as the noblest of demands, but the author qualifies it with 'in most cases'. Why the caution? Because under certain conditions, the universal affirmative can be equal in nobility to other proposition types. Let's explore this nuance and its implications for evaluating syllogistic conclusions in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Understand 'noblest of demands'
Recognize that in classical logic, the universal affirmative (e.g., 'Every A is B') is considered the most informative and valuable conclusion because it affirms the predicate for every individual of the subject.
This is a value judgment based on epistemic yield.
2
Recall first figure conclusions
The first figure can produce four types: universal affirmative (Barbara), universal negative (Celarent), particular affirmative (Darii), particular negative (Ferio). Each has different logical strength.
3
Identify when universal affirmative equals others
The author notes that sometimes the universal affirmative may be 'equal'—for instance, when the subject and predicate are co-extensive (e.g., 'Every human is rational'), the universal negative or particular would be equally informative or less? Actually equality here likely refers to the value of the conclusion, not quantity. The nuance is that in some contexts, other propositions can be as valuable (e.g., when the universal affirmative is trivially true).
Equality here means the superiority is not absolute.
4
Reflect on the author's caution
The author uses 'in most cases' (ghalib) to avoid overstatement. This reflects the careful nature of logical discourse—qualifications matter. The universal affirmative is typically the strongest, but exceptions exist.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هذا فى الموجبة الكلية التى هى اشرف المطالب و انما قال في الغالب، اذ قديكون مساوياً. (عبدالرحيم)
This is in the universal affirmative, which is the noblest of demands, and he only said 'in most cases', because sometimes it may be equal. (Abd al-Rahim)
Source pages p. 344

Commentary
This short passage notes that a previous statement (likely about the superiority of the first figure) applies specifically to the universal affirmative mood (Barbara), which is the most noble type of conclusion. The author (Abd al-Rahim) points out that the phrase 'in most cases' is used because sometimes the conclusion may be equal (i.e., in terms of quantity, not superiority). For example, in the first figure, the conclusion can be universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, or particular negative; but the universal affirmative is considered the noblest because it affirms the predicate for all individuals of the subject, e.g., 'Every act of injustice is forbidden.'

Why the First Figure Is Superior
Author lists reasons for the superiority of the first figure over other figures.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three reasons the first figure is considered the best and explain why it is self-evident while others are theoretical.

Core Idea
The first figure's conclusion follows naturally without conversion, unlike other figures.

This passage from Yazdi's Hashiya explains why the first figure (shakl awwal) of the categorical syllogism is the most perfect. The author lists three advantages: immediate production, natural order, and ability to yield all four types of propositions. Understanding these reasons helps you appreciate why the first figure is foundational in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three reasons
In the Arabic, locate the phrases 'وذلك لان' (the first reason), 'وايضاً' (second reason), and another 'وايضاً' (third reason). These mark the three advantages of the first figure.
2
Understand the self-evident nature
The first reason states that the major premise indicates the ruling applies to everything that has the middle term, including the minor term directly. Thus, no additional thought or conversion is needed—the conclusion is immediate (badihi). Other figures require mental effort (nazari).
Self-evident here means the conclusion follows directly without external steps.
3
Explain natural order and productivity
The second reason: the first figure follows the natural order of reasoning—from subject (minor term) to middle term to predicate (major term). The third reason: it can produce all four quantified propositions (universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative), while other figures cannot.
This productivity is demonstrated later in the text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و ذلك لان الكبرى تدل على ثبوت الحكم لكل ما ثبت له الوسط ومن جملته الاصغر فلا حاجة فى انتاجه الى فكر و استعمال روية و هذا بخلاف البواقى فلذا كان انتاجها نظرياً وايضاً هو على النظم الطبيعي لان الانتقال فيه من الاصغر الذى هو موضوع المطلوب الى الاوسط ومنه الى الاكبر الذي هو محمول فيلزم الانتقال من موضوع المطلوب على محموله وأيضاً هو منتج للمحصورات الاربع كما سيأتي بخلاف البواقي (محمد علی) و ذلك لان الاوسط محمول فى كل واحد من صغريها وايضاً مشترك فى الكبرى ايضاً حيث الكمية فان كليتهما شرط في كليهما كما سيأتي. انما كانت الصغرى اشرف المقدمتين، لاشتمالها على موضوع المطلوب الذى هو اشرف من المحمول و دلالته على الصفة ولا ريب في تبعية الصفة و ثانويته بالنسبة الى الذات (میرزا محمد علی)
And that is because the major premise indicates the affirmation of the ruling for everything for which the middle term is affirmed, and among them is the minor term, so there is no need in its production for thought and deliberation, unlike the others, so their production is theoretical. Also, it is in the natural order, because the transition in it is from the minor term, which is the subject of the desired [conclusion], to the middle term, and from it to the major term, which is the predicate, so the transition from the subject of the desired to its predicate follows. Also, it produces all four [quantified] conclusions, as will come, unlike the others. (Muhammad Ali) And that is because the middle term is a predicate in both of its two [premises]? Actually, it is common in the major also with respect to quantity, for their universality is a condition in both, as will come. The minor premise is more noble than the major because it includes the subject of the desired, which is more noble than the predicate, and its indication of the attribute; and there is no doubt about the dependence of the attribute and its secondariness relative to the essence. (Mirza Muhammad Ali)
Source pages p. 344

Commentary
This passage combines two notes that explain why the first figure (shakl awwal) is the best and most perfect syllogistic figure. The first note lists three reasons: (1) The major premise is such that the ruling applies to everything that has the middle term, including the minor term; thus the conclusion is immediate and does not require additional thought (it is self-evident). Other figures require conversion, so their production is theoretical. (2) It follows the natural order: from subject (minor term) to middle term to predicate (major term), which is the natural progression in reasoning. (3) The first figure can yield all four types of categorical propositions (universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative), while other figures cannot. The second note adds that the minor premise is more noble than the major because it contains the subject (the essence) which is more noble than the predicate (the attribute). For example, in 'Every human is rational, every rational is a body, so every human is body', the minor premise 'Every human is rational' has the subject 'human' (the essence) and the predicate 'rational' (an attribute). The essence is primary, the attribute secondary.

Key Terms
شكل أول
نظري
محصورات أربع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does the first figure not require thought to produce its conclusion?
A) Because the middle term is the subject in both premises.
B) Because the major premise indicates the ruling for everything that has the middle term, including the minor term.
C) Because the minor premise is more noble than the major.
D) Because it produces all four types of propositions.

Key Takeaway
The first figure's superiority lies in its self-evident conclusion, natural order, and ability to produce all four categorical propositions.

Why al-Farabi and Ibn Sina Rejected the Fourth Figure
Author comments on the inferiority of a figure and the invalidity of the fourth figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the reason for the rejection of the fourth figure and identify its structural flaw.

Core Idea
The fourth figure differs from the first in both premises, making it unnatural.

In this gloss on a classical logic text, the commentator discusses a phrase indicating the inferiority of a certain figure. Another note explains why prominent logicians like al-Farabi and Ibn Sina omitted the fourth figure altogether. This lesson unpacks their reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the figure being discussed
Look for references to 'the less and lower of them' (أنقصهما وأدناهما) which indicates the fourth figure.
The fourth figure is often considered the least natural.
2
Understand the reason for rejection
The passage states: 'because it differs from it in both premises' (لمخالفته اياه فى كلتا المقدمتين). This means the arrangement of terms is reversed compared to the first figure.
In the first figure, the middle term is subject in the major and predicate in the minor; in the fourth, it is predicate in the major and subject in the minor.
3
Connect to the authority of al-Farabi and Ibn Sina
Al-Farabi and Ibn Sina omitted the fourth figure from consideration entirely. This implies they did not consider it a valid figure for natural inference.
Some later logicians even omitted the division that includes the fourth figure.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ای انقصهما و ادناهما. (عبد الرحيم) و ذلك لمخالفته اياه فى كلتا المقدمتين و لذا اسقطه الفارابي و الشيخ عن الاعتبار وبعضهم القسمة ايضاً . ميرزا محمد على
I.e., the less and lower of them. (Abd al-Rahim) And that is because it differs from it in both premises, and therefore al-Farabi and the Shaykh (Ibn Sina) omitted it from consideration, and some even omitted the division as well. (Mirza Muhammad Ali)
Source pages p. 344

Commentary
This brief passage consists of two notes. The first (Abd al-Rahim) explains a phrase 'أنقصهما وأدناهما' likely referring to a particular figure (probably the fourth) as 'the less and lower' of the figures. The second note (Mirza Muhammad Ali) explains that the fourth figure is considered inferior and even invalid because it differs from the standard figures in both premises (i.e., its arrangement is reversed compared to the first figure). Consequently, major logicians like al-Farabi and Ibn Sina (the 'Shaykh') omitted it from consideration entirely, and some even omitted the division (the classification of figures) that includes it. For example, the fourth figure has the middle term as predicate in the major and subject in the minor, which is unnatural; thus many logicians reject it.

The Syllogism
intermediate

Why the Minor Premise Must Be Affirmative in the First Figure
Author explains the necessity of an affirmative minor premise in the first figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the minor premise must be affirmative in a first-figure categorical syllogism and identify invalid syllogisms that violate this condition.

Core Idea
Affirmation of the minor premise is the bridge that transfers the ruling from the middle to the minor.

The first figure of the categorical syllogism has a strict condition: the minor premise must be affirmative. Why? Because the major premise gives a ruling about the middle term, and only if the minor premise affirms that the minor falls under the middle can that ruling be transferred. Understanding this condition is essential for constructing valid syllogisms in Islamic sciences such as fiqh and kalam.

Guided Path
1
Identify the roles of the terms in the first figure
In the first figure, the middle term is the subject of the major premise and the predicate of the minor premise. The minor term is the subject of the minor premise, and the major term is the predicate of the major premise. The major premise gives a ruling about the middle term.
This structure is often remembered as: middle is subject in major, predicate in minor.
2
Understand the transfer of the ruling
The goal is to apply the ruling from the major premise to the minor term. This requires that the minor premise affirmatively connects the minor term to the middle term. For example, if the major says 'Every middle is major', and the minor says 'Every minor is middle', then the conclusion 'Every minor is major' follows.
The ruling transfers because the minor is under the scope of the middle.
3
Test the condition with a negative minor premise
If the minor premise is negative, e.g., 'No minor is middle', then the minor term is not among the things for which the middle is affirmed. Consequently, the ruling about the middle does not apply to the minor. Example: 'Every intoxicant is haram' (major), 'No nabidh is intoxicant' (minor) → no conclusion about nabidh.
This shows why the minor must be affirmative for the syllogism to be valid.
4
Formulate the necessary condition
Therefore, for a valid first-figure syllogism, the minor premise must be affirmative. This is not arbitrary but logically necessary for the connection between terms.
This condition applies regardless of whether the major is affirmative or negative.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بين السبب فى هذا الشرط والذي يليه دون الاول اى ايجاب الصغرى اعتماداً على كونه معلوماً بالقياس اليهما كما هو ظاهر ونحن نذكره ايضاً لزيادة التوضيح والايضاح فنقول : انما اشترط ذلك ليتعدى الحكم من الاوسط الى الأصغر فان الحكم في الكبرى سلباً كان او ايجاباً على ما ثبت له الاوسط فلو كانت الصغرى سالبة لم يكن الاصغر مما ثبت له الاوسط ضرورة ان الحكم فيها على هذا التقدير بان الاوسط مسلوب عن الاصغر فلا يلزم من الحكم على الأوسط الحكم على الأصغر
He explained the reason for this condition and the one after it, without the first, i.e., the affirmation of the minor premise, relying on it being known by qiyas to both, as is apparent. And we mention it also for additional clarification and elucidation. We say: He only stipulated that so that the ruling may transfer from the middle to the minor. For the ruling in the major, whether negative or affirmative, applies to what the middle is established for. If the minor were negative, then the minor would not be among what the middle is established for, necessarily because the ruling in it on this assumption is that the middle is negated from the minor. So the ruling on the middle does not entail the ruling on the minor.
Source pages p. 344

Commentary
This passage explains a condition for the first figure, specifically that the minor premise must be affirmative. The author states that this condition is necessary so that the ruling (hukm) in the major premise can be transferred from the middle term to the minor term. In the first figure, the major premise gives a ruling about the middle term (e.g., 'Every middle is major'), and the minor premise connects the minor to the middle ('Every minor is middle'). If the minor were negative ('No minor is middle'), then the minor would not be among the things for which the middle is affirmed, so the ruling about the middle would not apply to the minor. For example, if the major is 'Every intoxicant is haram' (middle: intoxicant, major: haram) and the minor is 'No nabidh is intoxicant', then we cannot conclude that nabidh is haram because the middle is negated from the minor. So the minor must be affirmative for the syllogism to work.

شرط

Key Takeaway
A negative minor premise in the first figure severs the link between the minor term and the middle term, preventing the transfer of the ruling.

The Immediate Certainty of the First Figure
Author explains why first figure is self-evident and natural

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the first figure produces immediate certainty and identify its natural order.

Core Idea
The first figure's validity is self-evident because the major covers all instances of the middle.

The passage begins by extolling the first figure of the categorical syllogism as self-evident (badīhī), meaning its conclusion follows without the need for additional inference or deliberation. This is contrasted with the other three figures, whose validity requires theoretical reasoning. Understanding why the first figure is immediate is crucial for appreciating the foundational role it plays in logical argumentation within the Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the middle term's role
In the first figure, the middle term is predicate in the minor premise and subject in the major. This unique arrangement enables the direct transfer of the ruling from the major to the subject.
The middle term is the bridge between the two premises.
2
Examine the major premise's universal claim
The major premise asserts the ruling for everything that has the middle term. For example, 'Every intoxicant is forbidden' covers all intoxicants.
3
Connect the minor premise
The minor premise affirms that the subject (minor term) has the middle term. For instance, 'Wine is an intoxicant' places wine under the coverage of the major.
The minor must be affirmative for the connection to hold.
4
Recognize the conclusion's emergence
Because the subject is included in the major's scope, the conclusion about the subject follows without additional reasoning. This is why the first figure is called 'natural order' (al-niẓām al-ṭabīʿī).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و ذلك لان الكبرى تدل على ثبوت الحكم لكل ما ثبت له الوسط ومن جملته الاصغر فلا حاجة فى انتاجه الى فكر و استعمال روية و هذا بخلاف البواقى فلذا كان انتاجها نظرياً وايضاً هو على النظم الطبيعي لان الانتقال فيه من الاصغر الذى هو موضوع المطلوب الى الاوسط ومنه الى الاكبر الذي هو محمول فيلزم الانتقال من موضوع المطلوب على محموله وأيضاً هو منتج للمحصورات الاربع كما سيأتي بخلاف البواقي (محمد علی) (۲۷) و ذلك لان الاوسط محمول فى كل واحد من صغريها وايضاً مشترك فى الكبرى ايضاً حيث الكمية فان كليتهما شرط في كليهما كما سيأتي. انما كانت الصغرى اشرف المقدمتين، لاشتمالها على موضوع المطلوب الذى هو اشرف من المحمول و دلالته على الصفة ولا ريب في تبعية الصفة و ثانويته بالنسبة الى الذات (میرزا محمد علی) (۲۸) ای انقصهما و ادناهما. (عبد الرحيم) (۲۹) و ذلك لمخالفته اياه فى كلتا المقدمتين و لذا اسقطه الفارابي و الشيخ عن الاعتبار وبعضهم القسمة ايضاً . ميرزا محمد على (۳۰) بين السبب فى هذا الشرط والذي يليه دون الاول اى ايجاب الصغرى اعتماداً على كونه معلوماً بالقياس اليهما كما هو ظاهر ونحن نذكره ايضاً لزيادة التوضيح والايضاح فنقول : انما اشترط ذلك ليتعدى الحكم من الاوسط الى الأصغر فان الحكم في الكبرى سلباً كان او ايجاباً على ما ثبت له الاوسط فلو كانت الصغرى سالبة لم يكن الاصغر مما ثبت له الاوسط ضرورة ان الحكم فيها على هذا التقدير بان الاوسط مسلوب عن الاصغر فلا يلزم من الحكم على الأوسط الحكم على الأصغر. الا ترى ان الحق في قولنا: لاشيء من الانسان بحجر وكل حجر جماد السلب؟ ولو بدلنا الكبرى :بقولنا و كل حجر جسم كان الحق الايجاب والاختلاف دليل عدم لزوم النتيجة كما سيوضحه المحشى فى بيان شرايط الشكل الثاني (محمد على)
And that is because the major premise indicates the affirmation of the ruling for everything to which the middle term is affirmed, and among them is the minor, so there is no need in its production for thought and deliberation, and this is unlike the remaining [figures] for which production is theoretical. Also, it is upon natural order because the transition in it is from the minor, which is the subject of the sought conclusion, to the middle, and from it to the major, which is the predicate, so the transition from the subject of the sought conclusion to its predicate follows. Also, it produces the four quantified propositions as will come, unlike the remaining. (Muhammad Ali) (27) And that is because the middle is predicate in each of its two premises, and also it is common in the major in terms of quantity, for their universality is a condition in both as will come. The minor premise is the more honorable of the two premises because it contains the subject of the sought conclusion, which is more honorable than the predicate, and it indicates the attribute, and there is no doubt about the attribute being secondary and subordinate to the essence. (Mirza Muhammad Ali) (28) i.e., the lesser and lower of the two. (Abd al-Rahim) (29) And that is because it differs from it in both premises, so al-Farabi and the Shaykh [Ibn Sina] dropped it from consideration, and some also dropped the division. Mirza Muhammad Ali. (30) He explains the reason for this condition and the one following it, not the first, i.e., the affirmation of the minor, relying on its being known by comparison to them as is apparent, and we mention it as well for more clarification and explanation. We say: This condition was stipulated so that the ruling transfers from the middle to the minor. For the ruling in the major, whether negative or affirmative, is upon that to which the middle is affirmed. If the minor were negative, then the minor would not be among those to which the middle is affirmed, necessarily because the ruling in it on this assumption is that the middle is negated of the minor. So the ruling upon the middle does not entail the ruling upon the minor. Do you not see that the truth in our statement: 'No human is a stone, and every stone is an inanimate thing' is negation? And if we changed the major to 'every stone is a body', the truth would be affirmation, and the difference indicates the non-necessity of the result, as the commentator will clarify in the explanation of the conditions of the second figure. (Muhammad Ali)
Source pages p. 344

Commentary
This passage is a commentary explaining two key features of the first figure of the categorical syllogism: its immediate certainty and the necessary condition that its minor premise be affirmative. The author begins by noting that the first figure is self-evident (badi') because the major premise asserts the ruling for every instance of the middle term, and since the minor premise affirms that the middle term belongs to the subject (the minor), the conclusion follows without additional thought. This contrasts with other figures, which require theoretical reasoning. He adds that the first figure follows the natural order, moving from subject to middle to predicate. Then he explains that the minor premise is more honorable because it contains the subject of the conclusion, which is prior to the predicate. A marginal note clarifies that 'inferior' means the lesser of the two. Another note mentions that the fourth figure was dropped by al-Farabi and Ibn Sina because it differs in both premises. The main discussion then turns to the condition that the minor must be affirmative. The reason is that the major premise applies the ruling only to things that actually have the middle term. If the minor were negative, the subject would not be among those things, so the ruling would not transfer. The author gives an example: 'No human is a stone, and every stone is inanimate' would yield a negative conclusion ('No human is inanimate' is false; actually the correct conclusion would be negative but the example is meant to show that a negative minor prevents the transfer). He contrasts with an affirmative minor: 'Every stone is a body' with a stone as middle? The example is a bit confusing but illustrates the point: with an affirmative minor, the conclusion follows; with a negative, it does not. The passage ends with a reference to the second figure conditions.

الكبرى

The first figure's self-evidence arises from the major premise's universal coverage and the minor's affirmation of the middle, making the conclusion immediate.

Condition of Affirmative Minor in the First Figure
Author explains why the minor must be affirmative

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a first-figure syllogism violates the condition that the minor premise must be affirmative and explain why a negative minor invalidates the argument.

Core Idea
A negative minor premise breaks the transfer of the ruling from the middle term to the subject.

The second part of the passage addresses a crucial condition for the first figure: the minor premise must be affirmative. The author explains that if the minor were negative, the subject would not be among the things to which the middle term is affirmed, so the ruling in the major would not apply. This condition is essential for the logical validity of the syllogism and is illustrated with a contrasting example.

Guided Path
1
Recall the function of the major premise
The major premise applies the ruling to every instance that has the middle term. For example, 'Every stone is inanimate' says that inanimacy is true of all stones.
2
Consider what an affirmative minor does
An affirmative minor (e.g., 'This is a stone') places the subject under the set of things that have the middle term, allowing the major's ruling to transfer.
3
Imagine a negative minor
If the minor is negative (e.g., 'No human is a stone'), the subject is excluded from the set of things that have the middle term.
4
See the consequence
Because the subject is not covered by the major's scope, no conclusion about the subject can be drawn from the major. The ruling does not transfer.
5
Test with the passage's example
The author contrasts: 'No human is a stone, every stone is inanimate' gives a conclusion? Actually it yields negation, but if the major is changed to 'every stone is a body', the conclusion becomes affirmation. The difference shows that the result is not necessary, indicating invalidity when the minor is negative.

If the minor were negative, then the minor would not be among those to which the middle is affirmed, necessarily because the ruling in it on this assumption is that the middle is negated of the minor. So the ruling upon the middle does not entail the ruling upon the minor. Do you not see that the truth in our statement: 'No human is a stone, and every stone is an inanimate thing' is negation? And if we changed the major to 'every stone is a body', the truth would be affirmation, and the difference indicates the non-necessity of the result, as the commentator will clarify in the explanation of the conditions of the second figure. (Muhammad Ali)
Source pages p. 344

Commentary
This passage is a commentary explaining two key features of the first figure of the categorical syllogism: its immediate certainty and the necessary condition that its minor premise be affirmative. The author begins by noting that the first figure is self-evident (badi') because the major premise asserts the ruling for every instance of the middle term, and since the minor premise affirms that the middle term belongs to the subject (the minor), the conclusion follows without additional thought. This contrasts with other figures, which require theoretical reasoning. He adds that the first figure follows the natural order, moving from subject to middle to predicate. Then he explains that the minor premise is more honorable because it contains the subject of the conclusion, which is prior to the predicate. A marginal note clarifies that 'inferior' means the lesser of the two. Another note mentions that the fourth figure was dropped by al-Farabi and Ibn Sina because it differs in both premises. The main discussion then turns to the condition that the minor must be affirmative. The reason is that the major premise applies the ruling only to things that actually have the middle term. If the minor were negative, the subject would not be among those things, so the ruling would not transfer. The author gives an example: 'No human is a stone, and every stone is inanimate' would yield a negative conclusion ('No human is inanimate' is false; actually the correct conclusion would be negative but the example is meant to show that a negative minor prevents the transfer). He contrasts with an affirmative minor: 'Every stone is a body' with a stone as middle? The example is a bit confusing but illustrates the point: with an affirmative minor, the conclusion follows; with a negative, it does not. The passage ends with a reference to the second figure conditions.

Key Terms
الوسط
الصغرى
الكبرى
الشكل الأول

Key Takeaway
For a first-figure syllogism to be valid, the minor premise must be affirmative to ensure the subject is included under the middle term's scope, allowing the ruling to transfer.

Condition of Actuality in the First Figure Major Premise
Author explains the actuality condition for the first figure and the dispute over its meaning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine when a first-figure syllogism is invalid due to the major premise's actuality condition, and explain why possibility in the minor prevents a conclusion.

Core Idea
For the first figure, the major premise must judge the middle term as actual, not merely possible.

In the first figure of categorical syllogisms, the middle term is the subject of the major premise. The author explains a subtle condition: the major premise must apply to the middle term actually, not potentially. If the minor only affirms the middle as possible, the ruling in the major cannot transfer to the minor term because the major assumes actuality. This lesson unpacks this condition with an example from the tradition and examines a scholarly dispute about what 'actual' means.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition
Find the passage where the author states that the major premise's subject (the middle term) must be described actually, not potentially. This is the core condition for a valid first-figure syllogism.
The condition is stated at the beginning: 'the truth of the description of the subject upon itself is only actual.'
2
Analyze the example
Work through the example of Zayd's mount (horse) and 'Amr's mount (donkey). The major says 'All that is Zayd's mount is necessarily a horse' (actual). The minor says 'All that is 'Amr's mount is potentially Zayd's mount' (potential). Observe why the attempted conclusion fails: possibility does not entail actuality, so no transfer occurs.
The example demonstrates that even a universal major does not rescue the syllogism if the minor is only potential.
3
Understand the transfer condition
The ruling in the major applies to the middle term actually. If the minor only affirms the middle potentially, the middle is not actually present in the minor term, so the ruling cannot extend to the minor.
This is the logical mechanism: actuality is required for the middle term to serve as a connecting link.
4
Examine the dispute on interpretation
The author mentions two interpretations of 'actual': real actuality (according to the state of affairs) vs mental supposition (hypothetical). The author prefers real actuality, but notes an exception for mental propositions with no real instances.
This nuance matters because mental propositions (like mathematical concepts) might not satisfy real actuality, yet they are still used in syllogisms.
5
Apply to a new example
Construct a first-figure syllogism in fiqh: major 'Every intoxicant is forbidden', minor 'Nabidh is potentially intoxicating'. Explain why no conclusion follows using the actuality condition.
This shows the practical importance of the condition beyond theoretical logic.

(محمد على) (۳۲) حاصله انه اذا حكم فى الصغرى بثبوت الاوسط للاصغر بالامكان لم يتعد الحكم من الاوسط المحكوم عليه بالاكبر الى الاصغر لان الحكم بالاكبر على ما هو لوسط بالفعل و الاصغر اوسط بالامكان و الممكن يجوز بقائه تحت الامكان ابدأ بحيث لا يخرج إلى الفعل اصلاً. مثلاً لو فرض ان مركوب زيد بالفعل منحصر بالفرس و مركوب عمرو بالفعل منحصر بالحمار فح يصدق كل ماهو مركوب عمرو مرکوب زید بالامكان و كل ما هو مركوب زيد فهو فرس بالضرورة، ولا يصدق بعض ما هو مركوب عمرو فرس بالامكان لان كل مركوب عمرو حمار بالضرورة ولاشيء من الحمار بفرس بالضرورة. ثم لا يخفى ان هذا الشرط بناء على ماهو المشهور عند الجمهور من ان مراد الشيخ بالفعل انما هو بحسب نفس الأمر و اما على ما ذكره جمع من المحققين من ان المراد به الفعل بحسب الفرض العقلى سواء كان مطابقاً للواقع اولا، فلا ايضاً فان بقاء المحمول فى حيز الامكان لاينا فيه الفعل بحسب الفرض العقلي كما مر . ثم لا يخفى ايضاً : ان ظاهر المصنف هنا كغيره ان الفعلية المعتبرة في عقد الحمل انما يراد بها الفعلية بحسب الواقع كما ان الفعلية المعتبرة في عقد الوضع على مذهب الشيخ كذلك، على ما ذكره الجماعة وقد عرفت سابقاً ان هذا لا يجرى فى القضاياء الذهنية التي لم يوجدلها فرد في الواقع فتأمل. (ميرزا محمد على)
(31) And that is because the middle here is subject in the major, and it has preceded in the simple conversion that the Shaykh's doctrine, which the author chose, is that the truth of the description of the subject upon itself is only actual. (Muhammad Ali) (32) Its summary is that if it is judged in the minor that the middle is affirmed of the minor by possibility, the ruling does not transfer from the middle (which is judged by the major) to the minor, because the ruling by the major is upon what is actually the middle, while the minor is potentially the middle, and the possible may remain in possibility forever such that it never becomes actual. For example, if it is assumed that Zayd's mount is actually restricted to a horse, and 'Amr's mount is actually restricted to a donkey, then it is true: 'Everything that is 'Amr's mount is potentially Zayd's mount' and 'Everything that is Zayd's mount is necessarily a horse', but it is not true that 'Some of what is 'Amr's mount is potentially a horse' because every mount of 'Amr is necessarily a donkey, and no donkey is necessarily a horse. Then it is not hidden that this condition is based on what is famous among the majority that the Shaykh means by 'actual' is according to the real state of affairs. But according to what a group of investigators mentioned—that what is meant is actuality according to mental supposition, whether it corresponds to reality or not—then it is also not, for the remaining of the predicate in the realm of possibility does not entail actuality according to mental supposition as has passed. Then it is also not hidden that the apparent meaning of the author here, like others, is that the actuality considered in the copula is meant as actuality according to reality, just as the actuality considered in the subject-term according to the Shaykh's doctrine is likewise, according to what the group mentioned. And you have known previously that this does not apply to mental propositions that have no individual in reality, so reflect. (Mirza Muhammad Ali)
Source pages p. 345

Commentary
This passage discusses a subtle condition for the first figure: the major premise must attribute the middle term actually (bi'l-fi'l), not potentially. The author notes that according to Ibn Sina, the truth of a description applying to a subject requires actuality. If the minor premise only affirms the middle term potentially (bi'l-imkan), the ruling does not transfer because the major assumes the middle actually. He gives an example: Suppose Zayd's actual mounts are only horses, and 'Amr's actual mounts are only donkeys. Then it is true that 'Everything that is 'Amr's mount is potentially Zayd's mount' (since a donkey could potentially be a horse, though not actually) and 'Everything that is Zayd's mount is necessarily a horse'. But it does not follow that 'Some of 'Amr's mount is potentially a horse' because all his mounts are actually donkeys, which are not horses necessarily. Thus, possibility does not yield actuality. The commentator then notes a dispute: Does Ibn Sina's 'actual' mean real actuality or mental supposition? He favors real actuality, consistent with the author's approach, but notes that mental propositions (like mathematical objects) are an exception.

Condition for Conclusion in Second Figure: Judgment Does Not Transfer
Author explains why judgment on one differentiator does not entail judgment on the other, with example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a conclusion necessarily follows in the second figure of a categorical syllogism by checking whether the judgment on one distinct thing transfers to another.

Core Idea
In the second figure, the subject terms are distinct; a judgment on one does not transfer to the other unless the middle term properly connects them.

This passage clarifies a key condition for drawing conclusions in second-figure syllogisms. The author warns that just because we judge one thing (like 'man') relative to a middle term (like 'stone'), that judgment does not automatically apply to another distinct thing (like 'inanimate'). The example shows how changing the major premise alters the conclusion, proving that the conclusion is not forced unless the terms are arranged correctly.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two distinct subject terms in the premises
In the example, 'man' and 'stone' are the subjects of the two premises. They are distinct (mutabāyin) – they have no inherent logical connection. Note that the conclusion is supposed to relate 'man' and 'inanimate' (or 'body' in the second version).
Distinctness means that the subject terms do not overlap in intension or extension, unless the syllogism is properly structured.
2
Recognize the figure and the role of the middle term
This is a second-figure syllogism: the middle term (stone) is the predicate in both premises. The major premise is 'Every stone is inanimate' (or 'body'), the minor premise is 'No man is stone'. The middle term appears as predicate in both, but note that the subject terms 'man' and 'stone' are different.
In the second figure, the middle term is the predicate of both premises, and the two subject terms are distinct.
3
Analyze the conclusion from the first premised pair
With premises 'No man is stone' and 'Every stone is inanimate', the conclusion that seems to follow is negative (e.g., 'Some man is not inanimate' or 'No man is inanimate'). The author says 'the truth is the negative' – meaning the conclusion in this case would be a negative proposition. But is it valid? The condition here hinges on whether the judgment on 'stone' (that it is inanimate) can transfer to 'man'.
The conclusion is not automatically valid just because the premises are true – the logical form must satisfy the conditions of the second figure.
4
Change the major premise and observe the effect
When the major premise is changed to 'Every stone is a body', the conclusion changes to affirmative (e.g., 'Some man is a body'? or 'Every man is a body'?). The author says 'the truth is the affirmative'. This difference shows that the conclusion is not necessitated by the premises alone; it depends on the specific predicates and the relation between the distinct subject terms.
The change in conclusion demonstrates that judgment on 'stone' does not automatically transfer to 'man' – the middle term must be properly related to both subject terms in a way that forces a conclusion.
5
Generalize the condition: judgment does not transfer between distinct things
The author's point is that in the second figure, because the two subject terms are distinct (each appears only in one premise), a judgment about one does not entail a judgment about the other. The conclusion follows only if the middle term is arranged so that the two subject terms are implicitly linked (e.g., through inclusion or exclusion). This condition is crucial for validating second-figure syllogisms.
The commentator (muḥashshī) will elaborate on the precise conditions in the full exposition of the second figure.
