Ibn Sina's Refutation of the Circularity Objection
Abu Sa'id objects to first figure; Ibn Sina distinguishes general vs detailed knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Abu Sa'id's circularity objection against the first figure syllogism and explain Ibn Sina's distinction between general and detailed knowledge that refutes it.

Core Idea
The first figure avoids circularity because the conclusion is known generally in the major premise, but only in detail after the syllogism.

This passage records a critical dialectical exchange between Abu Sa'id and Ibn Sina. Abu Sa'id argues that the first figure syllogism involves circular reasoning, because to know the universal major premise you must already know the conclusion. Ibn Sina responds with a subtle epistemological distinction between general (ijmālī) and detailed (tafsīlī) knowledge. Understanding this distinction is essential for appreciating why the first figure is considered the most certain form of reasoning in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Abu Sa'id argues that the first figure syllogism is circular because knowledge of the major premise requires knowing that every instance of the middle term (including the minor) has the property of the major, which is exactly the conclusion.
2
Trace the circularity
The major premise is a universal statement. To know it, one must already know the truth of all its instances, one of which is the conclusion. Hence the conclusion is needed to know a premise, but the conclusion is supposed to be derived from the premises. This creates a circle.
3
Find Ibn Sina's distinction
He distinguishes between general (ijmālī) knowledge and detailed (tafsīlī) knowledge. The major premise contains the conclusion in a general way, not in its specific, detailed form.
4
Apply the distinction
In the major premise, the conclusion is known generally (e.g., 'all A are B' includes knowledge that any particular A is B). In the conclusion, after the syllogism, that same knowledge becomes detailed and explicit for the specific minor term. The dependence of detailed knowledge on general knowledge is not a vicious circle.
5
Evaluate the resolution
The objection is refuted because it conflates two different modes of knowledge. A circle occurs only if the same mode is required, but here general knowledge suffices for the premise, while the conclusion adds detail.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و اورد الشيخ ابوسعيد على الشيخ الرئيس ابن سينا ان الاستدلال بهذا الشكل الذي هو المناط الاصل في الاستدلال لا بتناء الثلاثة الباقية عليه كما سيظهر يستلزم الدور فلا يكون منتجاً فضلاً عن ان يكون بيناً، وذلك ، لان العلم بالنتيجة موقوف على العلم بالمقدمات التى من جملتها الكبرى الكلية والعلم بها انما يحصل لو علم ثبوت الحكم بالاكبر بالايجاب او السلب على كل واحد من افراد الأوسط التي من جملتها الاصغر فيكون موقوفاً على العلم بثبوت الحكم بالاكبر للاصغر الذي هو عين المنتيجة فيلزم الدور. واجاب الشيخ بالفرق بين العلم الحاصل في النتيجة و بين العلم الحاصل في الكبرى بالاجمال و التفصيل وتوقف حصول العلم التفصيلى على العلم الاجمالي غير مضر، فيندفع الدور. والحاصل ان العلم بالمنتيجة فى الكبرى اجمالى و في النتيجة نفسها بعد استخراجها من المقدمات تفصيلي فباعتباره الأول يكون موقوفاً عليه وباعتباره الثاني يكون موقوفاً اذ لا استحالة في ان يكون للشيء وصفان يكون باعتبار احدهما معلوماً وباعتبار الآخر مجهولاً.
And Sheikh Abu Sa'id objected to Sheikh al-Ra'is Ibn Sina that reasoning by this figure, which is the principal basis in reasoning because the other three depend on it as will appear, necessitates circularity, so it is not productive, let alone being evident. That is because knowledge of the conclusion depends on knowledge of the premises, among which is the universal major premise, and knowledge of it is only obtained if one knows the affirmation of the judgment by the major (affirmatively or negatively) of every individual of the middle term, among which is the minor. So it depends on knowledge of the affirmation of the judgment by the major for the minor, which is exactly the conclusion, so circularity follows. And the Sheikh (Ibn Sina) answered by distinguishing between the knowledge obtained in the conclusion and the knowledge in the major premise as general and detailed, and the dependence of obtaining detailed knowledge on general knowledge is not harmful, so the circularity is repelled. The summary is that knowledge of the conclusion in the major premise is general, and in the conclusion itself after extracting it from the premises is detailed. So by the first consideration, the conclusion is dependent on it, and by the second consideration, it is dependent, since there is no impossibility in something having two descriptions, by one of which it is known and by the other unknown.
Source pages p. 345

Commentary
This passage records a classic objection by Abu Sa'id against Ibn Sina's claim that the first figure yields certain knowledge without circularity. The objection argues that to know the universal major premise (e.g., 'Every A is B'), one must already know that every individual A is B, including the minor term that appears in the conclusion. Thus, the conclusion is already known before the syllogism, making it circular. Ibn Sina's response is to distinguish between general (ijmālī) and detailed (tafsīlī) knowledge. The major premise contains the conclusion in a general way (as a universal), but the conclusion is known in detail after the syllogism. The dependence of detailed knowledge on general knowledge is not a vicious circle. This is a key epistemological point: the same proposition can be known generally and later known in detail without circularity.

Key Terms
الدور (al-dawr): Circular reasoning; a logical fallacy where the conclusion is presupposed in the premises. [Note: Standard term in Mantiq.]
اجمالى (ijmālī): General or summary knowledge; knowledge of a universal without detailed specification. [Note: In later texts, ijmālī often contrasts with tafsīlī in legal theory as well.]
تفصيلى (tafsīlī): Detailed knowledge; knowledge of particulars or specifics. [Note: Standard contrast.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How does Ibn Sina refute the charge of circularity in the first figure?
A) He denies that the major premise contains the conclusion.
B) He distinguishes between general and detailed knowledge.
C) He argues that the minor premise is not needed.
D) He says the conclusion is known prior to the syllogism.

Key Takeaway
The first figure syllogism is not circular because the conclusion is known generally in the major premise and in detail after extraction, avoiding vicious circularity.

Non-Productive Modal Premise Combinations
Commentator lists non-productive modal premise combinations with example

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which combinations of modal premises fail to produce a conclusion and explain why, using the example of the conditioned particular and temporal premises.

Core Idea
Modal differences between premises can block a syllogistic conclusion even if the form is valid.

So far, we have learned the conditions for a productive syllogism in categorical logic. But when modal premises are involved, additional constraints arise. The commentator Yazdi here lists specific types of modal premises that, when combined, do not yield a conclusion. This lesson walks you through his analysis and teaches you to recognize these non-productive combinations.

Guided Path
1
Classify the premise types
Identify the minor premises as 'non-permanent and non-necessary' (ghayr dā'ima wa lā ḍarūriyya) — eleven types. The major premises are from the seven types whose negatives do not convert. Understand the scope of modal premises under consideration.
The eleven and seven categories are standard in modal logic.
2
Locate the most specific minors
The conditioned particular (mashrūṭa khāṣṣa) and temporal (waqtiyya) are the most specific minors. Why? The conditioned particular has a particular condition, and the temporal is true only at a specific time.
These are more specific than the conditioned general and the two conventional, etc.
3
Find the most specific major
The temporal major is the most restrictive among the majors. It applies only at a specific time.
4
Test the combination
Mixing a conditioned particular or temporal minor with a temporal major is non-productive. The example shows: minor 'Nothing of what is eclipsed is luminous necessarily at a specific time not always' and major 'Every moon is luminous necessarily at a specific time not always'. No conclusion follows.
The difference (ikhtilāf) between the premises causes non-productivity.
5
Understand why it fails
The modal mismatch prevents a valid inference. The example illustrates that the expected conclusion (either universal negative or particular affirmative) is impossible due to the modal conditions.
If the most specific combinations are unproductive, so are the more general ones.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۳۷) وذلك ، لانه اذا انتفيا كان الصغرى غير الدائمة والضرورية وهى احدى عشرة و الكبرى من القضاياء السبع التي لا تنعكس سوالبها واخص الصغريات المشروطة الخاصة والوقتية، لان المشروطة الخاصة اخص من المشروطة العامة والعرفيتين والوقتية من الثمانية الباقية واخص الكبريات الوقتية و اختلاط الصغريين وهما المشروطة الخاصة والوقتية مع الكبرى الوقتية غير منتج للاختلاف الموجب لعدم الانتاج فانه يصدق قولنا : لاشيء من المنخسف بمضىء بالضرورة في وقت معين لا دائماً و كل قر مضيء بالضرورة في وقت معين لا دائماً، مع امتناع السلب بالامكان العام، لصدق: كل منخسف قر بالضرورة ولو بدلنا الكبرى بقولنا وكل شمس مضيئة في وقت معين لا دائما امتنع الايجاب ومتى لم ينتج هذان الاختلافان لم ينتج ساير الاختلاطات لاستلزام عدم انتاج الاخص عدم انتاج الاعم.
(37) That is because if they are absent, the minor is non-permanent and non-necessary, and it is eleven, and the major is from the seven propositions whose negatives do not convert. And the most specific of the minors are the conditioned particular and the temporal, because the conditioned particular is more specific than the conditioned general and the two conventional, and the temporal is more specific than the remaining eight. And the most specific of the majors is the temporal. And the mixture of the two minors, which are the conditioned particular and the temporal, with the temporal major, is non-productive due to the difference that necessitates non-productivity. For it is true to say: 'Nothing of what is eclipsed is luminous necessarily at a specific time not always' and 'Every moon is luminous necessarily at a specific time not always', with the impossibility of the negative by general possibility, because it is true that 'Every eclipsed thing is a moon necessarily'. And if we change the major to 'and every sun is luminous at a specific time not always', the affirmative becomes impossible. And when these two differences do not produce, the other mixtures do not produce, because the non-productivity of the more specific entails the non-productivity of the more general.
Source pages p. 346

Commentary
This passage discusses the conditions for productivity in modal syllogisms. The commentator lists the types of premises that are 'non-permanent and non-necessary' (ghayr dā'ima wa lā ḍarūriyya) which are eleven types, and the major premises that do not have convertible negatives (seven types). The most specific minor premises are the 'conditioned particular' (mashrūṭa khāṣṣa) and the 'temporal' (waqtiyya). The most specific major is the temporal. Mixing these specific minors with the temporal major does not yield a conclusion because of a difference that prevents productivity. He gives an example: 'Nothing of what is eclipsed is luminous necessarily at a specific time not always' (minor), and 'Every moon is luminous necessarily at a specific time not always' (major). The conclusion should be about the eclipsed thing and luminous? But it says the negative by general possibility is impossible because 'Every eclipsed thing is a moon necessarily' is true. If we change the major to 'every sun is luminous...' then the affirmative becomes impossible. So the two differences (the specific combination) do not produce, and thus broader combinations also do not produce because if the more specific is unproductive, the more general is also unproductive.

Sterility of Possible Minor with Permanent Major
Commentator explains sterility of possible minor with permanent and conventional majors.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify why a possible minor premise fails to produce a conclusion when paired with a permanent or conventional major premise in a modal syllogism.

Core Idea
A possible premise cannot give certainty when paired with a permanent major because what is possible may never happen.

In modal syllogistics, not all combinations of premises yield a valid conclusion. The commentator explains that when the minor premise is possible (mumkina) and the major is permanent (dā'ima) or conventional ('urfiyya), the syllogism is sterile—no conclusion follows. Understanding this restriction is crucial for constructing sound modal arguments in Islamic theology and jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Recall the first condition
The passage builds on a prior condition: the possible minor does not produce a conclusion with the seven propositions whose negatives do not convert. This condition restricts which majors can combine with a possible minor.
2
Understand sterility
Sterility means no necessary conclusion follows. The author shows that with a permanent major, the mixture is sterile because what is possible for a subject may never be actually true of it, but the permanent major asserts something is always true. These modalities clash.
The example: 'Every Roman is possibly black' (possible affirmative) and 'No Roman is permanently black' (permanent negative). The universal negative major denies the predicate permanently, but the minor only asserts possibility, so no conclusion can be drawn.
3
Extend to conventional majors
The sterility extends to the two conventional majors ('urfiyya 'āmma and 'urfiyya khāṣṣa) because the permanent is more specific than the conventional general, and the sterility of the more specific implies sterility of the more general. Also, the conventional general possible does not produce, and the non-permanent also fails.
4
Generalize the principle
A possible minor cannot combine with any major that asserts actuality (permanent or conventional). This is because possibility and actuality are different modal categories; a possible proposition does not entail actual existence, so a major that requires actuality cannot infer anything from a possible minor.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۴۲) قوله وحاصله ان الممكنة ان كانت صغري ...» اما الاول فلانه قد ظهر من الشرط الأول ان الممكنة الصغرى لا ينتج مع القضاياء السبع التي لا تنعكس سوالبها فلو استعمل الممكنة الصغرى مع غير الضروريات الثلاث، لكان اختلاطها الدائمة والعرفيتين لكن اختلاطها مع الدائمة عقيم الجواز ان يكون الثابت لشيء بالامكان مسلوباً عنه دائماً كقولنا كل رومي فهو اسود بالامكان ولاشيء من الرومي با سود دائماً مع امتناع سلب الشيء عن نفسه ولو بدلنا الكبرى بقولنا: ولاشيء من التركي باسود دائماً امتنع الايجا الايجاب ويلزم من عقم هذا الاختلاط عقم اختلاط الممكنة الصغرى مع العرفيتين اما مع العرفية العامة، فلان الدائمة اخص منها وعقم الاخص يستلزم عقم الاعم و اما مع العرفية الخاصة فلعدم انتاج العرفية العامة الممكنة و عدم انتاج اللادوام ايضاً لان الأصل لما كان مخالفاً للمكنة في مع

But its mixture with the permanent is sterile, because it is possible that what is affirmed of something by possibility is denied of it permanently, like our saying: 'Every Roman is black by possibility' and 'Nothing from the Roman is black permanently' with the impossibility of negating a thing from itself. Commentary
This passage continues the discussion on modal syllogisms focusing on the possible minor premise. The first condition stated that a possible minor does not produce a conclusion with the seven propositions whose negatives do not convert. If the possible minor is used with premises other than the three necessary (ḍarūriyyāt), then it mixes with the permanent (dā'ima) and the two conventional ('urfiyyat). The mixture with the permanent is sterile because a thing can be possibly something but never actually be that, as in the example: 'Every Roman is possibly black' and 'No Roman is permanently black' yields nothing? Conditions for the First Figure with Possible Premises
Author explains non-productivity of certain modal combinations in the first figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine which modal combinations in the first figure are productive and identify the sterility condition for possible premises.

Core Idea
Not every modal premise combination in the first figure yields a valid conclusion; some are inherently sterile.

This lesson dives into the detailed commentary on modal syllogisms in the first figure. The author explains why certain mixtures, especially those involving possible (mumkin) premises, fail to produce a single conclusion. By understanding these conditions, you will avoid common errors in modal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first condition for productivity
Find in the text the requirement that either the minor must be perpetual (da'ima) or the major must be among propositions whose negatives convert.
This is called al-shart al-awwal.
2
Analyze the case of possible minor with perpetual major
Read the Roman example: 'Every Roman is possible black; No Roman is perpetually black.' See why this leads to a contradiction of self-negation.
The conclusion would force something to be negated of itself, which is impossible.
3
Analyze the case of possible major with perpetual minor
Study the second case where the major is possible and the minor is perpetual; it also yields sterility for similar reasons.
The example uses 'Every Roman is white perpetually; No Roman is white by possibility'.
4
Examine the most specific mixtures: conditioned particular and temporal
Look at the eclipse example: minor as 'nothing eclipsed is luminous at a specific time' and major as 'every moon is luminous at a specific time'. Notice that the conclusion changes depending on whether the major is moon or sun.
This difference (ikhtilaf) causes non-productivity.
5
Generalize the condition
The text states that if the most specific mixtures are sterile, then all less specific ones are also sterile because the specific implies the general.
Understand the logical principle of entailment in deduction.

And the most specific of the nine majors is the temporal, as mentioned. And the mixing of the conditioned particular minor with the temporal major is unproductive due to the difference that causes sterility and non-productivity. For it is true: 'Nothing that is eclipsed is luminous by necessity as long as it is eclipsed, not perpetually, and every moon is luminous by necessity in a specific time, not perpetually,' while the negation of some eclipsed thing not being a moon by general possibility is impossible; rather, every eclipsed thing is a moon by necessity. So the truth in some cases is affirmation and in some cases negation, which is the difference causing sterility. Then it is not hidden that what we mentioned—that the most specific of the minor propositions is the conditioned particular—is based on what the author chose.

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary on the conditions for productivity in the first figure of the categorical syllogism when modal premises are involved, especially possible (mumkin) propositions. The author addresses two main cases: when the minor is possible and when the major is possible, and explains why certain combinations are unproductive (sterile).

The discussion begins with a note from Muhammad 'Ali about the truth values of negative and affirmative conclusions in certain combinations. Then 'Abd al-Rahim explains the first condition for non-productivity: if the minor is not perpetual and not necessary, and the major is among the seven types of propositions whose negatives do not convert, then the most specific minors (conditioned particular and temporal) when mixed with the most specific major (temporal) produce contradictory results (affirmation in some cases, negation in others), making the syllogism invalid. The example uses lunar eclipse and moonlight: 'Nothing eclipsed is luminous by necessity in a specific time' and 'Every moon is luminous by necessity in a specific time' yield a true affirmative conclusion that every eclipsed thing is a moon, but if the major is changed to 'every sun...' the conclusion becomes negative. This difference indicates sterility.

Next, Muhammad 'Ali clarifies that perpetuity (dawam) is more general than necessity, and enumerates the types: perpetuals, generals, specifics, temporals, etc. The distinction between 'prevention of emptiness' (man' al-khulu) and 'real separation' (infisal haqiqi) is also noted.

The commentary then addresses the possible minor case in more detail. If the minor is possible and the major is perpetual, the syllogism is sterile because the possible attribute could be negated perpetually, as in 'Every Roman is possible black' and 'No Roman is perpetually black' — the conclusion that some Roman is not a Roman is impossible. Similarly, with conventional majors, the sterility follows from the perpetual case. For the possible major, the same problem arises: if the major is possible and the minor is perpetual, the syllogism fails because the possible negation could be contradicted by a perpetual affirmation.

Finally, the author provides a summary proof of the first condition: if the minor is not perpetual/necessary and the major is not among those with converting negatives, then the most specific minors (conditioned particular and temporal) with the temporal major yield a difference that prevents a single conclusion—hence the syllogism is unproductive. The example with lunar eclipse and moon/sun illustrates this. The author also notes that the classification of the conditioned particular as the most specific minor is based on the original author's (al-musannif) choice.

All examples used are from astronomy (lunar eclipse, sun, moon) and nationalities (Roman, Turk, Indian) to illustrate modal logic. The key lesson is that the first figure requires certain modal conditions to be productive, and when these are violated, the syllogism fails.

الدائمة
المنعكسة السوالب

Proof of the First Condition for Modal Syllogisms
Author proves first condition for modal syllogisms using counterexample

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a modal syllogism is infertile due to the absence of perpetuity and convertibility conditions, and apply the same reasoning to Islamic legal examples.

Core Idea
Not all modal premise combinations yield a conclusion; specific conditions on perpetuity and convertibility must hold.

In modal syllogisms, not every combination of premises produces a valid conclusion. This lesson examines the first condition: the minor must be perpetual or the major must be from the convertible negatives. We will see how the author proves that when both conditions are absent, the syllogism is infertile, using the example of lunar eclipse and solar illumination. The proof involves identifying the most specific non-perpetual minors and non-convertible majors, and showing that their combination leads to 'difference' (ikhtilaf), meaning both affirmative and negative conclusions are possible, hence no single necessary conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Understand the first condition
The first condition for a productive modal syllogism states: either the minor premise must be perpetual (da'im) or the major premise must be from the six propositions whose negatives are convertible (mun'akisa al-sawalib). If both conditions are absent, the combination is infertile.
Perpetual here means necessary or always true; convertible negatives are those where the negative is logically equivalent to the original affirmative under conversion by contraposition.
2
Identify the non-conforming premises
If both conditions fail, the minor is from the 13 non-perpetual propositions (not necessary and not perpetual), and the major is from the 9 non-convertible negatives. The most specific minor is the particular conditional (al-mashruta al-khassa), and the most specific major is the temporal (al-waqtiyya).
The particular conditional asserts necessity of the relation as long as the description holds; the temporal asserts truth at a specific time.
3
Construct the counterexample
The author gives: minor = 'No eclipse is luminous necessarily while it is eclipsed (not perpetual)'; major = 'Every moon is luminous at a specific time (not perpetual)'. From these, one can derive 'Every eclipse is a moon' (necessarily true) but also, by substituting a different temporal major (e.g., 'Every sun is luminous at a specific time'), one gets 'No eclipse is a sun'. Thus, the same minor with different majors yields opposite conclusions, showing infertility.
The 'difference' (ikhtilaf) is that the premises do not force a single conclusion; both affirmative and negative are possible depending on the major.
4
Generalize the infertility
If the most specific combination (particular conditional minor + temporal major) is infertile, then any combination where the minor is non-perpetual and the major is non-convertible-negative is infertile. This is because the specific case is the most restrictive; if it fails, the more general cases also fail.
This reasoning follows from the principle that if a more specific condition does not produce, the less specific cannot either.

لا بأس أن نشير إليه بطريق الاختصار فنقول: أما دليل الشرط الأول: فهو أنه لو انتفى كلاهما: أي دوام الصغرى وكون الكبرى من القضايا المنعكسة السوالب، لكانت الصغرى غير الضرورية والدائمة، وهي ثلاثة عشر على ما ذكره المصنف، والكبرى إحدى التسع الغير المنعكسة السوالب، وأخص الصغريات المشروطة الخاصة، لكونها أخص من المشروطة العامة والعرفيتين كما هو ظاهر، والوقتية التي هي أخص من البواقي الثمانية، وذلك لأنه متى تحققت الضرورة في جميع أوقات الوصف تحققت في بعض أوقات الذات من غير عكس، وأخص الكبريات التسع الوقتية كما ذكر، واختلاط الصغرى المشروطة الخاصة الكبرى الوقتية غير منتج للاختلاف الموجب للعقم وعدم الإنتاج، فإنه يصدق: لا شيء من المنخسف بمضيء بالضرورة مادام منخسفاً لا دائماً، وكل قمر مضيء بالضرورة في وقت معين لا دائماً، مع امتناع بعض المنخسف ليس بقمر بالامكان العام، الصدق كل منخسف قمر بالضرورة. ولو بدلنا الكبرى بقولنا: كل شمس مضيئة في وقت معين لا دائماً، صدق لا شيء من المنخسف بشمس، فكان الحق في بعض المواضع الإيجاب وفي بعضها السلب، وهو الاختلاف الموجب للعقم، فإذا لم ينتج الأخص لم ينتج الأعم كما مر معللاً من المحشي. ثم لا يخفى أن ما ذكرنا من أن أخص الصغريات المشروطة الخاصة بناء على ما اختاره المصنف سابقاً من أن المشروطة ما حكم فيها بضرورة النسبة مادام الوصف، وأما على ما ذكره القوم من أنها ما حكم فيها بالضرورة بشرط الوصف، فتغير الأسلوب بأن نقول: وأخص الصغريات المشروطة الخاصة والوقتية، أما الأولى فمن المشروطة العامة والعرفيتين، وأما الثانية فمن الثمانية الباقية إلى آخر ما ذكره.
We have no problem to point to it in brief and say: As for the proof of the first condition: It is that if both are absent—i.e., the perpetuity of the minor and the major being from the propositions whose negatives are convertible—then the minor would be other than necessary and perpetual, and they are thirteen as mentioned by the author, and the major would be one of the nine whose negatives are not convertible. And the most specific of the minor propositions is the particular conditional, because it is more specific than the general conditional and the two 'urfiyyas, as is apparent, and the temporal is the most specific of the remaining eight. That is because whenever necessity obtains in all times of the description, it obtains in some times of the essence without inversion. And the most specific of the nine majors is the temporal, as mentioned. And the mixing of the particular conditional minor with the temporal major is unproductive because of the difference that causes infertility and lack of production. Commentary
This passage is a proof of the first condition for productive modal syllogisms. The author, Mirza Muhammad Ali, explains that if two conditions are absent—the minor not being perpetual and the major not being from the six convertible negatives—then the combination is infertile. He lists the 13 possible non-perpetual minors and the 9 non-convertible majors, identifying the most specific ones: the particular conditional (mashruta khassa) and the temporal (waqtia). Using the example of lunar eclipse and solar illumination, he demonstrates that mixing these specific premises leads to both affirmative and negative conclusions, i.e., difference (ikhtilaf), which causes infertility (uqm). The proof concludes that if the specific combination fails, the more general also fails. A note follows refining the definition of 'mashruta' based on the author's versus the common view, affecting which minors are considered most specific.

Proof of the Second Condition for Modal Syllogisms
Proof of the second condition for modal syllogisms

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a possible minor premise combined with a non-necessary major premise is infertile unless the major is perpetual or from the convertible negatives.

Core Idea
A possible minor with a non-necessary major is infertile unless the major is perpetual or convertible.

The second condition of modal syllogisms governs what happens when the minor premise is possible (mumkina) and the major is not necessary. In this passage from Yazdi's Hashiya, the author provides a meticulous proof that such a combination is infertile unless the major is perpetual (da'ima) or belongs to the six convertible negatives. We will walk through the logical reasoning behind this condition, using concrete examples from the text.

Guided Path
1
Identify the case under discussion
The author is examining what happens when the minor premise is possible (mumkina) and the major is non-necessary (i.e., not from the convertible negatives). This is the scenario for the second condition.
The convertible negatives are a set of propositions that allow certain transformations.
2
Understand the three subcases for the major
The author considers three types of non-necessary major: the perpetual (da'ima), the general 'urfiyya, and the special 'urfiyya. He will show each leads to infertility.
Perpetual means true at all times; general 'urfiyya has a general condition like 'always'; special 'urfiyya has a special condition like 'as long as'.
3
Follow the counterexample for the perpetual case
Take the premises: 'Every Roman is black possibly' (possible minor) and 'Nothing of the Roman is black perpetually' (perpetual major). The author argues that from these, no valid conclusion follows. He tests by substituting the major to show both affirmative and negative conclusions would be required.
The example uses ethnic groups as placeholders; the logical point is that the same substitution leads to contradictory requirements.
4
Extend the reasoning to the general 'urfiyya and special 'urfiyya
The perpetual is more specific than the general 'urfiyya, so if the perpetual leads to non-production, the general 'urfiyya also does. The special 'urfiyya is a conjunction of the general 'urfiyya and a 'non-perpetuity' component, both infertile.
The 'non-perpetuity' component differs in quality from the minor, causing infertility as per earlier rules.
5
Analyze the second part: major possible with minor non-necessary
When the major is possible and the minor is non-necessary, the minor must be perpetual to have any chance of production. Otherwise, the combination fails.
This mirrors the first condition but with roles reversed.

وأما دليل الشرط الثاني فدليل الأمر الأول منه: أنه قد تبين أنفاً أن الممكنة الصغرى لا تنتج مع القضايا التسع الغير المنعكسة السوالب، فلو اختلط الممكنة الصغرى مع غير الضرورية والمشروطتين كان اختلاطها الدائمة والعرفية العامة والعرفية الخاصة، والأول عقيم فإنه يصدق قولنا: كل رومي فهو أسود بالامكان، ولا شيء من الرومي بأسود دائماً لجواز أن يكون الثابت للشيء بالامكان مسلوباً عنه، أنه لا يصدق بعض الرومي ليس برومي بالامكان، الصدق كل رومي رومي بالضرورة ومع سلب الشيء عن نفسه، ولو بدلنا الكبرى بقولنا: لا شيء من التركي بأسود دائماً صدق يمتنع لا شيء من الرومي بتركي دائماً فكان الحق في الأول الإيجاب وفي الثاني السلب وهو دليل عدم الإنتاج، وكذلك الثاني فإن الدائمة أخص من العرفية العامة وقد تقدم أن عدم إنتاج الأخص يستلزم عدم إنتاج الأعم. وأما الثالث: فلأن العرفية الخاصة مشتملة على جزئين: العرفية العامة وقد تقدم أنها عقيمة ومفهوم اللادوام وهو أيضاً عقيم لأنه مخالف للجزء الأول في الكيف وهو مخالف للصغرى فيه فلابد أن يكون هو موافقاً للصغرى فيه وقد تقدم أن المقدمتين لو لم تختلفا في الكيف لم تنتجا أيضاً. ودليل الأمر الثاني منه: أنه إذا كانت الكبرى الممكنة مع الصغرى الغير الضرورية فلابد أن تكون الدائمة لما سبق في الشرط الأول من أن الصغرى لابد وأن يصدق عليها الدوام إذا لم تكن الكبرى من الستة المنعكسة السوالب كما فيما نحن فيه، وظاهر أنها لا تنتج معها لحصول الاختلاف فإنه يصدق قولنا: كل رومي فهو أبيض دائماً، ولا شيء من الرومي بأبيض بالامكان، ولا يصدق بعض الرومي ليس بالامكان، أما الأول فلجواز أن يكون الثابت للشيء بالدوام مسلوباً عنه بالامكان، وأما الثاني فلصدق كل رومي رومي بالضرورة ولامتناع سلب الشيء عن نفسه، ولو بدلنا الكبرى بقولنا: لا شيء من الزنجي بأبيض صدق: لا شيء من الرومي برنجي؟ (ميرزا محمد علي)
As for the proof of the second condition, the proof of its first part: It has become clear earlier that the possible minor does not produce with the nine propositions whose negatives are not convertible. So if the possible minor is mixed with the non-necessary and the two conditionals, its mixture would be with the perpetual, the general 'urfiyya, and the special 'urfiyya. The first is infertile, for our statement is true: 'Every Roman is black possibly,' and 'Nothing of the Roman is black perpetually,' because it is permissible that what is affirmed of a thing possibly is denied of it. That it is not true: 'Some Roman is not Roman possibly' is true? Actually: 'Every Roman is Roman necessarily,' and with the denial of a thing from itself. And if we substitute the major with our statement: 'Nothing of the Turk is black perpetually,' it is true: 'Nothing of the Roman is Turk perpetually.' So the truth is in the first case affirmative and in the second case negative, and that is the evidence of non-production. Similarly the second, for the perpetual is more specific than the general 'urfiyya, and it has preceded that the non-production of the specific entails the non-production of the general. As for the third: because the special 'urfiyya comprises two parts: the general 'urfiyya, and it has preceded that it is infertile, and the concept of non-perpetuity, and it is also infertile because it differs from the first part in quality, and it differs from the minor in it, so it must agree with the minor in it, and it has preceded that if the two premises do not differ in quality, they do not produce either. And the proof of its second part: That if the possible major is with the non-necessary minor, then the minor must be perpetual because of what preceded in the first condition: that the minor must be perpetual if the major is not from the six convertible negatives, as in our case. And it is apparent that it does not produce with it because of the occurrence of difference. For our statement is true: 'Every Roman is white perpetually,' and 'Nothing of the Roman is white possibly,' and it is not true that 'Some Roman is not possibly'? As for the first, because it is permissible that what is affirmed of a thing perpetually is denied of it possibly. As for the second, because 'Every Roman is Roman' is necessarily true, and it is impossible to deny a thing from itself. And if we substitute the major with our statement: 'Nothing of the Zanj is white,' it is true: 'Nothing of the Roman is Zanj.' (Mirza Muhammad Ali)

Commentary
This passage proves the second condition for modal syllogisms. The proof has two parts. The first part addresses the case where the minor premise is possible (mumkina). The author shows that if the possible minor is combined with a non-necessary major (i.e., not from the convertible negatives), the combination is infertile. He demonstrates with examples using ethnic groups: 'Every Roman is black possibly' and 'Nothing of the Roman is black perpetually' lead to a true universal affirmative (every Roman is Roman) and thus no valid conclusion. The second part shows that if the major is possible and the minor is non-necessary, then the minor must be perpetual to avoid infertility, again using examples. The proof relies on the principle that difference in quality or lack of universality causes infertility.

Reducing Syllogistic Moods: From 16 to 4
Author enumerates the 16 possible moods and reduces to 4 via conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the conditions of difference in quality and universality of the major premise to reduce the possible moods in any categorical syllogism figure from 16 to 4 valid moods.

Core Idea
Two conditions eliminate 12 invalid syllogisms, leaving only 4 productive moods per figure.

In categorical syllogisms, each figure has 16 possible premise combinations (moods), but not all are valid. Logicians identified two key conditions—difference in quality and universality of the major—that filter out defective ones. This lesson walks through the reduction process, showing how only 4 moods survive and why they are prioritized.

Guided Path
1
Understand the 16 base moods
Recognize that each figure can have 4 types of minor premise (A, E, I, O) combined with 4 types of major premise, yielding 4×4=16 combinations.
2
Apply the difference condition
The two premises must differ in quality (one affirmative, one negative). This eliminates all combinations where both are affirmative or both are negative, removing 8 moods.
3
Apply the universality condition
The major premise must be universal (A or E), not particular (I or O). This eliminates moods where the major is particular, removing another 4 moods.
The author notes an alternative counting where universality removes 8 first, but prefers the given order.
4
Identify the 4 remaining moods
After both conditions, only 4 moods remain. These are the ones that can yield valid conclusions. The first two produce universal conclusions, and the first and third are especially linked to the first figure.
5
Compare alternative orderings
The author discusses an alternative reduction sequence but emphasizes that the order of conditions affects which moods are removed. The priority of moods is based on productivity and connection to the first figure.
This highlights the logical rigor in constructing syllogisms.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قد مر أن الضروب الممكنة الانعقاد في كل شكل ستة عشر حاصلة من ضرب الصغريات الأربع في الكبريات الأربع، فإذا اشتراط اختلاف المقدمتين في الكيف سقط ثمانية أضرب وهي الحاصلة من ضرب الموجبتين في الموجبتين والسالبتين في السالبتين، وإذا اشتراط كلية الكبرى سقط أربعة أخرى وهي الموجبتان مع السالبة الجزئية والسالبتان مع الموجبة الجزئية، فبقي أربعة وهي التي يذكرها المحشي. ولك أن تقول: سقط باشتراط كلية الكبرى ثمانية أضرب وهي الصغريات الأربع مع الكبرى الموجبة الجزئية والسالبة الجزئية، وباشتراط الاختلاف أربعة أخرى وهي الموجبتان مع الموجبة الكلية والسالبتان مع السالبة الكلية، لكن الأول أولى لتقدم الاختلاف في الذكر على الكلية فافهم هذا بطريق الحذف، أما بطريق التحصيل، فقد أشار إليه المحشي بقوله: (حاصلة من ضرب الكبرى الكلية...) (ميرزا محمد علي). وأما تقدم الأوليين على الأخريين فلانتاجهما للكلى دونها، وأما تقدم الأول على الثاني والثالث على الرابع فلمزيد اختصاصهما بالشكل الأول لاشتراكهما معه في أشرف المقدمتين.
It has passed that the possible moods in each figure are sixteen, resulting from multiplying the four minors by the four majors. So if the condition of difference in quality of the two premises is imposed, eight moods fall away—namely those resulting from multiplying the two affirmatives by the two affirmatives and the two negatives by the two negatives. And if the condition of universality of the major is imposed, another four fall away—namely the two affirmatives with the particular negative and the two negatives with the particular affirmative. So four remain, which the commentator mentions. And you could say: By imposing the condition of the major's universality, eight moods fall away—the four minors with the particular affirmative major and the particular negative major; and by imposing the condition of difference, another four fall away—the two affirmatives with the universal affirmative major and the two negatives with the universal negative major. But the first is better because difference precedes universality in mention. Understand this by way of deletion; as for by way of acquisition, the commentator indicated it by his saying: 'resulting from multiplying the universal major...' (Mirza Muhammad Ali). And as for the priority of the first two over the last two, it is because they produce the universal without them. And as for the priority of the first over the second and the third over the fourth, it is for their greater specificity to the first figure because they share with it the more honorable of the two premises.
Source pages p. 348

Commentary
This passage summarizes the possible moods in each figure of the categorical syllogism. The author states that there are 16 possible combinations of minor and major premises (four types each: universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative). Two conditions reduce these: (1) the two premises must differ in quality (one affirmative, one negative), and (2) the major premise must be universal. Under these, only four moods remain. The author notes an alternative counting but prefers the given ordering, explaining the priority of certain moods based on their ability to produce universal conclusions and their connection to the first figure.

الاختلاف في الكيف

The Syllogism

Reducing the Sixteen Moods to Four in Each Figure
Author counts the possible moods and explains conditions that reduce them to four.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the conditions of quality difference and universal major to reduce the sixteen possible syllogistic moods to the four valid ones in any figure.

Core Idea
Sixteen moods collapse to four once quality difference and universal major are required.

The author of this hashīya explains how the sixteen possible combinations of premises (four minor types times four major types) are reduced to four valid moods by imposing two simple conditions: the two premises must differ in quality (one affirmative, one negative), and the major premise must be universal. This reduction is essential for constructing valid syllogisms, especially in the first figure, which serves as the foundation for logical proof in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Enumerate the sixteen possible combinations
List all combinations of the four minor types (A, E, I, O) with the four major types (A, E, I, O). This gives 16 pairs. Example: minor A with major A (AA), minor A with major E (AE), etc.
A = universal affirmative, E = universal negative, I = particular affirmative, O = particular negative.
2
Apply the condition of differing quality
Remove any combination where both premises are affirmative (AA, AI, IA, II) or both negative (EE, EO, OE, OO). This removes 8 moods, leaving 8 where one premise is affirmative and the other negative.
This condition ensures that the premises relate correctly.
3
Apply the condition of universal major
From the remaining 8, remove those where the major premise is particular (i.e., I or O). The majors that are particular are: A_O, I_O, E_I, O_I (if those pairs remain). This removes 4 more, leaving 4 moods: (A,E), (I,E), (E,A), (O,A).
The major is the predicate of the conclusion; it must be universal for a valid syllogism in any figure.
4
Identify the remaining four moods and their ordering
The four moods are: (A,E), (I,E), (E,A), (O,A). The first two (A,E and I,E) are given precedence because they produce universal conclusions. Within each pair, the first is more specific to the first figure due to sharing the more honorable premise (the major).
These four moods are the only ones that can be valid in the first figure after additional conditions are met.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۴۵) قد مر ان الضروب الممكنة الانعقاد في كل شكل ستة عشر حاصلة من ضرب الصغريات الاربع فى الكبريات الاربع فاذا اشترط اختلاف المقدمتين فى الكيف سقط ثمانية اضرب وهي الحاصلة من ضرب الموجبتين في الموجبتين و السالبتين فى السالبنين واذا اشترط كلية الكبرى سقط اربعة أخرى وهي الموجبتان مع السائبة الجزئية والسالبتان مع الموجبة الجزئية فبقى اربعة و هي التي يذكرها المحشى. ولك ان تقول: سقط باشتراط كلية الكبرى ثمانية اضرب وهى الصغريات الأربع مع الكبرى الموجبة الجزئية والسالبة الجزئية وباشتراط الاختلاف اربعة اخرى وهى الموجبتان مع الموجبة الكلية و السالبتان مع السالبة الكلية لكن الأول أولى لتقدم الاختلاف فى الذكر على الكلية فافهم هذا بطريق الحذف اما بطريق التحصيل، فقد اشار اليه المحشى بقوله : (حاصلة من ضرب الكبرى و الكلية... » (میرزا محمد علی) (۴۶) اما تقدم الأوليين على الاخريين فلانتاجهما للكلى دونها و اما تقدم الأول على الثانى والثالث على الرابع فلمزيد اختصاصهما بالشكل الاول لاشتراكهما معه في اشرف المقدمتين ايضاً. (محمد على)
(45) It has passed that the possible moods in every figure are sixteen, obtained by multiplying the four minors by the four majors. If it is conditioned that the two premises differ in quality, eight moods drop—those obtained from multiplying the two affirmatives by the two affirmatives and the two negatives by the two negatives. And if universal major is conditioned, another four drop—the two affirmatives with the particular negative and the two negatives with the particular affirmative. So four remain, which the commentator mentions. And you may say: by conditioning universal major, eight drop—the four minors with the particular affirmative major and the particular negative major; and by conditioning difference, another four—the two affirmatives with the universal affirmative and the two negatives with the universal negative. But the first is better because difference is mentioned before universality. Understand this by way of elimination, or by way of acquisition—the commentator has indicated it by his saying: 'Obtained from multiplying the major and the universal...' (Mirza Muhammad Ali). (46) As for the precedence of the first two over the other two, it is because they produce the universal while the others do not. And as for the precedence of the first over the second and the third over the fourth, it is because of their greater specialization to the first figure, since they share with it the more honorable of the two premises. (Muhammad Ali).
Source pages p. 348

Commentary
This passage, consisting of two comments, explains how the sixteen possible moods in each figure (from combining four types of minors and four types of majors) are reduced to four valid ones by imposing conditions. The first comment (45) by Mirza Muhammad Ali describes two approaches: one where difference in quality removes eight moods and universal major removes four, leaving four; another order where universal major removes eight and difference removes four. The author prefers the first because difference is mentioned first. The second comment (46) by Muhammad Ali explains the ordering of the remaining four moods: the first two (which produce universal conclusions) are preferred over the last two (which produce particular); and within each pair, the first in each pair is more specific to the first figure because it shares the more honorable premise (the major) with the first figure.

Key Terms
الضروب (al-ḍurūb): Moods; combinations of premises in a syllogism, distinguished by quantity and quality. [Note: In earlier texts, ḍurūb may refer to figures; here it means specific premise combinations.]
الموجبتان (al-mūjibatān): The two affirmative types (universal and particular affirmative). [Note: Standard term for affirmative propositions.]

Proof by Contradiction for Syllogistic Moods
Author demonstrates proof by contradiction for all four moods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct a proof by contradiction for any valid categorical syllogism mood.

Core Idea
Proof by contradiction validates a conclusion by showing its opposite leads to a contradiction with the premises.

This lesson explores dalīl al-khalf, a powerful method to confirm the validity of a conclusion. The author demonstrates how assuming the contradictory of the conclusion, when combined with the given premises, inevitably produces a contradiction, thereby confirming the original conclusion. We will walk through the example of the first mood and extend the method.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original syllogism and its conclusion
The first mood example: minor premise 'Every color is an accident', major premise 'Nothing of the substance is an accident', conclusion 'Nothing of the colored is a substance'. Note the conclusion is a universal negative.
The conclusion is the statement we aim to prove using khalf.
2
Assume the contradictory of the conclusion
The contradictory of 'Nothing of the colored is a substance' is 'Some color is a substance' (an I proposition). We assume this is true temporarily.
This assumption is the key step – it is the opposite of what we want to prove.
3
Combine the assumed premise with one original premise
Combine 'Some color is a substance' (assumed) with the major premise 'Nothing of the substance is an accident'. This yields a new conclusion: 'Some color is not an accident'.
This deduction uses the same syllogistic form as the original.
4
Check for contradiction with the remaining premise
The new conclusion 'Some color is not an accident' directly contradicts the minor premise 'Every color is an accident'. Since the major premise is assumed true and the syllogistic form is valid, the only way to avoid contradiction is that the assumed premise (the contradictory of the original conclusion) is false. Thus the original conclusion must be true.
This reasoning applies identically to all four moods.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
راشي الحاشية (۴۷) ای دليل الخلف جار فى الضروب الاربعة كلها، مثلاً يقال في الضرب الأول : كل لون عرض ولاشيء من الجوهر بعرض ينتج: لاشيء من الملون بجوهر والالصدق نقيضه اعنى بعض اللون جوهر فيضم مع كبرى القياس و هكذا بعض اللون جوهر ولا شيء من الجوهر بعرض ينتج: بعض اللون ليس بعرض و هذا مناف لصغرى الضرب الاول وليس منشأه هيئة القياس لانها بديهى الانتاج ولا كبراه لانها مفروض الصدق فتعين الصغرى فتكون باطلة فيكون النتيجة حقا و قس على هذا الضروب الباقية (عبدالرحيم)
Glossator of the gloss (47) i.e., the proof by contradiction applies to all four moods. For example, in the first mood: 'Every color is an accident and nothing of the substance is an accident' produces 'Nothing of the colored is a substance.' Its contradictory is true, namely 'Some color is a substance.' This is combined with the major of the syllogism: 'Some color is a substance and nothing of the substance is an accident' produces 'Some color is not an accident.' That contradicts the minor premise of the first mood. And its source is not the figure of the syllogism because it is self-evidently productive, nor its major because it is assumed true. So the minor is determined to be false, hence the conclusion is true. And apply the same to the remaining moods. (Abd al-Rahim).
Source pages p. 349

Commentary
This passage explains the proof by contradiction (dhalil al-khalf) as applied to the four valid moods of any figure. The author uses the first mood as an example: from 'Every color is an accident' (minor) and 'Nothing of the substance is an accident' (major), the conclusion 'Nothing of the colored is a substance' is valid. If we assume the contradictory 'Some color is a substance' and combine it with the same major, we get 'Some color is not an accident,' which contradicts the minor 'Every color is an accident.' Since the major is assumed true and the syllogistic form is self-evidently valid, the only way to avoid contradiction is that the assumed premise (the contradictory of the conclusion) is false, making the original conclusion true. The same method applies to all moods.

Reductio ad Absurdum in All Four Figures
Explains that reductio ad absurdum works in all four syllogistic figures with an example from the first figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the reductio ad absurdum (dalīl al-khalf) to verify the conclusion of any categorical syllogism in any figure.

Core Idea
Reductio ad absurdum works in all four syllogistic figures by assuming the contradictory and deriving a contradiction.

In this passage, the commentator demonstrates how dalīl al-khalf is not limited to the first figure but applies universally. By working through an example, we see how assuming the contradictory of the conclusion leads to a contradiction with a known premise, thereby proving the conclusion. This lesson will walk you through the steps of this logical method.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original syllogism and its conclusion
Find the two premises and the conclusion that is to be proved. In the example: minor premise 'Every colour is an accident', major premise 'Nothing of the substance is an accident', conclusion 'Nothing of the coloured is a substance'.
The conclusion is the target of the reductio proof.
2
Assume the contradictory of the conclusion
Take the opposite of the conclusion. If the conclusion is universal negative (E), its contradictory is particular affirmative (I). Here, assume 'Some colour is a substance'.
This assumption is temporary; we aim to derive a contradiction.
3
Combine the assumption with the original major premise
Use the assumed proposition as a minor premise with the original major premise to deduce a new conclusion. The combination 'Some colour is a substance' and 'Nothing of the substance is an accident' yields 'Some colour is not an accident'.
Check that the syllogism formed is valid.
4
Observe the contradiction with the original minor premise
The derived conclusion ('Some colour is not an accident') contradicts the original minor premise ('Every colour is an accident'). Since the major premise is assumed true and the syllogistic form is valid, the contradiction must come from the assumed contradictory.
The only source of falsehood is the assumption.
5
Conclude that the assumption is false, so the original conclusion is true
Because the assumption leads to a contradiction, it is false. Therefore, its contradictory (the original conclusion) is true. Thus 'Nothing of the coloured is a substance' is proven.
This method can be applied to any figure by analogy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۴۷) ای دليل الخلف جار فى الضروب الاربعة كلها، مثلاً يقال في الضرب الأول : كل لون عرض ولاشيء من الجوهر بعرض ينتج: لاشيء من الملون بجوهر والالصدق نقيضه أعنى بعض اللون جوهر فيضم مع كبرى القياس و هكذا بعض اللون جوهر ولا شيء من الجوهر بعرض ينتج: بعض اللون ليس بعرض و هذا مناف لصغرى الضرب الاول وليس منشأه هيئة القياس لانها بديهى الانتاج ولا كبراه لانها مفروض الصدق فتعين الصغرى فتكون باطلة فيكون النتيجة حقا و قس على هذا الضروب الباقية (عبدالرحيم)
Meaning: The reductio ad absurdum is applicable in all four figures. For example, in the first figure one says: Every colour is an accident and nothing of the substance is an accident, so it produces: Nothing of the coloured is a substance. The truth of its contradictory, namely 'Some colour is a substance', is [assumed]. Then it is combined with the major premise of the syllogism, and thus: Some colour is a substance and nothing of the substance is an accident, so it produces: Some colour is not an accident. This contradicts the minor premise of the first figure. Its source is not the form of the syllogism, because that is intrinsically productive, nor its major premise, because that is assumed true. So the minor premise is determined [to be false], thus it is invalid, and so the conclusion is true. Analogize this to the remaining figures.
Source pages p. 349

Commentary
This passage is a commentary explaining that the reductio ad absurdum (dalīl al-khalf) is valid in all four figures of the categorical syllogism, not just the first. The author provides an example from the first figure: the premises 'Every colour is an accident' (minor) and 'Nothing of the substance is an accident' (major) yield the conclusion 'Nothing of the coloured is a substance'. To prove this via reductio, we assume the contradictory of the conclusion, i.e., 'Some colour is a substance'. Combining this assumption with the original major premise 'Nothing of the substance is an accident' we derive 'Some colour is not an accident', which contradicts the original minor premise 'Every colour is an accident'. Since the major premise is assumed true and the syllogistic form is valid, the contradiction must arise from the assumed contradictory, proving that the original conclusion is true. The commentator then instructs the reader to analogize this method to the remaining figures.

دليل الخلف
ضروب

Why 'Or' Misleads in Syllogistic Methods
Criticizes the matn's wording that suggests each conclusion method applies to all figures, clarifying correct usage.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the logical error in the matn's use of 'or' and correctly rephrase the rule specifying which methods apply to which figures.

Core Idea
Not every method works in every figure; 'or' falsely implies universal applicability.

In logical texts, even a single conjunction like 'or' can lead to serious misunderstandings. The matn stated that certain syllogistic conclusions are produced by reductio, conversion of the major, or conversion of the minor, using 'or'. The commentator shows that this implies each method works for all figures, which is false. Understanding this critique sharpens your ability to read logical rules precisely.

Guided Path
1
Identify the matn's claim
Locate the statement: 'the two universals produce a universal negative, and the two differing in quantity also produce a particular negative, by reductio or by conversion of the major or by conversion of the minor.' Notice the conjunction 'or' (أو).
The matn is describing how to derive conclusions in four syllogistic figures.
2
Recognize the implication of 'or'
The word 'or' here suggests that each of these methods (reductio, conversion of major, conversion of minor) can be used in every figure to obtain the conclusion. That is, the listener might think all three work universally.
In Arabic logic, 'or' can be exclusive or inclusive; here the context suggests exclusivity, which causes the error.
3
Understand which methods apply to which figures
The commentator clarifies: reductio works in all four figures, but conversion of the major does not work in the second and fourth figures, and conversion of the minor only works in the second figure. So each method has its domain.
This specificity is crucial for correct syllogistic reasoning.
4
Rephrase the rule correctly
The commentator suggests either replacing 'or' with 'and' (واو) — meaning the totality of methods covers all figures — or understanding 'or' in the inclusive sense (meaning 'and/or') as in classical Arabic poetry. The corrected phrasing lists which method(s) apply for each case.
Precise wording prevents overgeneralization and ensures valid reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اشارة الى ما تقدم في أخر مبحث عكس النقيض من انه قدبين انعكاس الخاصتين من السالبة الجزئية إلى العرفية الخاصة بدليل الافتراض. بقى هنا شيء وهو: أن كلام المصنف فى هذا المقام ليس بصريح فى المرام فان المفهوم من قوله : لينتج الكليتان سالبة كلية والمختلفتان فى الكم ايضاً سالبة جزئية بالخلف او عكس الكبرى او عکس الصغرى» حيث عطفهما بـ «او» ان انتاج كل واحد من هذه الضروب الاربعة بهذه النتايج ثابت بكل واحد من هذه الامور بمعنى ان كل واحد من هذه على سبيل منع الخلو، يجرى في كل واحد منها وليس كذلك ، فان الخلف و ان كان يجرى في كل واحد من الاربعة لكن عكس الكبرى لا يجرى فى الثانى والرابع و عكس الصغرى لا يجرى الا فى الثانى كما صرح بها المحشى. فالحق في العبارة ان يقال: «لينتج السالبة الجزئية مع الموجبة الكلية سالبة جزئية بالخلف و الموجبة الكلية أو الجزئية مع السالبة الكلية سالبة كلية او جزئية به ايضاً او بعكس الكبرى والسالبة الكلية مع الكبرى الموجبة الكلية، سالبة كلية به ايضاً أو بعكس الصغرى ...» او يبدل لفظة «او» بالواو. ولا يخفى انه لا يلزم ح ان يكون كل واحد من هذه الامور جارياً في كل واحد من الضروب الاربعة بل اللازم ان يكون مجموع هذه جارية فيها كلها وان لم يكن بعضها جارياً الا في البعض. اللهم الا ان يقال: ان لفظة «او» في كلامه بمعنى الواو كما في قول «النابغة»: الالـيـتـمـا هـذا الحمام لنا الى حمامتنا اونصفه فقدى... ثم ان هذا وارد عليه في الاشكال الاخيرة ايضاً ايضاً. (محمد على)
Reference to what preceded at the end of the topic of conversion of the contradictory, that it was shown that the conversion of the particular negative to the common specific is by the proof of postulation. There remains something here: namely that the author's statement in this place is not clear regarding the intention. For what is understood from his saying: 'So that the two universals produce a universal negative, and the two differing in quantity also produce a particular negative, by reductio or by conversion of the major or by conversion of the minor' – since he conjoined them with 'or' – is that the production of each of these four figures with these results is established by each of these things, meaning that each one of these, on a permissive basis, runs in each one of them. But it is not so. For the reductio, although it runs in all four, the conversion of the major does not run in the second and fourth, and the conversion of the minor does not run except in the second, as the commentator has stated. So the correct phrasing is to say: 'So that the particular negative with the universal affirmative produces a particular negative by reductio, and the universal affirmative or particular with the universal negative produces a universal or particular negative by it as well or by conversion of the major, and the universal negative with the universal affirmative major produces a universal negative by it as well or by conversion of the minor…' or he should replace the word 'or' with 'and'. And it is not hidden that it does not require that each one of these things be applicable in each of the four figures, but rather the requirement is that the totality of these be applicable in all of them, even if some of them are only applicable in some. Unless one says that the word 'or' in his statement is in the meaning of 'and' as in the saying of al-Nabigha: '…' Then this also applies to the later figures as well.
Source pages p. 349

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement by the commentator on the matn's wording. The matn had stated that certain conclusions (e.g., universal negative or particular negative) in the four figures are produced by reductio, conversion of the major, or conversion of the minor, using the conjunction 'or' (أو). The commentator points out that this implies each of these methods works for every figure, which is false. For example, conversion of the major does not work in the second and fourth figures, and conversion of the minor only works in the second. He then suggests a corrected phrasing that uses 'and' (واو) or interprets 'or' as 'and' in the sense of inclusive conjunction, citing a line of poetry by al-Nabigha as a precedent. The commentator emphasizes that not every method applies to every figure; rather, the totality of methods covers all figures. This critique refines the logical understanding of how syllogistic conclusions are derived.

Key Terms
المصنف
عكس الكبرى
عكس الصغرى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, in which figures does conversion of the major premise NOT apply?
A) First and third
B) Second and fourth
C) First and second
D) Third and fourth

Key Takeaway
Precise wording in logical rules is essential: using 'or' can falsely imply that every method applies universally, whereas careful specification (with 'and' or inclusive 'or') is needed to reflect the actual domains of validity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Task: Consider the statement: 'In the first figure, a universal affirmative conclusion is obtained by conversion of the minor premise or by reductio.' Using the lesson's critique, identify why this use of 'or' is problematic and correct it by specifying which method actually applies.
?
Task: In a debate, a student claims that conversion of the major premise can be used to derive a conclusion in the second figure. Based on the commentator's clarification, explain whether this claim is valid and why.

The Syllogism

Condition of Actual Unity in the Minor Premise
Explains the requirement of actual unity in the minor premise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a given minor premise in a first-figure syllogism satisfies the condition of actual unity and explain why possible or negative premises are invalid.

Core Idea
The minor premise must be actually affirmative; possible unity is not enough.

In the first figure of categorical syllogism, the minor premise must connect the minor term to the middle term with actual certainty. A negative premise destroys all unity, while a merely possible premise only suggests hesitation. This lesson unpacks why the condition of actual unity (bi al-fiʿl) is essential for a valid proof.

Guided Path
1
Identify the minor and middle terms
In a first-figure syllogism, locate the minor term (subject of conclusion) and the middle term (appearing in both premises). The minor premise should assert a relationship between them.
The major premise relates the middle to the major term.
2
Check the quality of the minor premise
Determine whether the minor premise is affirmative or negative. If negative, there is no unity at all between minor and middle – the condition fails immediately.
3
Check the modality of the minor premise
If the minor premise is affirmative, examine its modality. Is it actual (e.g., 'All A are B') or possible (e.g., 'Some A may be B')? Only actual affirmations provide the required definite connection.
A possible premise indicates hesitation, not certainty.
4
Apply the condition to a concrete example
Take a theological proof: 'Every temporally originated thing has a cause; the world is temporally originated; therefore the world has a cause.' The minor 'the world is temporally originated' is actual and affirmative, so the judgment passes. If it were 'the world may be temporally originated', the conclusion would not follow.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(60) His saying: 'That they are not united at all' – and that is because, as has been repeated many times, that the negation of a composite sometimes is by the negation of one of its parts and sometimes by the negation of all of them. The conclusion is that he required in the minor premise the unity of the minor term with the middle term and that this unity be actual. So if this composite is negated, then either it is by the negation of all parts, that there is no unity between the minor and the middle at all, so that the minor is negative, ruling that the minor and middle are mutually exclusive; or it is by the negation of one part, that there is unity between them but not actual, rather possible, so that the minor is an affirmative possible. (61) An explicative conjunction, and likewise his saying: 'and the minor is an affirmative possible' – understand. (62) His saying: 'or they are united but not actually, rather potentially' and his saying: 'and the minor is an affirmative possible' is also an explicative conjunction to his saying: 'or they are united but not actually'. It is not hidden that the judgment qualified by possibility is not a real judgment, because its meaning is that this may be this and may not be, and the meaning of this speech is hesitation in the judgment, not certainty, as is clear from the approximation p. 98. (63) His saying: 'the judgment does not pass from the middle actually to the minor' – his saying 'does not pass' is the answer to 'if' in his saying: 'for if the minor is not united'. And the intended meaning of 'the judgment' is the judgment obtained in the major premise, whether it be affirmative or negative, as he stated at the beginning. So the 'lam' is for preceding remembrance, like in His statement: 'And We sent to...'
Source pages p. 350

Commentary
This passage discusses the condition for a valid first figure syllogism regarding the minor premise. The author of the matn had stated that the minor term and middle term must be 'united' (muttaḥid) in the minor premise for the judgment of the major premise to pass to the minor term. The commentator explains that this unity must be actual (bi al-fiʿl), not merely possible (bi al-imkān). If the premise is negative (there is no unity at all), or if it is affirmative but only possible (e.g., 'some A may be B'), then the composite condition is negated. The possible judgment is not a real judgment because it involves hesitation, not certainty. Thus, the minor premise must be actually affirmative to allow the major premise's judgment to transfer. The commentator also clarifies that the 'judgment' in the major can be either affirmative or negative. This elaboration guards against the misconception that a possible premise suffices for a valid syllogism.

Key Terms
اتحاد (ittiḥād): Unity or identity between two terms; in this context, the minor term and middle term must be actually (not merely possibly) united in the minor premise. [Note: Stable term; condition of actuality is standard in first figure.]
بالفعل (bi al-fiʿl): Actual, as opposed to potential or possible; the unity must be actual for the syllogism to be valid. [Note: Standard modal distinction; no shift.]
بالامكان (bi al-imkān): Possible; a judgment qualified by possibility is not sufficient because it indicates uncertainty. [Note: Standard term in logic.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) It must be possible
C) It must be actual and affirmative
D) It must be universal

Key Takeaway
The minor premise in the first figure must be actually affirmative; otherwise, the judgment cannot pass from the middle to the minor term.
