Why First Figure Moods Yield Particular Conclusions
Author explains why all six valid moods of the first figure produce particular conclusions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the first and fourth moods of the first figure produce particular conclusions despite having universal premises.

Core Idea
Even universal premises can only yield particular conclusions when the minor term is more general.

In the previous lesson, you learned the conditions that reduce the sixteen possible moods to six valid ones in the first figure. Now the commentator Muhammad Ali explains a surprising property: all six moods produce only particular conclusions. For most moods, this is obvious because one premise is particular. But for the first and fourth moods, both premises are universal, yet the conclusion is particular. Why? The answer lies in the relationship between the terms.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first figure structure
Recall that in the first figure, the middle term is subject of the major premise and predicate of the minor premise. The minor term is subject of the conclusion and predicate of the minor premise; the major term is predicate of the conclusion and predicate of the major premise.
2
Understand the generality issue
The predicate (major term) can be more general than the subject (middle term). If the major term is more general, a universal conclusion would assert that a more specific attribute applies to all members of a more general class, which is impossible.
3
Apply to the first mood example
Consider the premises 'Every human is an animal' and 'Every human is rational'. The major term 'rational' is more specific than the middle term 'human'. A universal conclusion 'Every animal is rational' would wrongly predicate the specific of the general universally. Hence the conclusion must be particular: 'Some animal is rational'.
4
Apply to the fourth mood example
Similarly, from 'Every human is an animal' and 'No human is a horse', a universal negative conclusion 'No animal is a horse' would wrongly negate the specific from all of the general. The correct particular conclusion is 'Some animal is not a horse'.
5
Generalize to all first figure moods
All valid moods of the first figure share this property: whether because one premise is already particular (moods 2,3,5,6) or because of the generality relation (moods 1 and 4), the conclusion is always particular.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قد عرفت أن الضروب المحتملة في كل شكل ستة عشر حاصلة من ضرب الصغريات الأربع في الكبريات الأربع فبالشرط الأول سقط ثمانية أضرب حاصلة من ضرب الصغريين السالبتين في الكبريات الأربع. وبالشرط الثاني سقط اثنان الصغرى الموجبة الجزئية مع الجزئيتين فبقي ستة يذكرها المحشي على التفصيل ووجه الترتيب بينها يعلم من القاعدة التي ذكرناها في بيان شرايط الشكل الأول. و هذه الضروب كلها مشتركة في أنها لا تنتج إلا الجزئية: أما فيما عدا الضرب الأول والرابع، فظاهر لظهور أن إحدى المقدمتين فيها جزئية. وأما فيهما، فإنه وإن كانت كلتا مقدمتيهما كلية، لكن لما كان الأصغر محمولا في الصغرى والأوسط موضوعا كما هو قياس هذا الشكل، والمحمول من حيث هو محمول يجوز أن يكون أعم من الموضوع، فلم يلزم من الحكم بالأكبر على الأوسط بالكلية، الحكم به على الأصغر بالكلية. فإن ثبوت حكم الأخص بالإيجاب أو السلب لا يستلزم ثبوته للأعم كذلك، كما هو ظاهر. مثلا إذا قلنا: كل إنسان حيوان وكل إنسان ناطق لا يصح لنا أن نقول كل حيوان ناطق، وإلا لزم صدق الأخص على الأعم كلية وهو ممتنع. وكذا إذا قلنا كل إنسان حيوان ولا شيء من الإنسان بفرس لا يصح أن نقول: لا شيء من الحيوان بفرس، وإلا لزم سلب الأخص عن الأعم وهو ممتنع أيضا. إنما كانت نتيجة هذا الضرب جزئية مع أن مقدمتيه كليتان، لأن الموضوع قد يكون في المطلوب أعم وصدق الأخص على الأعم كلية ممتنع. وأما نتيجة الضرب الرابع فوجه كونها جزئية هو أنه لو كانت كلية يلزم سلب الأخص عن جميع أفراد الأعم فيما إذا كان الموضوع أعم كما في نحو: كل إنسان حيوان ولا شيء من الإنسان بفرس، وهو ممتنع.
You have known that the possible moods in each figure are sixteen, resulting from multiplying the four minor premises by the four major premises. By the first condition, eight moods drop out, resulting from multiplying the two negative minor premises by the four major premises. By the second condition, two drop out: the particular affirmative minor with the two particular [majors]. So six remain, which the commentator mentions in detail, and the order among them is known from the rule we mentioned in explaining the conditions of the first figure. And all these moods share in that they produce only the particular: As for other than the first and fourth moods, it is obvious because one of the two premises therein is particular. And as for those two, even though both of their premises are universal, since the minor term is a predicate in the minor premise and the middle term is a subject, as is the measure of this figure, and the predicate qua predicate may be more general than the subject, therefore it does not follow from the judgment of the major term about the middle term universally that the judgment applies to the minor term universally. For the affirmation of a judgment of the more specific by affirmation or negation does not necessitate its affirmation of the more general likewise, as is apparent. For example, if we say: 'Every human is an animal' and 'Every human is rational', it is not valid for us to say 'Every animal is rational'; otherwise it would necessitate the universal truth of the more specific about the more general, which is impossible. Similarly, if we say: 'Every human is an animal' and 'No human is a horse', it is not valid to say: 'No animal is a horse'; otherwise it would necessitate the negating of the more specific from the more general, which is also impossible. The result of this mood is particular even though both of its premises are universal, because the subject in the desired conclusion may be more general, and the universal truth of the more specific about the more general is impossible. As for the result of the fourth mood, the reason it is particular is that if it were universal it would necessitate negating the more specific from all individuals of the more general when the subject is more general, as in the example: 'Every human is an animal' and 'No human is a horse', which is impossible.
Source pages p. 350

Commentary
This passage is an explanation by the commentator (Muhammad Ali) of why all six valid moods of the first figure yield particular conclusions. The author first reminds that the total possible moods for any figure are sixteen, obtained by multiplying four types of minor premises (universal affirmative, particular affirmative, universal negative, particular negative) by four types of major premises. Two conditions eliminate some: (1) both premises cannot be negative, which removes eight moods where the minor is negative; (2) if both premises are particular, they yield no conclusion, removing two more (particular affirmative minor with particular affirmative major and with particular negative major). Thus six moods remain. The commentator then notes that all six share the property of producing a particular conclusion. For moods other than the first and fourth, this is obvious because one premise is itself particular. However, for the first and fourth moods, both premises are universal, yet the conclusion is particular. The reason is structural: in the first figure, the minor term is predicate in the minor premise and the middle term is subject; the major term is predicate in the major premise. The predicate (major term) can be more general than the subject (middle term). Therefore, even if we know that the major term applies universally to the middle term, it does not follow that it applies universally to the minor term, because the minor term may be more specific. The commentator gives two examples from the Islamic logical tradition: (1) 'Every human is an animal' and 'Every human is rational' does not allow us to conclude 'Every animal is rational', because that would wrongly assert that a more specific attribute (rationality) applies universally to a more general subject (animal). (2) 'Every human is an animal' and 'No human is a horse' does not allow 'No animal is a horse', because that would deny a more specific term (horse) universally from a more general term (animal), which is false. Thus the conclusion must be particular: 'Some animal is rational' and 'Some animal is not a horse' (or equivalent). The commentator further explains that for the first mood (Barbara, with two universal affirmatives), the conclusion is particular because the subject in the conclusion (the minor term) may be more general; it is impossible for the more specific to be truly predicated of the more general universally. For the fourth mood (Celarent, with universal negative major and universal affirmative minor), a universal conclusion would require negating the more specific from all individuals of the more general, which is impossible as shown. Therefore, all valid moods of the first figure produce particular conclusions.

Key Terms
الضرب
الشكل الأول

Key Takeaway
The first and fourth moods of the first figure produce particular conclusions because the minor term may be more general than the middle, making a universal conclusion impossible.

Enumerating the Valid Moods of the Third Figure
Author enumerates the six valid moods remaining after eliminating eight by first condition and two by second.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to enumerate the six valid moods of the third figure and justify why the other ten combinations are invalid.

Core Idea
Only six out of sixteen mood combinations survive the conditions in the third figure.

Now that you understand the conditions for syllogistic validity, we apply them to the third figure. The author guides us through the elimination process, leaving only six productive moods. This exercise sharpens your ability to quickly assess which premise combinations yield a valid conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Recall the sixteen possible combinations
In each figure, there are four types of minor premises (A, E, I, O) and four types of major premises, giving 4 × 4 = 16 possible moods.
A = universal affirmative, E = universal negative, I = particular affirmative, O = particular negative.
2
Apply the first condition: minor cannot be negative
The first condition eliminates all moods where the minor premise is negative (E or O). This removes 2 (negative minors) × 4 (majors) = 8 moods.
After this, only minors A and I remain.
3
Apply the second condition: particular minor paired with particular majors
The second condition drops moods where the minor is particular affirmative (I) and the major is particular (I or O). This removes 2 more moods: (I, I) and (I, O).
Now only (A, A), (A, E), (A, I), (A, O), (I, A), (I, E) remain.
4
Count the remaining six valid moods
After both conditions, 16 - 8 - 2 = 6 moods survive. These are the productive moods of the third figure.
The commentator will detail each mood's conclusion and validity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

You have known that the possible moods in each figure are sixteen, resulting from multiplying the four minors by the four majors. By the first condition, eight moods are dropped, resulting from multiplying the two negative minors by the four majors. And by the second condition, two are dropped: the particular affirmative minor with the two particulars. So six remain, which the commentator will detail, and the order among them is known from the rule we mentioned in the explanation of the conditions of the first figure.

Commentary
This passage enumerates the valid moods of the third figure. The author states that theoretically there are sixteen possible combinations of four types of minor premises (universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative) and four types of major premises. Two conditions eliminate the invalid ones. First condition: both minors cannot be negative—this eliminates eight moods (the two negative minors combined with each of the four majors). Second condition: both minors cannot be negative is already covered, but here it specifies that when the minor is particular affirmative, the major cannot also be particular? Actually the text says 'the particular affirmative minor with the two particulars' meaning that the combination of a particular affirmative minor with a particular universal or particular particular? The wording 'مع الجزئيتين' likely means 'with the two particular [majors]' i.e., particular affirmative and particular negative majors? But given the typical conditions for the third figure, the valid moods require that the minor be affirmative and universal, or if particular then the major must be universal. The exact meaning: after the first condition, eight are dropped; then by the second condition, two more are dropped: the minor being particular affirmative and the majors being the two particular ones (i.e., particular affirmative and particular negative). So the remaining six moods are those that satisfy both conditions. The commentator will detail them, and the ordering follows the rules set for the first figure.

Why Third Figure Conclusions Are Always Particular
Author explains limited scope of third figure conclusions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why even universal premises in the third figure yield only particular conclusions, and you will be able to test the validity of any attempted universal conclusion in that figure.

Core Idea
In the third figure, the specific cannot be forced upon the general.

In the first and fourth moods of the third figure, both premises can be universal. Yet the conclusion remains particular. Why? This passage by the commentator (probably Sheikh Abd al-Rahim) unpacks the reason: the minor term is predicated in the minor premise and serves as the subject of the conclusion's predicate, but the subject in the conclusion may be more general than the minor term. A universal judgment about the specific cannot automatically extend to the general. We will work through this logical constraint step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the moods with universal premises in the third figure
The author notes that for moods other than the first and fourth, particularity is obvious because one premise is particular. Focus on the first and fourth moods of the third figure, where both premises are universal.
These are the moods that might tempt one to expect a universal conclusion.
2
Examine the term order in the third figure
In the third figure, the middle term is the subject in both premises. The minor term is the predicate in the minor premise, and the major term is the predicate in the major premise. Thus the minor term is predicated of the middle.
Therefore the minor term (a specific) is said about the middle (possibly more general).
3
Consider the relation of minor to middle in the premises
Because the minor term is a predicate in the minor premise, it is always more specific (akhaṣṣ) than the middle term? Actually, it can be more specific or equal; but in the examples, the minor is more specific (e.g., 'human' is more specific than 'animal'). The key point: the predicate may be more specific than its subject.
The minor term is 'insān' (human) which is more specific than 'ḥayawān' (animal).
4
Test the attempted universal conclusion
If we try to conclude universally, e.g., 'Every animal is rational' from 'Every human is animal' and 'Every human is rational', we are asserting that the specific (rational) applies to every individual of the general (animal). This is invalid because the specific may not extend to all of the general.
Similarly, 'No animal is a horse' from 'Every human is animal' and 'No human is a horse' would require the specific negation to cover all animals.
5
Generalize the reason: the specific cannot be affirmed or negated universally of the general
The impossibility arises because the conclusion's subject (the middle term) is more general than the minor term. Since the premises only speak about the minor term (which is a subset), the conclusion cannot be universal about the broader set.
Only a particular conclusion escapes the impossibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : و هذه الضروب كلها مشتركة في انها لا تنتج الاجزئية: أما فيما عدا الضرب الأول والرابع، فظاهر لظهور ان احدى المقدمتين فيها جزئية و امافيهما، فانه و ان كانت كلتا مقدمتيها كلية، لكن لما كان الاصغر محمولا فى الصغرى والاوسط موضوعاً كما هو قياس هذا الشكل والمحمول من حيث هو محمول يجوز ان يكون اعم من الموضوع، فلم يلزم من الحكم بالأكبر على الاوسط بالكلية، الحكم به على الاصغر بالكلية فان ثبوت حكم الاخص بالايجاب او السلب لا يستلزم ثبوته للاعم كذلك، كما هو ظاهر مثلاً اذا قلنا : كل انسان حیوان و کل انسان ناطق لايصح لنا ان نقول كل حيوان ناطق و الالزم صدق الاخص على الاعم كلية و هو ممتنع و كذا اذا قلنا كل انسان حيوان ولاشيء من الانسان بفرس لا يصح ان نقول: لاشيء من الحيوان بفرس والألزم سلب الاخص عن الاعم وهو ممتنع ايضاً. (محمد على) (۶۶) انما كانت نتيجة هذا الضرب جزئية مع ان مقدمتيه كليتان، لان الموضوع قد يكون في المطلوب اعم وصدق الاخص على الاعم كلية ممتنع و اما نتيجة الضرب الرابع فوجه كونها جزئية هو انه لو كانت كلية يلزم سلب الاخص عن جميع افراد الاعم فيما اذا كان الموضوع اعم كما في نحو: كل انسان حيوان ولا شيء من الانسان بفرس و هو ممتنع شيخ عبد الرحيم
His statement: 'And all these moods share that they only yield particular conclusions': As for other than the first and fourth moods, it is clear because one of the premises is particular. But for those two, even though both premises are universal, yet since the minor term is predicated in the minor premise and the middle is subject (as is the rule of this figure), and the predicate as such may be more general than the subject, it does not follow from the universal judgment of the major about the middle that the judgment applies universally to the minor. For the affirmation or negation of a more specific judgment does not entail the same for the more general, as is clear: for example, if we say 'Every human is an animal and every human is rational', it is not valid for us to say 'Every animal is rational', because that would require the specific to be true of the general universally, which is impossible. Similarly, if we say 'Every human is an animal and no human is a horse', it is not valid to say 'No animal is a horse', because that would require negating the specific from the general, also impossible. (Muhammad Ali) (66) The result of this mood is particular even though both premises are universal, because the subject in the conclusion may be more general, and affirming the specific of the general universally is impossible. As for the result of the fourth mood, the reason it is particular is that if it were universal, it would require negating the specific from all individuals of the general when the subject is more general, as in 'Every human is an animal and no human is a horse', which is impossible. — Sheikh Abd al-Rahim
Source pages p. 351

Commentary
This passage defends why all moods of the third figure yield only particular conclusions, even those moods with both premises universal. The author notes that for moods other than the first and fourth (i.e., those with a particular premise), the particularity is obvious. But for the first and fourth moods, which have both premises universal, one might expect a universal conclusion. The explanation: in the third figure, the minor term is predicated of the middle in the minor premise (e.g., 'every human is an animal'), so the minor term (human) is the predicate, and the middle (animal) is subject. In the conclusion, the major term (say, rational or horse) is predicated of the minor term. However, the minor term (human) is more specific than the middle (animal). If the conclusion were universal, it would assert the major term (e.g., rational) of all individuals of the minor term's subject? Actually careful: the conclusion's subject is the middle term (the subject of the major premise). In the third figure, the conclusion has the middle as subject and the major as predicate. But the minor term is the predicate of the minor premise, not directly in the conclusion. The point is that the major term may be more general than the minor term. For example, from 'every human is rational' and 'every human is animal', we cannot conclude 'every animal is rational' because rational (the specific) is not necessarily true of all animals. Similarly, from 'every human is animal' and 'no human is horse', we cannot conclude 'no animal is horse' because negation of a specific from all individuals of a general is false. So in the third figure, the relation of the terms prevents a universal conclusion; only a particular (affirmative or negative) is justified. The comment then reiterates for the fourth mood the same reasoning: a universal conclusion would imply the impossible.

Key Terms
أخص
أعم
جزئية

C) Because the premises are not both universal.
D) Because the minor premise is negative.

Key Takeaway
In the third figure, even universal premises force a particular conclusion because the specific predicate of the minor premise cannot be extended to the entire general subject in the conclusion.

Commentator's Clarification on 'aks' and Two Moods
Commentator clarifies that the phrase covers two moods and that 'aks' refers only to reversing the second mood.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a commentator uses 'aks' in its non-technical sense (swapping premises) versus the technical conversion, and recognize the two moods included in the phrase 'linyutij al-mujibatani'.

Core Idea
In this commentary, 'aks' simply means swapping the premises, not conversion.

Classical logical commentaries often refine ambiguous terms from the original matn. Here, the commentator clarifies that the author's phrase 'linyutij al-mujibatani' actually covers two distinct moods. Additionally, the term 'aks' is given a non-technical meaning – swapping premises – which is crucial for correctly understanding the author's intention without falling into repetition or confusion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the phrase being clarified
Find the original author's phrase 'linyutij al-mujibatani ma'a al-mujibati al-kulliyati'. The commentator explains that this phrase refers to two specific moods, not just one.
The phrase translates to 'so that the two affirmatives produce a universal affirmative'.
2
List the two moods included
The first mood consists of two universal affirmative premises. The second mood consists of a particular affirmative minor and a universal affirmative major. Recognize that both are valid first-figure moods.
First mood: both premises universal affirmative. Second mood: minor particular affirmative, major universal affirmative.
3
Understand the commentator's point about 'aks'
The original says 'wa bil'aksi' (and by its reversal). The commentator states this reversal applies only to the second mood, because reversing the first mood (two universal affirmatives) yields the exact same mood – no new syllogism.
Reversal here means swapping the positions of the premises (minor becomes major and vice versa).
4
Distinguish the two meanings of 'aks'
Note the explicit shift: in this passage 'aks' means simply swapping the two premises, not the logical operation of conversion (changing subject and predicate). The commentator and a super-commentator stress this to avoid misinterpretation.
Technical 'aks' (conversion) changes the proposition form; here it only changes premise order.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يعنى : ان قول المصنف لينتج الموجبتان مع الموجبة الكلية»، متضمن الضربين من الضروب السنة الاول المركب من الموجبتين الكليتين والثاني المركب من الموجبة الجزئية و الموجبة الكلية. ومراده من قوله: «و بالعكس عكس الضرب الثاني فقط لكونه اقرب اليه في الملاحظة والاعتبار لاعكس كليها ليلزم التكرار فان عكس الأول هو الاول كما هو ظاهر. (میرزا محمد علی (ره) (۶۸) لان المراد من العكس هيهنا تبديل المقدمتين لا معناه الاصطلاحي (عبد الرحيم)
He means: The author's statement 'so that the two affirmatives produce a universal affirmative' includes two of the six moods: the first composed of two universal affirmatives, and the second composed of a particular affirmative and a universal affirmative. And by his statement 'and by reversal', he only intended the reversal of the second mood, because it is closer to him in observation and consideration; he did not reverse both, because that would cause repetition. For reversal of the first mood is identical to itself, as is clear. (Mirza Muhammad Ali) (68) Because here the meaning of 'aks' is swapping the two premises, not its technical meaning. (Abd al-Rahim)
Source pages p. 351

Commentary
This passage clarifies a specific phrase from the original text. The original author said 'linyutij al-mujibatani ma'a al-mujibati al-kulliyati' meaning 'so that the two affirmative premises produce a universal affirmative'. The commentator explains that this actually covers two moods: (1) two universal affirmatives, (2) a particular affirmative minor with a universal affirmative major. Then the original says 'wa bil'aksi' meaning 'and by its reversal'. The commentator says this reversal applies only to the second mood, because reversing the first mood (both universal affirmatives) yields the same thing. For the second mood, swapping the premises turns it into a valid mood of the first figure. The reversal here does not mean the technical logical conversion; it simply means exchanging the positions of the two premises (so the minor becomes major and vice versa). This is a subtle textual point to avoid misunderstanding the author's intention.

Permissibility of Implicit Repetition in Syllogistic Reversal
Supercommentator clarifies that repetition is only blameworthy if explicit.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when repetition in logical argumentation is blameworthy versus acceptable, based on the principle that speech aims to convey benefit.

Core Idea
Repetition is only blameworthy when the wording is solely for repeating a previous meaning.

In this passage, the supercommentator (Mirza Muhammad Ali) clarifies a subtle point about repetition in logical figures. The commentator had written 'fata'amal' (so contemplate), hinting at a difficulty. The supercommentator explains that the original author may have intended to allow the reversal of both moods, because repetition is only problematic when it is the explicit purpose of the wording. This lesson will help you see how commentators refine logical rules by distinguishing between explicit and implicit repetition.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the role of 'fata'amal'
In hashiya literature, 'fata'amal' is a cue that the commentator sees a subtle issue. Here, it signals that the permissibility of reversing both moods needs deeper reflection.
This term often precedes a resolution or alternative interpretation.
2
Distinguish explicit vs implicit repetition
The supercommentator states that repetition is blameworthy only if the wording is brought specifically to convey the repeated meaning (explicit). If the repeated meaning arises incidentally from new speech meant to convey a fresh meaning, it is not blameworthy.
This distinction hinges on the intention behind the wording.
3
Apply the principle to the reversal of both moods
The original author might have intended the reversal ('aks') to apply to both moods. The only objection would be repetition. But since the reversal of the first mood would not be a mere repetition but a separate logical operation, it is acceptable.
This shows how a potential flaw in the text is resolved by clarifying the nature of repetition.
4
Evaluate the supercommentator's reasoning
The reasoning is based on the maxim that speech rests on conveying benefit, not on repetition. Thus, incidental repetition is not a defect. This is a sound logical principle applied to textual interpretation.
Notice how the supercommentator uses a general principle (speech is for benefit) to resolve a specific technical issue.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: «فتأمل»: لعل وجهه انه يجوز ان يكون مراد المصنف عكس الضربين كليهما ايضاً اذلا يعقل فيه مانع الا التكرار وهو انما يعاب اذا حصل بلفظ جيء لاداء المعنى المكرر بخصوصه و اما اذا اتى بكلام لافادة معنى لم يذكر بعد وحصل في ضمنه معنى أخر ذكر قبل تبعاً فلا، كما يظهر من تعليلهم ذلك بان مبنى الكلام على الافادة لا على الاعادة فتأمل. (ميرزا محمد علی)
His statement: 'So contemplate' — perhaps its reason is that it is possible that the author intended the reversal of both moods as well, for there is no obstacle except repetition, and repetition is only blameworthy when it occurs in wording brought specifically to convey the repeated meaning. But if speech comes to convey a meaning not previously mentioned, and within it another meaning previously mentioned occurs incidentally, then there is no blame. This is clear from their explanation that speech is based on conveying benefit, not repetition. So contemplate. (Mirza Muhammad Ali)
Source pages p. 351

Commentary
This passage comments on the word 'fata'amal' (so contemplate) used by a previous commentator. The supercommentator suggests that the original author might have intended the reversal ('aks') to apply to both moods, not just the second. The only objection to that would be repetition, but repetition is not automatically prohibited. It is only blameworthy if the wording is designed solely to repeat a previously stated meaning. If the new speech conveys a new meaning, and the repeated meaning comes only incidentally, then it is acceptable. The principle is that speech is for conveying benefit, not for repetition. So the word 'fata'amal' invites the reader to ponder this possibility. This is a typical hashiya-style refinement: the supercommentator offers an alternative interpretation that softens a perceived textual problem.

Key Takeaway
Repetition is not automatically a flaw; only explicit repetition aimed at re-conveying a known meaning is blameworthy, while incidental repetition in new speech is acceptable.

Reductio Ad Absurdum in the Third Figure
Author presents the method of proof by contradiction for the third figure with an example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct a proof by contradiction for a valid third-figure categorical syllogism and explain why the minor premise must be an actual affirmative.

Core Idea
Proof by contradiction in the third figure uses a universal contradictory as major because the conclusion is particular.

In this lesson, we explore how the proof by contradiction (dalīl al-khalaf) is applied specifically to the third figure of categorical syllogisms. The author of the hashiya explains that unlike the second figure, the minor premise here is an actual affirmative, making it suitable as a minor for a new syllogism. The conclusion of the third figure is particular, so its contradictory is a universal proposition that can serve as the major. Understanding this procedure strengthens your ability to validate syllogisms and construct indirect proofs in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original third-figure syllogism
Take the given example: 'Every human is an animal' and 'Every human is straight-statured' yield 'Some animal is straight-statured'. This is a third-figure syllogism (both majors are same subject, conclusion particular affirmative).
The minor premise is 'Every human is an animal' (actual affirmative) and the major premise is 'Every human is straight-statured'.
2
Note that the minor is actual affirmative
The author emphasizes that the minor here is 'actual affirmative' (mūjiba fiʿliyya), like in the first figure. This allows it to serve directly as a minor in a new first-figure syllogism. Recognize that the minor is not possible or hypothetical but a definite affirmative statement.
3
Assume the contradictory of the conclusion
The conclusion of the original syllogism is 'Some animal is straight-statured'. Its contradictory is 'No animal is straight-statured', which is a universal negative. This assumption will be used as a premise in the reductio proof.
The contradictory is universal because the original conclusion is particular.
4
Construct the new syllogism and derive contradiction
Combine the original minor ('Every human is an animal') with the assumed contradictory ('No animal is straight-statured') as a new syllogism in the first figure. This yields 'No human is straight-statured'. But this contradicts the original major premise ('Every human is straight-statured'), showing the assumption is false and the original conclusion must be true.
The new syllogism is valid: first figure, mood Camestres (universal affirmative minor, universal negative major, universal negative conclusion).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يشير الى ان دليل الخلف فى هذا الشكل يخالف ما تقدم في الشكل الثاني كما هو ظاهر. (محمد علی) (۷۱) و ذلك لما عرفت من ان الصغرى هنا موجبة فعلية كما فى الشكل الاول فتصلح ان تكون صغرى له و النتيجة في تجميعها جزئية فيكون نقيضها كلية البتة فتصلح ان يكون كبرى له، مثلا اذا صدق کل انسان حیوان و کل انسان مستقيم القامة صدق: بعض الحيوان مستقيم القامة والالصدق نقيضه اعنى لاشيء من الخيوان بمستقيم القامة، فتضمه مع الصغرى فنقول : کل انسان حیوان ولاشيء من الحيوان بمستقيم القامة ينتج: لاشيء من الانسان مستقيم القامة وقد كان حكم الأصل كل انسان مستقيم القامة هف وعلى هذا قياس البواق. (محمد على)
He points out that the proof by contradiction in this figure differs from what was presented in the second figure, as is clear. (Muhammad Ali) (71) And that is because you know that the minor here is an actual affirmative, as in the first figure, so it is suitable as a minor for it. And the conclusion, when collected, is particular, so its contradictory is necessarily universal, and it is suitable as a major for it. For example: if 'Every human is an animal' and 'Every human is straight-statured' are true, then 'Some animal is straight-statured' is true. Its contradictory, i.e., 'No animal is straight-statured', when joined with the minor, we say: 'Every human is an animal and no animal is straight-statured' yields 'No human is straight-statured', whereas the original judgment was 'Every human is straight-statured'. And the rest are analogous. (Muhammad Ali)
Source pages p. 352

Commentary
This passage explains the proof by contradiction (daliil al-khalaf) specific to the third figure. It differs from that in the second figure. In the third figure, the minor premise is an actual affirmative (like in the first figure), so it can serve as the minor of a new syllogism. The original conclusion of the given mood is particular; its contradictory is a universal proposition. This universal contradictory can serve as the major premise. For example, suppose we have a valid syllogism in the third figure: 'Every human is an animal, every human is straight-statured; therefore, some animal is straight-statured.' To prove it by contradiction, assume the contradictory of the conclusion: 'No animal is straight-statured.' Combine this with the original minor 'Every human is an animal' in the first figure (since the minor is actual affirmative, it fits the first figure). From these, we derive 'No human is straight-statured.' This contradicts the original minor premise 'Every human is straight-statured', showing the assumption is false. So the original conclusion must be true. This method works for all moods of the third figure.

Key Terms
دليل الخلف
موجبة فعلية
نقيض

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) No human is an animal.

Key Takeaway
In the third figure's proof by contradiction, the minor must be an actual affirmative so it can serve in a first-figure syllogism, and the contradictory of the particular conclusion is universal, yielding a contradiction with the original major.

Reducing Third and Fourth Figures to the First
Demonstrates reduction of third and fourth figures to the first by inverting a premise or reversing premise order.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reduce any syllogism from the third or fourth figure to the first figure by applying inversion or reversal of premises.

Core Idea
Any syllogism converts to the first figure by inverting one premise or reversing the premise order.

You already know that the first figure is the only one that yields certain conclusions directly. But what if a syllogism you encounter is in the third or fourth figure? The logicians show that you can 'reduce' it to the first figure by simple operations: either invert a premise (swap subject and predicate) or reverse the order of the two premises. This keeps the conclusion the same, while turning the syllogism into the reliable first figure. Let's see how this works through the comments on our passage.

Guided Path
1
Identify the figure of the syllogism
Look at the position of the middle term (the term repeated in both premises). In the third figure, it is subject in both; in the fourth, it is subject in the minor and predicate in the major.
2
Apply the appropriate transformation
For the third figure: invert the minor premise (swap subject and predicate) to make the middle term predicate in the minor. For the fourth figure: reverse the order of the two premises (make the major premise become the minor and vice versa).
Inversion here means simple conversion (e.g., 'Some S is P' to 'Some P is S').
3
Check that the result is a first figure syllogism
After the operation, confirm that the middle term is predicate in the minor and subject in the major. The premises should still lead to the same conclusion.
4
Apply the transformation to a concrete example
Take the third figure example: 'Every human is animal' and 'Some human is rational'. Invert the minor 'Some human is rational' to 'Some rational is human'. Now the premises are 'Every human is animal' (major, middle as subject?) Actually careful: after inversion, we have 'Some rational is human' (now minor?) and 'Every human is animal' (major). The middle term 'human' is now predicate in the minor and subject in the major → first figure. Reverse order of premises if needed.
Follow the text's example step by step.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: «ليرجع الى الشكل الاول»: وذلك لما عرفت سابقاً من انه لا مخالفة بينهما الا في ان الوسط موضوع فى صغراه و محمول فى صغرى الشكل الاول فاذا عكست هى بان جعل موضوعها محمولاً وبالعكس كان الشكل الاول بعينه. (محمد على) (۷۳) ليصلح ان يكون كبرى للشكل الاول (عبد الرحيم) (۷۴) اذلا فرق بينهما الا في الكبرى، فان الوسط موضوع فى كبراء و محمول في كبرى الشكل الرابع فاذا عكست هى يكون هو الشكل الرابع بعينه (میرزا محمد علی) (۷۵) اى ليرتد الشكل الرابع شكلا اولاً، فان الاوسط فيه موضوع في الصغرى ومحمول في الكبرى وبالعكس فى الشكل الأول فاذا عكس الترتيب فيه حصل الشكل الاول بعينه. (محمد على) (۷۶) قوله: كما في الضرب الاول و الثالث : مثلاً نقول فيها كلما صدق كل انسان حیوان و کل انسان او بعضه ناطق صدق قولنا بعض الحيوان ناطق و ذلك بمكس الكبرى بان نقول : كل انسان حیوان و بعض الناطق انسان ليصير شكلا رابعاً ثم بعكس الترتيب بان تجعل الكبرى الصغرى و بالعكس ليصير شكلا اولا :هكذا بعض الناطق انسان و كل انسان حيوان ينتج بعض الناطق حيوان تنعكس إلى قولنا بعض الحيوان ناطق و هو المطلوب. (محمد علی)
His statement: 'so that it returns to the first figure': And that is because you have known previously that there is no difference between them except that the middle is subject in its [the third's?] minor and predicate in the minor of the first figure. So if it is inverted, by making its subject a predicate and vice versa, it becomes the first figure itself. (Muhammad Ali) (73) So that it may become a major for the first figure. (Abd al-Rahim) (74) For there is no difference between them except in the major, since the middle is subject in its major and predicate in the major of the fourth figure. So if it is inverted, it becomes the fourth figure itself. (Mirza Muhammad Ali) (75) That is, so that the fourth figure returns to the first figure. For the middle in it is subject in the minor and predicate in the major, and vice versa in the first figure. So if the order is reversed, it becomes the first figure itself. (Muhammad Ali) (76) His statement: 'as in the first and third moods' – for example, we say: whenever 'Every human is an animal' and 'Every human or some human is rational' are true, then our statement 'Some animal is rational' is true. And that is by converting the major, so we say: 'Every human is an animal' and 'Some rational is human', so that it becomes a fourth figure. Then by reversing the order, making the major the minor and vice versa, it becomes the first figure: thus 'Some rational is human' and 'Every human is animal' yields 'Some rational is animal', which converts to our statement 'Some animal is rational', which is the desired conclusion. (Muhammad Ali)
Source pages p. 352

Commentary
This series of comments explains how the third and fourth figures can be reduced to the first figure. Comment (72) explains that the third figure differs from the first only in the position of the middle term: in the third figure, the middle is subject in the minor premise, while in the first figure, it is predicate in the minor. By 'inverting' (i.e., converting) the minor premise of the third figure (making its subject the predicate and vice versa), the middle becomes predicate, thus yielding the first figure. Comment (74) notes a similar relation between the third and fourth figures: the difference is in the major premise; inverting the major of the third yields the fourth. Comment (75) then shows how the fourth figure can be returned to the first: since the middle is subject in the minor and predicate in the major in the fourth, opposite to the first, reversing the order of the premises (making the minor the major and vice versa) transforms it into the first figure. Finally, comment (76) gives a concrete example: from 'Every human is an animal' and 'Some human is rational' (or 'Every human is rational'), we want to prove 'Some animal is rational'. First, we convert the major 'Some human is rational' to 'Some rational is human' (by simple conversion). This gives a fourth figure syllogism: 'Every human is animal' (major? actually careful). Then we reverse the order: 'Some rational is human' becomes the minor, 'Every human is animal' becomes the major, yielding a first figure syllogism: 'Some rational is human, every human is animal, therefore some rational is animal.' By converting the conclusion, we get 'Some animal is rational'. This demonstrates the reduction process.

Conditions for Third Figure Productive Moods
Enumeration of the two necessary conditions for a valid mood in the third figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a given mood in the third figure is productive by checking the two disjunctive conditions.

Core Idea
Two alternative conditions, not one; true disjunction governs third figure validity.

In the third figure of categorical syllogisms, not every combination of premises yields a valid conclusion. The logicians have identified two exclusive conditions that, if either holds, guarantee a productive mood. Understanding these conditions allows you to quickly assess third figure arguments in Islamic legal and theological reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the mood
Determine the quantity and quality of both premises, and note which is the minor (subject of the conclusion) and which is the major.
The minor premise has the minor term (subject of the conclusion) as its subject.
2
Check Condition A
Condition A requires both premises to be affirmative AND the minor premise to be universal. If both hold, the mood is productive regardless of the major's quantity.
Example: 'All A are B; all A are C' yields 'Some B are C'.
3
Check Condition B
Condition B requires the premises to differ in quality (one affirmative, one negative) AND at least one of them to be universal. If condition A fails but condition B holds, the mood is also productive.
The universal can be either premise.
4
Apply the true disjunction
If neither condition A nor condition B is satisfied, the mood is not productive. Both conditions together exhaust the possibilities for valid moods in this figure.
The conditions are mutually exclusive due to the true disjunction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

When Repetition is Permissible in Logic: The Commentator's Insight on 'Consider'
Explaining the possibility that the author intended conversion of both types in the fourth figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between blameworthy and permissible repetition in logical discourse and apply this distinction to evaluate conversions in the fourth figure.

Core Idea
Incidental repetition is not blameworthy; only repetition that adds no new information is problematic.

In this passage, the commentator Mirza Muhammad Ali unpacks the author's cryptic 'Consider' (ta'ammal) by suggesting that the author likely intended to allow conversion of both moods of the fourth figure. The only potential objection is repetition, but the commentator clarifies that repetition is only condemned when it is the sole purpose of the utterance. If a statement conveys new meaning and repetition occurs incidentally, it is acceptable. This subtle refinement helps us understand how logicians handle apparent redundancy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the author's remark and the commentator's proposal
The author says 'Consider' (ta'ammal). The commentator suggests the author may have intended the conversion of both moods (ḍarbayn) of the fourth figure. Note that 'both moods' refers to the two valid moods of the fourth figure.
The commentator says 'as well' (ayḍan), implying he is considering a second possibility beyond what was previously thought.
2
Extract the commentator's argument structure
Premise: No obstacle to converting both moods except repetition (takrār). Objection: Repetition is blameworthy. Response: Repetition is only blameworthy if it is the primary purpose; incidental repetition is not blameworthy. Conclusion: Hence conversion of both moods is permissible.
The commentator uses the phrase 'it is not blameworthy' to make his point.
3
Understand the criterion for blameworthy vs. permissible repetition
Blameworthy repetition occurs when words are brought specifically to convey a meaning already stated (repetition for its own sake). Permissible repetition occurs when a new meaning is intended and the repeated meaning occurs incidentally.
The principle 'speech is for benefit, not repetition' is key.
4
Apply the criterion to the conversion of both moods
If the author converts both moods, the conversion of the second mood may repeat the conclusion of the first? But if each conversion serves to demonstrate a different point (e.g., different premises), the repetition is incidental and not blameworthy.
This application shows how the commentator's refinement resolves the potential objection.

But if one brings speech to benefit a meaning not mentioned before, and within it there incidentally occurs another meaning mentioned before, then it is not blameworthy, as is evident from their rationale that speech is based on benefit, not repetition. So consider. (Mirza Muhammad Ali)

Commentary
This passage is a commentator's note on the author's remark “Consider” (ta’ammal). The commentator, Mirza Muhammad Ali, suggests that the author may have intended the conversion (ʿaks) of both moods (ḍarbayn) of the fourth figure. He argues that the only objection to such a conversion is repetition (takrār), but repetition is only censured when it is a deliberate re-statement of the same meaning. If a statement introduces a new meaning, and the repeated meaning occurs only incidentally, then it is not blameworthy. He supports this by citing a principle: “Speech is for benefit, not repetition.” The passage thus refines the understanding of the author's intent, showing that the conversion of both moods is logically permissible despite apparent repetition.

Reducing the Fourth Figure to the First Figure
Explaining how converting the fourth figure's premises yields the first figure, with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reduce a fourth-figure categorical syllogism to the first figure by converting one premise and transposing the premises.

Core Idea
Convert a premise and swap the order to turn the fourth figure into the first.

The fourth figure is valid but less intuitive than the first. Logicians reduce it to the first by converting the minor or major premise and then reversing the order. This lesson walks through the process using a classical example from the Islamic logical tradition, showing how to obtain the desired conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the middle term's position
In the fourth figure, the middle term is the predicate in the minor premise and the subject in the major premise. For example, in 'Every human is animal' (major) and 'Every human is rational' (minor), the middle term 'human' appears as subject in both? Actually, in a fourth figure, the middle term should be predicate in minor and subject in major. The given example is actually first figure? Let's correct: The passage uses a different structure: they want to prove 'Some animal is rational' from 'Every human is animal' and 'Every human is rational'. That is a first figure? No, it's a fourth figure? The example in the passage is not a straightforward fourth figure; they construct a fourth figure by converting the major. So focus on the steps.
The key is to notice the opposite positions of the middle term in the two figures.
2
Convert the major premise
Take the major premise and convert it (swap subject and predicate). In the example, 'Every human is animal' becomes 'Some animal is human'. This yields a fourth figure: minor 'Every human is rational', major 'Some animal is human'? Wait, the passage directly says: they convert the major to get 'Every human is animal' and 'Some rational is human' (that is, they convert the major originally? Actually they had 'Every human is animal' and 'Some rational is human' as the converted major? The passage is a bit confusing. The guided path should stick to the reduction method: convert the premise that will make the middle term change position. Typically, you convert the minor to make it the major? The passage converts the major and then reverses order. So step: Convert the premise that makes the middle term predicate in one and subject in the other.
Conversion must be valid (e.g., A to I, E to E, etc.)
3
Reverse the order of premises
Swap the two premises so that the premise that was major becomes minor and vice versa. After conversion, the middle term's positions align with the first figure: predicate in the minor, subject in the major. Reversing yields the first figure order.
This is not a logical inference but a rearrangement of premises.
4
Verify the new syllogism is first figure and valid
Check that the resulting syllogism has the middle term as predicate in the minor and subject in the major. Then test the mood against first figure conditions. In the example, after conversion and reordering, we get 'Some rational is human. Every human is animal', which is a first figure Darii (some rational is animal). Then convert the conclusion to get the desired 'Some animal is rational'.
The conclusion may need a final conversion to match the target.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(72) قوله: «ليرجع الى الشكل الاول»: وذلك لما عرفت سابقاً من انه لا مخالفة بينهما الا في ان الوسط موضوع فى صغراه و محمول فى صغرى الشكل الاول فاذا عكست هى بان جعل موضوعها محمولاً وبالعكس كان الشكل الاول بعينه. (محمد على) (73) ليصلح ان يكون كبرى للشكل الاول (عبد الرحيم) (74) اذلا فرق بينهما الا في الكبرى، فان الوسط موضوع فى كبراء و محمول في كبرى الشكل الرابع فاذا عكست هى يكون هو الشكل الرابع بعينه (میرزا محمد علی) (75) اى ليرتد الشكل الرابع شكلا اولاً، فان الاوسط فيه موضوع في الصغرى ومحمول في الكبرى وبالعكس فى الشكل الأول فاذا عكس الترتيب فيه حصل الشكل الاول بعينه. (محمد علی)
(72) His statement: “to return to the first figure”: That is because you know previously that there is no difference between them except that the middle term is the subject in its minor and the predicate in the minor of the first figure. So when it (the minor) is converted, making its subject a predicate and vice versa, it becomes the first figure itself. (Muhammad Ali) (73) So that it may serve as a major for the first figure. (Abd al-Rahim) (74) Since there is no difference between them except in the major, for the middle term is the subject in its major and the predicate in the major of the fourth figure. So when it (the major) is converted, it becomes the fourth figure itself. (Mirza Muhammad Ali) (75) That is, the fourth figure returns to the first figure, for the middle term in it is subject in the minor and predicate in the major, while the opposite in the first figure. So when the order is reversed, the first figure itself results. (Muhammad Ali) (76) His statement: “as in the first and third moods”: For example, we say in them: Whenever it is true that every human is an animal and that every human or some human is rational, then our statement “some animal is rational” is true. And that is by converting the major saying: “Every human is an animal. Some rational is human.” So it becomes a fourth figure. Then by reversing the order, making the major the minor and vice versa, it becomes a first figure: thus “Some rational is human. Every human is an animal.” It produces: “Some rational is animal.” This converts to our statement: “Some animal is rational.” And that is the desired. (Muhammad Ali)
Source pages p. 352

Commentary
This series of comments explains how the fourth figure (al-shakl al-rabiʿ) can be reduced to the first figure through conversion and reordering. Multiple commentators weigh in. The key point is that the middle term has opposite positions in the two figures: in the fourth figure, the middle is subject in the minor and predicate in the major; in the first figure, it is predicate in the minor and subject in the major. By converting the minor premise (switching subject and predicate) and then reversing the order of the premises, the fourth figure becomes the first. An example is given for the first and third moods: from “Every human is an animal” and “Every human is rational” (or “some human is rational”), we want to prove “Some animal is rational”. They first convert the major to get “Every human is animal” and “Some rational is human” (this yields a fourth figure mood). Then they reverse the order to get “Some rational is human” and “Every human is animal”, which is a first figure mood (Barbara or Darii) producing “Some rational is animal”, which converts to the desired conclusion “Some animal is rational”. This illustrates the reduction process.

Definition of 'Uqm (Barrenness) in Logic
Defining the term 'uqm as barrenness or non-productivity in syllogisms, with linguistic origins.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define 'uqm (barrenness) and explain its linguistic origin as used in Islamic logic.

Core Idea
'Uqm means a syllogism that fails to produce a necessary conclusion, like a barren womb.

This passage from Mirza Muhammad Ali's hashiya defines the key term 'uqm (barrenness). Understanding this term is essential because when logicians judge a syllogism to be barren, they mean it does not yield the desired result. The term is rooted in an Arabic metaphor of a womb that cannot give birth. In this lesson, we will dissect both the definition and its linguistic origin.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
Find 'uqm in the Arabic text and note its logical definition: lack of production (intāj).
2
Extract the linguistic origin
Understand the phrase 'raḥim maʿqūma' (a tied-up womb) and how it metaphorically applies to a non-productive syllogism.
3
Distinguish 'uqm from related concepts
Compare 'uqm (non-production) with invalidity: a syllogism can be formally valid but still barren if premises are not actual or conclusion does not follow necessarily.
4
Recognize extended usage
Note the phrase 'kalām ʿaqim' (infertile speech) meaning obscure speech, expanding the metaphor beyond syllogisms.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(82) ای الاختلاف دليل العقم و هو عدم الانتاج من قولهم: رحم معقومة اى مشدودة لا تلد، يجوز الفتح و الضم ومنه كلام عقمى . (بالفتح) و عُقْمى بالضم) ای غامض. (میرزا محمد علی)
(82) That is, the difference is evidence of barrenness (al-ʿuqūm), which is the lack of production (intāj). It derives from their saying: “a barren womb” (raḥim maʿqūma), i.e., tied up, not giving birth. It may be with fatḥ (i.e., ʿaqm) or ḍamm (i.e., ʿuqm). From it comes ‘infertile speech’ (kalām ʿaqim). (with fatḥ) and ʿuqmā (with ḍamm) meaning obscure. (Mirza Muhammad Ali)
Source pages p. 353

Commentary
This passage defines the term ‘uqm (barrenness) as it is used in logic. The commentator, Mirza Muhammad Ali, explains that ‘uqm means the lack of production (ʿadam al-intāj), i.e., a syllogism that fails to yield a necessary conclusion. He gives a linguistic origin: the Arabic phrase “raḥim maʿqūma” (a barren womb), meaning tightly bound and unable to give birth. The term can be vocalized as ‘uqm (with ḍamm) or ‘aqm (with fatḥ). He also notes an extended usage: “kalam ‘aqim” (infertile speech) means obscure speech. This definition clarifies that when logicians speak of a syllogism being ‘aqīm (barren), they mean it does not produce a conclusion.

عقم
انتاج

C) An argument that is sound.
D) A syllogism that produces a conclusion.

Key Takeaway
'Uqm in logic refers to a syllogism's failure to produce a necessary conclusion, originating from the metaphor of a barren womb.

Given the following syllogism: 'Every created thing has a creator. The world is created. Therefore the world has a creator.' Is this syllogism 'aqim or productive? Explain why.

Identify the linguistic root of 'uqm in a short Arabic sentence, and describe how it maps to logical productivity.

The Syllogism
advanced
10 min
First Condition: Both Premises Must Be Actual
First condition: both premises actual

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the first modal condition for the fourth figure by identifying cases where a possible premise prevents a necessary conclusion and explaining why.

Core Idea
A modal mismatch in the fourth figure destroys deduction; both premises must be actual.

The first condition for a productive fourth-figure syllogism according to modality is that both premises be actual (فعلية). If one premise is possible, the conclusion may vary between negative and affirmative depending on the terms, so no fixed conclusion follows. Let's analyze why the author insists on this condition.

Guided Path
1
State the condition
The author asserts that both premises must be actual (necessary or perpetual). Identify this as the first condition.
Actual here means not possible.
2
Understand the reason
If one premise is possible, the truth of the conclusion becomes indeterminate. The author argues through case analysis: if the possible premise is negative, it's covered later; if affirmative, it can be minor or major.
3
Examine the examples
The author gives two examples: (1) Major possible: 'Everything ridden by Zayd is necessarily a horse' and 'Every donkey is possibly ridden by Zayd' – the truth is negative. (2) Changing the major to 'Every neighing thing is possibly ridden by Zayd' – the truth flips to affirmative. Similarly for possible minor.
Notice how the conclusion changes with term substitution.
4
Generalize the condition
Conclude that if one premise is possible, no single conclusion can be deduced. Hence both premises must be actual to ensure a determinate result.
This condition secures the modal consistency required for fourth-figure productivity.

فيه ذلك ليتمكن من اجراء هذا الدليل فيه هذا خلاصة ما ذكروه في هذا المقام.
Know that they mentioned for productivity of the fourth figure according to modality five conditions: First: that both its premises be actual; because if one of them were possible, productivity would not follow—if it were negative, as will come in the next condition, and if affirmative, either as minor or major, and in both cases difference follows. If it were major, the truth in our statement: 'Everything ridden by Zayd is necessarily a horse, and every donkey is possibly ridden by Zayd' in case of assuming that Zayd's riding is restricted to the horse, is the negative; and if we replaced the major with our statement 'Every neighing thing is possibly ridden by Zayd', the truth would be the affirmative. And if it were minor, the truth in the mentioned assumption when we say 'Every braying thing is possibly ridden by Zayd, and every donkey is necessarily a braying thing' is the negative; and if we said 'Every animal is possibly ridden by Zayd, and every neighing thing is necessarily an animal', the truth would be the affirmative. Second: that the negative used in it be from the six propositions whose negatives convert, not from the nine whose negatives do not convert, because the most specific of them, as previously, is the temporal, and it does not produce in this figure whether minor or major. If it were minor, because our statement 'Nothing of the moon is eclipsed at the time of quadrature, not always, and everything that is a crescent moon is necessarily the moon' is true, and 'Some of that which is eclipsed is not possibly a crescent moon' is not true because its contradictory, our statement 'Every eclipsed thing is necessarily a crescent moon' is true. And if it were major, because it is true: our statement 'Every eclipsed thing is necessarily a crescent moon, and nothing of the moon is eclipsed at the time of quadrature, not always', that 'Some crescent moon is not possibly eclipsed' is not true, as before. And if the more specific does not produce, the more general does not produce, as detailed from the commentator. Third: that the minor in the third mode be such that perpetuity applies to it, being necessary or perpetual, or the major such that common temporal applies to it, being from the six propositions whose negatives convert; because if both conditions were absent, the minor would be one of the four general and specific, since the minor in this mode is negative, and it has been conditioned that the negative used in this figure be from the six whose negatives convert. So if the minor is not necessary or perpetual, it must be one of the four mentioned, and then the major would be one of the nine whose negatives do not convert, and that is apparent. The most specific of those minors, the particular conditional, does not produce with the most specific of majors, the temporal, because it is true: 'Nothing of the eclipsed is illuminated by moonlight as long as it is eclipsed, not always, and every moon is eclipsed at the time of interposition, not always' while 'Some illuminated by moonlight is possibly not a moon' is false. Fourth: that the minor in the sixth and eighth modes be one of the two specific, and the major from the six whose negatives convert. As for the first, because its productivity, as will come, is only shown by converting the minor to revert to the second figure; so the minor must be one of the two specific because it is in this mode a particular negative, and it only converts if it is one of the two specific, as before. And the major must be from the six mentioned according to what preceded in the explanation of the conditions of the second figure: if perpetuity does not apply to the minor, then necessarily the major is from the six mentioned. As for the second, because its productivity, as will come, is only shown by reversing the order to revert to the first figure, then converting the conclusion. So its premises must be such that if one were exchanged for the other, they would produce from the first figure a particular negative convertible conclusion, and that only produces this result if the major is one of the two specific and the minor is one of the six mentioned, as is not hidden for one who examines the discussions of mixtures mentioned in the books of the scholars. So the minor in this mode must be one of the two specific because it is the major in the first figure after conversion, and the major from the six mentioned. Fifth: that the major in the seventh mode be one of the two specific, because, as will be mentioned, its productivity is only shown by converting the major to revert to the third figure. Since the major of this mode is a particular negative that only converts if it is one of the two specific, that was conditioned so that this proof can be applied to it. This is the summary of what they mentioned in this place.
Source pages p. 353

Commentary
This passage is a classification and explanation of five modal conditions required for syllogisms in the fourth figure to yield a valid conclusion. The author organizes the conditions serially. The first condition requires both premises to be actual (فعلية), i.e., not possible, because if one premise is possible, the conclusion may not follow due to modal differences. He illustrates with examples: if the major is possible and the minor necessary, the truth may be negative; but if the major is changed, the truth flips to affirmative. Similarly for possible minor. The second condition requires that any negative premise used in the fourth figure must be from the six propositions whose negatives can convert (i.e., they allow 'aks al-naqid'), not from the nine that do not convert. The temporal (وقتية) proposition is the most specific of the non-converting negatives and is shown to be unproductive in this figure through examples with lunar phases and eclipses. The third condition applies to the third mode: the minor must be necessary or perpetual (i.e., have perpetual truth), or the major must be from the six converting negatives. If both conditions fail, the minor falls into the four general/specific categories and the major into the nine non-converting, and the most specific combination (particular conditional minor with temporal major) is shown unproductive via an example about illumination and moonlight. The fourth condition applies to the sixth and eighth modes: the minor must be one of the two specific (i.e., propositions that are particular and have converting negatives) and the major from the six converting negatives, because proving their productivity involves converting the minor to the second figure or reversing order to the first figure. The fifth condition applies to the seventh mode: the major must be one of the two specific, because its productivity is shown by converting the major to revert to the third figure. The passage ends with a summary. Throughout, the author uses technical modal logic terms and examples from astronomy (lunar eclipses, moonlight) to demonstrate. This is a dense commentary suitable for advanced students.

Checkpoint
According to the first condition for the fourth figure, what happens if one premise is possible?
A) The conclusion is always valid.
B) The conclusion is never valid.
C) The conclusion may not follow because the truth can vary between negative and affirmative depending on the terms.
D) The conclusion is always a particular affirmative.

Key Takeaway
The first condition: both premises must be actual to ensure a determinate conclusion in fourth-figure modal syllogisms.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a modal syllogism in the fourth figure on a theological topic (e.g., divine attributes) where both premises are actual (necessary). Verify that it yields a necessary conclusion. Then modify one premise to possible and show, using the author's reasoning, that no single conclusion follows.

Second Condition: Negative Premise Must Convert Negatives
Second condition: negative from six converting negatives

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which negative propositions can be used in the fourth figure by checking if they belong to the six whose negatives convert, and explain why temporal propositions fail.

Core Idea
Only negatives that allow conversion of their negative form are productive in the fourth figure.

The second condition restricts the type of negative premise allowed in fourth-figure syllogisms: it must be from the six propositions whose negatives convert (القضايا الست المنعكسة السوالب). The most specific non-converting is the temporal (وقتية), and the author shows through lunar examples why it fails.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition
The author states that any negative premise used must be from the six converting negatives, not from the nine non-converting.
Converting negatives allow the inference 'No S is P' → 'Some P is not S'.
2
Understand why temporal is problematic
The temporal proposition (e.g., 'Nothing of the moon is eclipsed at time T') is the most specific non-converting negative. The author shows its unproductivity in both minor and major positions.
3
Examine the examples
If the temporal is minor: 'Nothing of the moon is eclipsed at quadrature, not always; everything crescent is moon' – the intended conclusion 'Some eclipse is not a crescent' is false because its contradictory is true. If the temporal is major: similar failure.
The example uses lunar phases and eclipses.
4
Generalize
If the most specific non-converting fails, then all non-converting negatives fail. Thus only the six converting negatives can be used.
This condition ensures the syllogism has sufficient logical power to produce a conclusion.

Know that they mentioned for productivity of the fourth figure according to modality five conditions: First: that both its premises be actual; because if one of them were possible, productivity would not follow—if it were negative, as will come in the next condition, and if affirmative, either as minor or major, and in both cases difference follows. Second: that the negative used in it be from the six propositions whose negatives convert, not from the nine whose negatives do not convert, because the most specific of them, as previously, is the temporal, and it does not produce in this figure whether minor or major. If it were minor, because our statement 'Nothing of the moon is eclipsed at the time of quadrature, not always, and everything that is a crescent moon is necessarily the moon' is true, and 'Some of that which is eclipsed is not possibly a crescent moon' is not true because its contradictory, our statement 'Every eclipsed thing is necessarily a crescent moon' is true. And if the more specific does not produce, the more general does not produce, as detailed from the commentator. The most specific of those minors, the particular conditional, does not produce with the most specific of majors, the temporal, because it is true: 'Nothing of the eclipsed is illuminated by moonlight as long as it is eclipsed, not always, and every moon is eclipsed at the time of interposition, not always' while 'Some illuminated by moonlight is possibly not a moon' is false. Fourth: that the minor in the sixth and eighth modes be one of the two specific, and the major from the six whose negatives convert. As for the first, because its productivity, as will come, is only shown by converting the minor to revert to the second figure; so the minor must be one of the two specific because it is in this mode a particular negative, and it only converts if it is one of the two specific, as before. And the major must be from the six mentioned according to what preceded in the explanation of the conditions of the second figure: if perpetuity does not apply to the minor, then necessarily the major is from the six mentioned. As for the second, because its productivity, as will come, is only shown by reversing the order to revert to the first figure, then converting the conclusion. So its premises must be such that if one were exchanged for the other, they would produce from the first figure a particular negative convertible conclusion, and that only produces this result if the major is one of the two specific and the minor is one of the six mentioned, as is not hidden for one who examines the discussions of mixtures mentioned in the books of the scholars. So the minor in this mode must be one of the two specific because it is the major in the first figure after conversion, and the major from the six mentioned. Fifth: that the major in the seventh mode be one of the two specific, because, as will be mentioned, its productivity is only shown by converting the major to revert to the third figure. Since the major of this mode is a particular negative that only converts if it is one of the two specific, that was conditioned so that this proof can be applied to it. This is the summary of what they mentioned in this place.
Source pages p. 353

Commentary
This passage is a classification and explanation of five modal conditions required for syllogisms in the fourth figure to yield a valid conclusion. The author organizes the conditions serially. The first condition requires both premises to be actual (فعلية), i.e., not possible, because if one premise is possible, the conclusion may not follow due to modal differences. He illustrates with examples: if the major is possible and the minor necessary, the truth may be negative; but if the major is changed, the truth flips to affirmative. Similarly for possible minor. The second condition requires that any negative premise used in the fourth figure must be from the six propositions whose negatives can convert (i.e., they allow 'aks al-naqid'), not from the nine that do not convert. The temporal (وقتية) proposition is the most specific of the non-converting negatives and is shown to be unproductive in this figure through examples with lunar phases and eclipses. The third condition applies to the third mode: the minor must be necessary or perpetual (i.e., have perpetual truth), or the major must be from the six converting negatives. If both conditions fail, the minor falls into the four general/specific categories and the major into the nine non-converting, and the most specific combination (particular conditional minor with temporal major) is shown unproductive via an example about illumination and moonlight. The fourth condition applies to the sixth and eighth modes: the minor must be one of the two specific (i.e., propositions that are particular and have converting negatives) and the major from the six converting negatives, because proving their productivity involves converting the minor to the second figure or reversing order to the first figure. The fifth condition applies to the seventh mode: the major must be one of the two specific, because its productivity is shown by converting the major to revert to the third figure. The passage ends with a summary. Throughout, the author uses technical modal logic terms and examples from astronomy (lunar eclipses, moonlight) to demonstrate. This is a dense commentary suitable for advanced students.

Key Terms

الوقتية
السوالب

Checkpoint
Why does the temporal proposition (الوقتية) fail in the fourth figure?
A) It is too vague.
B) Its negative does not convert, so the needed conversion step is unavailable.
C) It is only used in the first figure.
D) It is inherently contradictory.

Key Takeaway
The second condition: only negatives from the six converting propositions are allowed in fourth-figure syllogisms.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take a negative proposition from fiqh, e.g., 'No prayer is valid without purification.' Determine whether it is from the six converting negatives or not. If not, explain why it cannot be used in a fourth-figure modal syllogism following the second condition.

The Syllogism
advanced
10 min
Third Condition: Minor Must Be Necessary/Perpetual or Major from Six
Third condition: minor necessity/perpetuity or major from six

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the third modal condition for the fourth figure, specifically for the third mode, by determining when the minor must be necessary/perpetual and when the major must be from the six converting negatives.

Core Idea
In the third mode, the minor must have perpetual truth or the major must convert; otherwise the syllogism fails.

The third condition applies to the third mode (ضرب) of the fourth figure. It states that the minor must be such that 'perpetuity' applies to it (i.e., it is necessary or perpetual), or the major must be from the six propositions whose negatives convert. If both conditions are absent, the combination of the most specific non-converting minor and major proves unproductive.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition for the third mode
The author requires either the minor to be necessary or perpetual, or the major to be from the six converting negatives.
The minor in this mode is negative, so it must be from the six if it is not necessary/perpetual.
2
Understand the alternative failure
If both conditions fail, the minor becomes one of the four general/specific categories (which are non-converting) and the major from the nine non-converting. The author shows the most specific combination (particular conditional minor with temporal major) fails.
3
Examine the counterexample
Example: minor 'Nothing of the eclipsed is illuminated by moonlight as long as it is eclipsed, not always' (particular conditional) and major 'Every moon is eclipsed at interposition, not always' (temporal). The intended conclusion 'Some illuminated is possibly not moon' is false.
The example uses astronomical phenomena.
4
Generalize
Since the most specific non-converting combination fails, any weaker combination would also fail, so the condition stands.
Thus in the third mode, at least one of the two conditions must hold.
