Know that they mentioned for productivity of the fourth figure according to modality five conditions: First: that both its premises be actual; because if one of them were possible, productivity would not follow—if it were negative, as will come in the next condition, and if affirmative, either as minor or major, and in both cases difference follows. If it were major, the truth in our statement: 'Everything ridden by Zayd is necessarily a horse, and every donkey is possibly ridden by Zayd' in case of assuming that Zayd's riding is restricted to the horse, is the negative; and if we replaced the major with our statement 'Every neighing thing is possibly ridden by Zayd', the truth would be the affirmative. And if it were minor, the truth in the mentioned assumption when we say 'Every braying thing is possibly ridden by Zayd, and every donkey is necessarily a braying thing' is the negative; and if we said 'Every animal is possibly ridden by Zayd, and every neighing thing is necessarily an animal', the truth would be the affirmative. Second: that the negative used in it be from the six propositions whose negatives convert, not from the nine whose negatives do not convert, because the most specific of them, as previously, is the temporal, and it does not produce in this figure whether minor or major. If it were minor, because our statement 'Nothing of the moon is eclipsed at the time of quadrature, not always, and everything that is a crescent moon is necessarily the moon' is true, and 'Some of that which is eclipsed is not possibly a crescent moon' is not true because its contradictory, our statement 'Every eclipsed thing is necessarily a crescent moon' is true. And if it were major, because it is true: our statement 'Every eclipsed thing is necessarily a crescent moon, and nothing of the moon is eclipsed at the time of quadrature, not always', that 'Some crescent moon is not possibly eclipsed' is not true, as before. And if the more specific does not produce, the more general does not produce, as detailed from the commentator. Third: that the minor in the third mode be such that perpetuity applies to it, being necessary or perpetual, or the major such that common temporal applies to it, being from the six propositions whose negatives convert; because if both conditions were absent, the minor would be one of the four general and specific, since the minor in this mode is negative, and it has been conditioned that the negative used in this figure be from the six whose negatives convert. So if the minor is not necessary or perpetual, it must be one of the four mentioned, and then the major would be one of the nine whose negatives do not convert, and that is apparent. The most specific of those minors, the particular conditional, does not produce with the most specific of majors, the temporal, because it is true: 'Nothing of the eclipsed is illuminated by moonlight as long as it is eclipsed, not always, and every moon is eclipsed at the time of interposition, not always' while 'Some illuminated by moonlight is possibly not a moon' is false. Fourth: that the minor in the sixth and eighth modes be one of the two specific, and the major from the six whose negatives convert. As for the first, because its productivity, as will come, is only shown by converting the minor to revert to the second figure; so the minor must be one of the two specific because it is in this mode a particular negative, and it only converts if it is one of the two specific, as before. And the major must be from the six mentioned according to what preceded in the explanation of the conditions of the second figure: if perpetuity does not apply to the minor, then necessarily the major is from the six mentioned. As for the second, because its productivity, as will come, is only shown by reversing the order to revert to the first figure, then converting the conclusion. So its premises must be such that if one were exchanged for the other, they would produce from the first figure a particular negative convertible conclusion, and that only produces this result if the major is one of the two specific and the minor is one of the six mentioned, as is not hidden for one who examines the discussions of mixtures mentioned in the books of the scholars. Since the major of this mode is a particular negative that only converts if it is one of the two specific, that was conditioned so that this proof can be applied to it. Checkpoint
In the third condition for the fourth figure's third mode, what happens if both the minor is not necessary/perpetual and the major is not from the six converting negatives?
A) The syllogism becomes invalid because the minor is negative and cannot convert.
B) The syllogism becomes invalid because the major is affirmative.
C) The syllogism becomes invalid because the most specific combination (particular conditional minor and temporal major) fails, and by extension all such combinations fail.

Fourth Condition: Minor Specific and Major from Six in Modes Six and Eight
Fourth condition: minor specific, major from six for modes six and eight

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to verify the fourth condition for the sixth and eighth modes of the fourth figure: the minor must be one of the two specific propositions and the major from the six converting negatives.

Core Idea
In modes six and eight, the minor must be specific and the major from the six because proof requires conversion or reversal.

The fourth condition applies to the sixth and eighth modes of the fourth figure. It requires that the minor be one of the two specific propositions (الخاصتين) and the major from the six converting negatives. The author explains that this is necessary because the productivity of these modes is shown through converting the minor (to revert to the second figure) or reversing the order (to the first figure) and then converting the conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition for mode six
For mode six, the minor must be one of the two specific (particular propositions with converting negatives) and the major from the six converting negatives.
This is because the proof of productivity involves converting the minor to revert to the second figure, which requires the minor to be convertible.
2
Identify the condition for mode eight
For mode eight, similarly the minor must be one of the two specific and the major from the six, because the proof involves reversing the order (to the first figure) and then converting the conclusion.
After reversal, the minor becomes the major in the first figure, so it must be convertible.
3
Understand why the major must be from the six
The author references earlier conditions for the second figure: if the minor does not have perpetuity, then the major must be from the six converting negatives.
4
Synthesize
Thus in both modes, the minor must be specific (convertible) and the major must be from the six converting negatives to allow the necessary logical transformations.
This ensures the syllogism can be reduced to a valid figure.

ليرتد الى الشكل الثانى فلابد ان تكون الصغرى احدى الخاصتين، لانها في هذا الضرب سالبة جزئية و هي لا تنعكس الا اذا كانت احدى الخاصتين كمامر و ان تكون الكبري احدى القضاياء المست المذكورة لما سبق في بيان النتيجة، فلابد ان تكون مقدمتاه بحيث لو بدلت احديهما بالأخرى انتجتا من الشكل الاول سالبة جزئية قابلة الانعكاس و هو لا ينتج هذه النتيجة الا اذا كانت كبراء احدى الخاصتين و صغراه احدى القضاياء الست المذكورة كما لا يخفى على من لاحظ مباحث الاختلاطات المذكورة في كتب القوم فيجب ان يكون الصغرى فى هذا الضرب احدى الخاصتين، لانها الكبرى في الشكل الاول بعد العكس و الكبرى من القضاياء الست المذكورة. الخامس أن تكون الكبرى في الضرب السابع احدى الخاصتين وذلك لما سيذكر من ان انتاجه انما يتبين بعكس الكبرى ليرتد إلى الشكل الثالث و لما كانت كبرى هذا الضرب سالبة جزئية لا تنعكس الا اذا كانت احدى الخاصتين اشترط فيه ذلك ليتمكن من اجراء هذا الدليل فيه هذا خلاصة ما ذكروه في هذا المقام.
Know that they mentioned for productivity of the fourth figure according to modality five conditions: First: that both its premises be actual; because if one of them were possible, productivity would not follow—if it were negative, as will come in the next condition, and if affirmative, either as minor or major, and in both cases difference follows. If it were major, the truth in our statement: 'Everything ridden by Zayd is necessarily a horse, and every donkey is possibly ridden by Zayd' in case of assuming that Zayd's riding is restricted to the horse, is the negative; and if we replaced the major with our statement 'Every neighing thing is possibly ridden by Zayd', the truth would be the affirmative. And if it were minor, the truth in the mentioned assumption when we say 'Every braying thing is possibly ridden by Zayd, and every donkey is necessarily a braying thing' is the negative; and if we said 'Every animal is possibly ridden by Zayd, and every neighing thing is necessarily an animal', the truth would be the affirmative. Second: that the negative used in it be from the six propositions whose negatives convert, not from the nine whose negatives do not convert, because the most specific of them, as previously, is the temporal, and it does not produce in this figure whether minor or major. If it were minor, because our statement 'Nothing of the moon is eclipsed at the time of quadrature, not always, and everything that is a crescent moon is necessarily the moon' is true, and 'Some of that which is eclipsed is not possibly a crescent moon' is not true because its contradictory, our statement 'Every eclipsed thing is necessarily a crescent moon' is true. And if it were major, because it is true: our statement 'Every eclipsed thing is necessarily a crescent moon, and nothing of the moon is eclipsed at the time of quadrature, not always', that 'Some crescent moon is not possibly eclipsed' is not true, as before. And if the more specific does not produce, the more general does not produce, as detailed from the commentator. Third: that the minor in the third mode be such that perpetuity applies to it, being necessary or perpetual, or the major such that common temporal applies to it, being from the six propositions whose negatives convert; because if both conditions were absent, the minor would be one of the four general and specific, since the minor in this mode is negative, and it has been conditioned that the negative used in this figure be from the six whose negatives convert. So if the minor is not necessary or perpetual, it must be one of the four mentioned, and then the major would be one of the nine whose negatives do not convert, and that is apparent. The most specific of those minors, the particular conditional, does not produce with the most specific of majors, the temporal, because it is true: 'Nothing of the eclipsed is illuminated by moonlight as long as it is eclipsed, not always, and every moon is eclipsed at the time of interposition, not always' while 'Some illuminated by moonlight is possibly not a moon' is false. Fourth: that the minor in the sixth and eighth modes be one of the two specific, and the major from the six whose negatives convert. As for the first, because its productivity, as will come, is only shown by converting the minor to revert to the second figure; so the minor must be one of the two specific because it is in this mode a particular negative, and it only converts if it is one of the two specific, as before. And the major must be from the six mentioned according to what preceded in the explanation of the conditions of the second figure: if perpetuity does not apply to the minor, then necessarily the major is from the six mentioned. As for the second, because its productivity, as will come, is only shown by reversing the order to revert to the first figure, then converting the conclusion. So its premises must be such that if one were exchanged for the other, they would produce from the first figure a particular negative convertible conclusion, and that only produces this result if the major is one of the two specific and the minor is one of the six mentioned, as is not hidden for one who examines the discussions of mixtures mentioned in the books of the scholars. So the minor in this mode must be one of the two specific because it is the major in the first figure after conversion, and the major from the six mentioned. Fifth: that the major in the seventh mode be one of the two specific, because, as will be mentioned, its productivity is only shown by converting the major to revert to the third figure. Since the major of this mode is a particular negative that only converts if it is one of the two specific, that was conditioned so that this proof can be applied to it. This is the summary of what they mentioned in this place.
Source pages p. 353

Commentary
This passage is a classification and explanation of five modal conditions required for syllogisms in the fourth figure to yield a valid conclusion. The author organizes the conditions serially. The first condition requires both premises to be actual (فعلية), i.e., not possible, because if one premise is possible, the conclusion may not follow due to modal differences. He illustrates with examples: if the major is possible and the minor necessary, the truth may be negative; but if the major is changed, the truth flips to affirmative. Similarly for possible minor. The second condition requires that any negative premise used in the fourth figure must be from the six propositions whose negatives can convert (i.e., they allow 'aks al-naqid'), not from the nine that do not convert. The temporal (وقتية) proposition is the most specific of the non-converting negatives and is shown to be unproductive in this figure through examples with lunar phases and eclipses. The third condition applies to the third mode: the minor must be necessary or perpetual (i.e., have perpetual truth), or the major must be from the six converting negatives. If both conditions fail, the minor falls into the four general/specific categories and the major into the nine non-converting, and the most specific combination (particular conditional minor with temporal major) is shown unproductive via an example about illumination and moonlight. The fourth condition applies to the sixth and eighth modes: the minor must be one of the two specific (i.e., propositions that are particular and have converting negatives) and the major from the six converting negatives, because proving their productivity involves converting the minor to the second figure or reversing order to the first figure. The fifth condition applies to the seventh mode: the major must be one of the two specific, because its productivity is shown by converting the major to revert to the third figure. The passage ends with a summary. Throughout, the author uses technical modal logic terms and examples from astronomy (lunar eclipses, moonlight) to demonstrate. This is a dense commentary suitable for advanced students.

Key Terms

الضرب السادس
الضرب الثامن
عكس الصغرى

Checkpoint
Why must the minor be one of the two specific propositions in the sixth mode of the fourth figure?
A) Because the minor is affirmative.
B) Because the proof of productivity involves converting the minor, which is a particular negative that only converts if it is one of the two specific.
C) Because the major is universal.
D) Because the conclusion must be universal.

Key Takeaway
The fourth condition: in modes six and eight, the minor must be specific and the major from the six converting negatives.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take a sixth-mode fourth-figure syllogism from usul al-fiqh: minor 'Some permissible actions are not obligated' (specific negative), major 'Every obligated action is required' (from six converting). Check if the minor is one of the two specific and major from the six. If not, adjust to meet the condition.

Fifth Condition: Major Must Be Specific in Seventh Mode
Fifth condition: major specific in seventh mode

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the fifth modal condition for the fourth figure: in the seventh mode, the major must be one of the two specific propositions.

Core Idea
In the seventh mode, the major must be specific because its conversion is needed to revert to the third figure.

The fifth and final condition addresses the seventh mode of the fourth figure. It requires that the major be one of the two specific propositions (الخاصتين). The author explains that the productivity of this mode is shown by converting the major to revert to the third figure, and since the major is a particular negative, it only converts if it is specific.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition
The author states that the major in the seventh mode must be one of the two specific propositions.
The two specific propositions are particular negatives that can convert.
2
Understand the proof method
The productivity of the seventh mode is demonstrated by converting the major to revert to the third figure. Since the major is a particular negative, it only converts if it is specific.
3
Connect to conversion rules
Recall that particular negatives only convert if they are specific (i.e., have the property that their negative converts).
If the major were not specific, the conversion step would be invalid.
4
Conclusion
Therefore, the major must be one of the two specific propositions to allow the proof to proceed.
This completes the five conditions for fourth-figure modal syllogisms.

Commentary
This passage is a classification and explanation of five modal conditions required for syllogisms in the fourth figure to yield a valid conclusion. The author organizes the conditions serially. The Syllogism

Critique of the Second and Third Conditions for the Fourth Figure
Author argues that the proof for the second and third conditions is incomplete because impossibility of universal necessity cannot be shown.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the critique against the proof for the second and third conditions of the fourth figure and evaluate its logical basis using temporal premises.

Core Idea
Temporal premises make universal necessary negation impossible, undermining the conditions' proof.

The author challenges the proof for the second and third conditions of the fourth figure, which are meant to ensure that the conclusion follows from the premises. The critique centers on the claim that the proof is incomplete because it cannot establish the impossibility of a universal necessary negation when one premise is temporal. Understanding this critique is crucial for correctly applying syllogistic rules in contexts involving temporal propositions, which are common in Islamic legal and theological reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditions being critiqued
Recall that the second and third conditions for the fourth figure typically require that the major premise be universal and that the minor premise be affirmative. The proof for these conditions attempts to show that if they are violated, the syllogism fails. Locate the specific conditions in your understanding of the fourth figure.
The conditions ensure the middle term is properly distributed.
2
Understand the original proof's structure
The original proof relies on demonstrating that if the conditions are not met, then there is no 'difference' (ikhtilaf) in truth value between the premises. Specifically, it tries to show that affirmation (positive judgment) is impossible in one subject-matter (madda) and negation in another. This would violate the conditions.
The proof assumes that the impossibility of affirmation in some material is necessary.
3
Locate the author's objection
The author argues that no such material (madda) can be found where affirmation (the possible particular affirmative) is impossible. This is because proving that would require showing that the universal necessary negation ('No S is P necessarily') is impossible. But in the case of temporal premises, such a proof fails.
The impossibility of the universal necessary negative would make the possible affirmative always true, but the author claims this impossibility cannot be shown.
4
Analyze the temporal premise argument
If one premise is temporal (i.e., true only at a specific time), then the subject and predicate terms can meet (co-extend) at some times and separate at others. Consequently, it is not possible to affirm a universal necessary negative, because there are times when the terms do meet. Therefore, the required contradiction does not arise.
The temporal nature prevents the universality needed for the negation to be necessary.
5
Evaluate the critique
Assess whether the critique successfully shows the proof is incomplete. The critique is logically sound: temporal premises allow for meeting at some times, so the universal necessary negative cannot hold. However, it limits the conditions to cases without temporal premises. This is a refinement rather than a refutation.
The critique highlights the importance of considering the modality (temporal vs. eternal) in syllogistic conditions.

ولا يذهب عليك : ان البيان فى الشرط الثانى والثالث غير تام لان الاختلاف انما يثبت اذا امتنع الايجاب فى مادة و السلب فى اخرى و الحال انه لم يظفر بمادة يمتنع فيها الايجاب، لأن هذا انما يتبين لوكان سلب الاكبر عن الاصغر بالضرورة على سبيل الكلية ممتنعاً، لئلا يصدق الممكنة العامة الموجبة الجزئية، لكنه غير ممكن فيه لانه اذا كانت احدى المقدمتين وقتية يجتمع الأصغر أو الاكبر مع الأوسط في وقت و يفترق عنه في وقت آخر كما يظهر بالتأمل فح لا يمكن سلب الأكبر عن الاصغر بالضرورة ليمتنع الايجاب بالامكان لظهور انه اذا اجتمع الاصغر مع الاوسط، اجتمع مع الاكبر ايضاً لاجتماع الأوسط معه ضرورة و كذا اذا اجتمع الاكبر مع الاوسط اجتمع مع الاصغر أيضاً لما ذكر، فاذا دل الدليل على امتناع سلب الاكبر عن الاصغر، فيكون الموجبة الممكنة العامة نتيجة لازمة لتلك الاختلاطات.
And let it not escape you: the explanation in the second and third conditions is incomplete, because difference is only established if affirmation is impossible in one material and negation in another; yet no material is found in which affirmation is impossible, because this is only shown if the universal necessary negation of the major about the minor were impossible, so that the general possible particular affirmative is not true. But it is not possible in it because if one of the premises is temporal, the minor or the major meets the middle term at one time and separates from it at another, as appears by reflection. So it is not possible to necessarily negate the major from the minor to preclude affirmative possibility, because it is evident that if the minor meets the middle, it meets the major as well due to the middle necessarily meeting it, and similarly if the major meets the middle, it meets the minor also because of what was mentioned. So if the proof indicates the impossibility of negating the major about the minor, then the general possible affirmative is a necessary result for those mixtures.
Source pages p. 354

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement (hāshiya) objecting to the second and third conditions for the fourth figure. The author, likely the commentator (Mīrzā Muḥammad ‘Alī), argues that the proof given for those conditions is incomplete. The conditions rely on the idea that difference in truth value occurs when affirmation is impossible in one subject-matter and negation in another. However, the critic claims that no such subject-matter can be found where affirmation (i.e., the possible affirmative) is impossible. That would require showing that the universal necessary negative ('No S is P necessarily') is impossible—meaning that the possible affirmative 'Some S is possibly P' is always true. But in the case where one premise is temporal (time-limited), the subject and predicate terms can meet (co-extend) at some times and separate at others. Because of the nature of temporal premises, if the minor term meets the middle term, then it also meets the major term (since the middle necessarily meets the major by the major premise), and vice versa. Therefore, at no time can a universal necessary negative hold, since there are times when the terms do meet. Consequently, the possible affirmative ( 'Some S is possibly P') is always true, and the contradiction needed for the conditions to fail does not arise. Thus the second and third conditions are not properly justified. This is a subtle logical critique that demonstrates the interplay between modal and temporal logic.

Key Terms
المادة (al-madda): subject-matter or content of a proposition
الوقتية (al-waqtiyya): temporal proposition

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

C) Because the conditions contradict each other.
D) Because the fourth figure does not produce any conclusion.

Key Takeaway
The critique reveals that the second and third conditions' proof depends on assuming non-temporal premises; temporal premises undermine the required impossibility of universal necessity, showing the need for careful consideration of modality in syllogistic conditions.

Core Idea

Why the Fourth Figure's Affirmative Conclusion is Particular
Author contrasts the third and fourth figures to explain that the fourth figure's affirmative conclusion is particular due to the minor premise being affirmative.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to compare the fourth and third figures and explain why the fourth figure yields a particular affirmative conclusion while the third yields a universal negative conclusion.

Core Idea
Universal premises do not guarantee a universal conclusion; figure and premise quality determine the quantity.

In this passage, the author contrasts two moods from the third and fourth figures to explain a subtle but important point: even when both premises are universal, the conclusion may be particular. The key lies in the quality of the minor premise and the relationship between the terms. Let's unpack the reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two moods being compared
Both have universal premises. The fourth mood has a universal affirmative minor premise; the third mood has a universal negative minor premise.
2
Analyze the fourth mood's minor premise
The minor premise is 'All B is C' (universal affirmative). The minor term (C) is predicated of the middle (B). Since the predicate C may be more general than the subject of the conclusion (A), the ruling transfers only partially, yielding 'Some A is C'.
3
Analyze the third mood's minor premise
The minor premise is universal negative (e.g., 'No B is C'). The negation between minor and middle is complete. Because the middle applies universally to the major (from the major premise), the negation transfers completely, giving a universal negative conclusion.
The principle: negation between a thing and another entails negation between that thing and the necessary concomitant of the other.
4
Contrast the two transfers
In the affirmative case, the transfer is only partial because the predicate may be more general. In the negative case, the negation is complete and transfers universally.
This explains why the fourth figure's affirmative conclusion is particular while the third figure's negative conclusion is universal.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Why Only Eight Syllogistic Moods Are Productive
Author states that sixteen possible moods per figure reduce to eight productive ones.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two rules that eliminate invalid premise combinations and list the eight productive moods per figure.

Core Idea
Only eight of sixteen premise combinations per figure yield a valid syllogism.

In this passage, al-Yazdi summarizes a key classification: each syllogistic figure allows sixteen possible combinations of premises (considering quantity and quality), but only eight produce a valid conclusion. The elimination is based on two rules you will learn. Understanding this reduction is essential for constructing sound arguments in Islamic theology and jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the two invalidating conditions
The first condition is that both premises cannot be negative. The second is that the minor premise cannot be particular affirmative when the major is affirmative or particular negative, and the symmetric case. These are given as the reasons for eliminating eight moods.
2
Count the sixteen moods
For a figure, consider all combinations of A, E, I, O for the major and minor premises, giving 4×4=16. The two conditions will eliminate eight of them.
3
Verify the eight remaining are productive
The eight moods that survive are the ones detailed in the text. They correspond to valid argument forms like 'Every A is B, Every C is A, therefore Every C is B' (first figure, Barbara).

Why the Fourth Mood Yields a Particular Conclusion
Commentator explains why the fourth mood yields a particular conclusion despite both universal premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to differentiate why the third and fourth moods of the first figure produce different types of conclusions (universal vs. particular) despite both having universal premises.

Core Idea
In the fourth mood, the minor term is predicated of the middle, allowing the predicate to be more general, blocking a universal conclusion.

This passage from the commentator explains a subtle point in categorical syllogisms: why the fourth mood of the first figure yields only a particular conclusion while the third mood yields a universal one, even though both moods have universal premises. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly evaluating syllogistic arguments in Islamic logic, especially when building proofs in kalam or fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two moods being compared
The third mood has a universal negative minor and universal affirmative major; the fourth mood has a universal affirmative minor and universal negative major. Note the order of premises as given by the commentator.
2
Examine the minor premise of the fourth mood
The minor premise is 'Every S is M' (universal affirmative). Here the minor term is predicated of the middle, meaning the middle term is the predicate. Since the predicate can be more general than the subject, the judgment about the middle does not necessarily apply to all of the minor term; it only applies to some of it.
3
Compare with the third mood's minor premise
The third mood's minor is 'No S is M' (universal negative). Here the minor term is the subject and the middle is the predicate. The premise states complete incompatibility between S and M, so if M is universally true of the major term, then S is completely incompatible with the major term, yielding a universal conclusion.
4
Understand the logical principle behind the difference
Incompatibility between a thing and another implies incompatibility between that thing and anything necessary to the other. This is why the third mood's universal negative minor combined with a universal affirmative major gives a universal negative conclusion, while the fourth mood's structure only gives a particular negative conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و انما لم تكن النتيجة فى الضرب الرابع كلية كما كانت فى الضرب الثالث كذلك، مع ان كتا مقدمتيه ايضاً كلية لان الصغرى فيه موجبة كلية عمل فيه الاصغر على الأوسط و المحمول يجوز أن يكون اعم فلا يلزم تعدى الحكم من الاوسط الاخص الى الاصغر الاعم الا على سبيل الجزئية و هذا بخلاف الضرب الثالث فان الصغرى فيه سالبة كلية حكم فيها يتنافى الاصغر والاوسط بالكلية فاذا حكم بالتنافي بينها، حكم بالتنافي بين الاصغر والأكبر لصدق الاوسط عليه بالكلية كما هو حكم الكبرى و التنافي بين الشيء و بين آخر يستلزم التنافي بينه و بين لازمه كما لا يخفى
And the conclusion in the fourth mood is not universal as it was in the third mood similarly, even though both its premises are also universal, because the minor premise in it is a universal affirmative in which the minor term is predicated of the middle, and the predicate may be more general, so the extension of the judgment from the more specific middle to the more general minor does not follow except as particular. This is unlike the third mood, for the minor premise in it is a universal negative in which it is judged that the minor and middle are completely incompatible. So when incompatibility is judged between them, incompatibility is judged between the minor and the major because the middle is true of the major universally as is the judgment of the major premise. And incompatibility between a thing and another entails incompatibility between it and what is necessary to that other, as is obvious.
Source pages p. 354

Commentary
This passage explains why the fourth mood (presumably in the first figure) does not yield a universal conclusion, whereas the third mood does, even though both have universal premises. In the fourth mood, the minor premise is universal affirmative (e.g., 'Every A is B') and the major premise is universal negative (e.g., 'No C is B'). The minor term is predicated of the middle. Since the predicate (middle) can be more general than the subject (minor), the judgment about the middle does not necessarily extend to all of the minor; it only extends as a particular. In contrast, in the third mood, the minor premise is universal negative (e.g., 'No A is B') and the major is universal affirmative (e.g., 'Every C is B'). Here the minor term is the subject, and the premise states complete incompatibility between minor and middle. Since the middle is true of all of the major (as per the major premise), the incompatibility extends to the major as well: if no A is B, and every C is B, then no A is C. This yields a universal negative conclusion. The commentator argues that incompatibility between a thing and another implies incompatibility between the thing and anything necessary to that other. Thus the fourth mood yields a particular conclusion (e.g., some A is not C) rather than universal.

Key Terms
الضرب الرابع (al-darb al-raabi’): The fourth mood in the first figure, with premises: universal affirmative minor, universal negative major. [Note: Part of standard classification; no shift.]
الضرب الثالث (al-darb al-thaalith): The third mood in the first figure, with premises: universal negative minor, universal affirmative major. [Note: No shift.]
تنافي (tanaafī): Incompatibility; mutual exclusion between terms. [Note: Stable in logical contexts.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the fourth mood not produce a universal conclusion according to the commentator?
A) Because the minor premise is negative
B) Because the major premise is particular
C) Because the minor term is predicated of the middle and the predicate may be more general
D) Because the middle term is not distributed

Key Takeaway
The fourth mood yields a particular conclusion because the minor premise is affirmative and the predicate (middle) may be more general than the subject, preventing universal transfer.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Given the syllogism: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden. No nabidh is forbidden.' Determine whether the conclusion should be universal or particular, and explain why, referencing the fourth mood analysis from this passage.
?
Construct a valid fourth-mood syllogism using a fiqh example (e.g., with terms: 'sale of gharar', 'valid contract', 'void contract') and state the conclusion as particular.

Ordering of the Eight Productive Moods in the First Figure
Commentator explains the rationale for the order of moods in the first figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the rationale behind the order of the eight productive moods in the first figure and identify the factors that determine their sequence.

Core Idea
The order of moods is determined by the nobility of their premises, not their conclusions.

In this passage, the commentator explains why the eight productive moods in the first figure are arranged in a specific sequence. While one might assume the order follows the nobility of the conclusions, the commentator clarifies that the order is based on the premises themselves. Understanding this ordering is essential for analyzing how syllogistic moods relate to each other and how they reduce to the first figure.

Guided Path
1
Identify the principle of ordering
The commentator states that moods are ordered based on their premises, not their conclusions. Find the Arabic phrase that expresses this (e.g., 'ليس باعتبار نتايجها، بل باعتبار أنفسها').
2
Examine the placement of the first mood
Why is the first mood placed first? Because it consists of two universal affirmatives, the noblest propositions. Also, its premises correspond to the first mood of the first figure (Barbara).
3
Trace the reasoning for the second mood
The second mood follows immediately because it shares affirmative premises with the first. Its premises also correspond to the second mood of the first figure in a general way.
They are not separated by a third or fourth mood despite universal vs particular considerations.
4
Understand reduction (irtidaad) as a factor
Some moods reduce to the first figure by reversal of order (e.g., third mood) or by conversion of premises (e.g., fourth and fifth). This affects their placement.
Reduction to other figures also influences order, e.g., sixth mood reduces to second figure.
5
Compare the nobility of premises
Universal affirmative is considered nobler than particular affirmative, so the fourth mood (universal minor) precedes the fifth (particular minor). Similarly, sixth and seventh include universal affirmatives, so they precede the eighth.
Nobility of propositions is a key criterion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( الاول من موجبتين كليتين قد عرفت فيا سبق فى القاعدة التي قررنا ها ان ترتيب الضروب فى هذا الشكل ليس باعتبار نتايجها، بل باعتبار أنفسها فلذا جعل الذي ينتج الايجاب الجزئى ضرباً ثانياً والذى ينتج السلب الكلى ضرباً ثالثاً مع ان الكلى و ان كان سلباً، اشرف من الجزئي و ان كان ايجاباً كمامر. فنقول قدم الاول لانه من موجبتين كليتين والايجاب الكلى اشرف المحصورات و ايضاً لاشتماله على مقدمتى الضرب الأول من الشكل الاول، فكما جعل هناك اولاً فكذلك هيهنا ثم الثاني لمشاركته الأول في ايجاب المقدمتين، فالاولى ان لا يفصل بينهما بواحد من الثالث والرابع و ان كان تركيبها من كليتين و الكلى مطلقا اشرف من الجزئى مطلقا و ايضاً لاشتماله على مقدمتى الضرب الثاني من الشكل الاول في الجملة ثم الثالث، لارتداده الى الشكل الأول بعكس الترتيب ثم الرابع و الخامس على البواق لانهما يرتدان إلى الشكل الاول بعكس المقدمتين دونها وقدم الرابع على الخامس لكونه اخس من الخامس لان صغراه موجبة كلية و صغرى الخامس موجبة جزئية والايجاب الكلى اشرف من الايجاب الجزئى كما مر ثم السادس والسابع على الثامن لاشتمالهما على الايجاب الكلى دونه وقدم السادس على السابع لانه يرتد إلى الشكل الثانى بعكس الصغرى دونه
His saying: 'The first is from two universal affirmatives — you have known previously in the rule we established that the ordering of the moods in this figure is not based on their conclusions but based on themselves. Therefore he made the one that produces the particular affirmative the second mood, and the one that produces the universal negative the third, even though the universal, even if negative, is nobler than the particular even if affirmative, as has passed. So we say: He placed the first first because it consists of two universal affirmatives, and the universal affirmative is the noblest of the categorical propositions, and also because it includes the premises of the first mood of the first figure. So just as it was made first there, so here. Then the second, for its sharing with the first in the affirmation of both premises. So it is proper not to separate between them by a third or fourth, even though their composition is from two universals, and the universal absolutely is nobler than the particular absolutely. Also because it includes the premises of the second mood of the first figure in a general way. Then the third, because it reduces to the first figure by reversal of order. Then the fourth and fifth after the others, because they reduce to the first figure by reversal of the premises without them. And he placed the fourth before the fifth because it is baser than the fifth: its minor is universal affirmative and the minor of the fifth is particular affirmative, and universal affirmative is nobler than particular affirmative as has passed. Then the sixth and seventh before the eighth, because they include a universal affirmative whereas the eighth does not. And he placed the sixth before the seventh because it reduces to the second figure by conversion of the minor without the seventh.
Source pages p. 354

Commentary
This passage is a detailed explanation of the rationale behind the order of the eight productive moods in the first figure. The commentator establishes that the order is based on the intrinsic nobility of the premises, not on the conclusions. He notes that although a universal negative conclusion is nobler than a particular affirmative, the moods are not ordered by conclusion nobility. The first mood is placed first because it has two universal affirmative premises, which are the noblest propositions. Additionally, its premises correspond to the first mood of the first figure (Barbara). The second mood shares affirmative premises with the first, so it should follow immediately, not be separated by a third or fourth mood. The third mood is placed third because it reduces to the first figure by reversal of the order of premises. The fourth and fifth come later because they reduce to the first figure by converting the premises (i.e., swapping them). The fourth is placed before the fifth because its minor is universal affirmative, which is nobler than the particular affirmative of the fifth. The sixth and seventh precede the eighth because they include a universal affirmative premise, whereas the eighth does not. The sixth precedes the seventh because it reduces to the second figure by converting the minor, unlike the seventh. This reflects the hierarchical classification of moods common in Islamic logic, where the universal is considered nobler than the particular.

Key Terms
ترتيب الضروب
ارتداد
اشرف

The order of the eight productive moods in the first figure is determined by the nobility of their premises and their reduction relationships, not by their conclusions.

The Syllogism

Verifying Syllogisms with Reductio ad Absurdum
Commentator explains the reductio method for three moods

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to verify the validity of a categorical syllogism's conclusion by applying the reductio ad absurdum method.

Core Idea
Reductio proves a conclusion by assuming its opposite and deriving a contradiction.

The commentator demonstrates a powerful technique to check the validity of categorical syllogisms: reductio ad absurdum (khalaf). By assuming the contradictory of the conclusion and combining it with one of the premises, we derive a contradiction that shows the original conclusion must be true. This method works for various moods, as shown in the passage.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original syllogism and its conclusion
Find the premises and the conclusion you want to verify. For example, in the first two moods, the premises are 'Every B is C' and 'Every A is B' (or some A is B), and the conclusion is 'Some C is A'.
2
Assume the contradictory of the conclusion
Take the proposition that directly opposes the conclusion in quality and quantity. For the particular affirmative 'Some C is A', the contradictory is 'No C is A'.
The contradictory must be exactly opposite: universal negative for particular affirmative.
3
Combine the contradictory with the original minor premise
Use the assumed contradictory as a major premise and keep the original minor premise ('Every B is C') to form a new syllogism in the first figure. This yields: 'No B is A' from 'Every B is C' and 'No C is A'.
The new conclusion is derived via the first figure (Barbara or Celarent).
4
Convert the new conclusion to check contradiction
Convert the new conclusion ('No B is A') to 'No A is B' by simple conversion. This directly contradicts the original major premise ('Every A is B' or 'Some A is B'), showing that the assumption of the contradictory leads to inconsistency.
The contradiction confirms that the original conclusion must be true.
5
Apply the same logic to the third mood and beyond
For universal negative conclusions, assume the particular affirmative contradictory, combine it with the major premise, and derive a conversion that contradicts the minor premise. The method adapts to different moods by choosing which premise to keep and which to contradict.
The commentator mentions that for the last two moods, either method works.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اما فى الاولين فيجعل نقيض النتيجة لكليته كبرى وصغرى القياس لا يجابه صغرى كما في الخلف المستعمل فى الشكل الثالث فينتج من الشكل الاول نتيجة تنعكس الى ما ينافي كبرى القياس، مثلاً نقول كلما صدق قولنا كل ب ج و كل الف او بعضه ب ، لصدق بعض ج الف و الالصدق نقيضه وهو: لاشيء من ج، الف فنضمه مع صغرى القياس هكذا كل ب ، ج ولا شيء من ينتج: لاشيء من ب الف و هو ينعكس الى قولنا لاشيء من الف، ب وقد كان حكم الكبرى ان كل الف او بعضه ب، هف. و اما فى الثالث فيجعل نقيض النتيجة لايجابه صغری و کبرى القياس تكليتها كبرى كما في الخلف المذكور فى الشكل الأول لينتج من الشكل الاول ما ينعكس الى ما ينا في الصغري، مثلا نقول : كلما صدق: لاشيء من ب ، ج و كل الف ب صدق لاشيء من ج، الف لانه لولم يصدق، الصدق نقيضه اعني بعض ج، الف فنضمه مع كبرى القياس هكذا بعض ج، الف وكل الف، ب ينتج: بعض ج ب وينعكس الى قولنا بعض ب ج وقد كان حكم الصغرى: لاشيء من ب، ج، هف. و اما في الاخيرين فيجوز ان يعمل بكلا الطريقين اما بالاول : فلا يجاب صغريهما وكلية نقيضي
As for the first two (moods), the contradictory of the conclusion is made as the major premise, and the minor premise of the syllogism is taken as it is, as in the reductio used in the third figure. Then from the first figure a conclusion is produced that converts to something contradicting the major premise of the syllogism. For example, we say: Whenever it is true that 'Every B is C' and 'Every A or some A is B', then 'Some C is A' is true. Its contradictory is 'No C is A'. We combine it with the minor premise of the syllogism thus: 'Every B is C' and 'No C is A' produces 'No B is A', which converts to 'No A is B'. But the ruling of the major premise was that 'Every A or some A is B'. Contradiction. As for the third (mood), the contradictory of the conclusion is made as the minor premise, and the major premise of the syllogism is kept universal as in the reductio mentioned in the first figure. Then from the first figure a conclusion is produced that converts to something contradicting the minor premise. For example, we say: Whenever it is true that 'No B is C' and 'Every A is B', then 'No C is A' is true. For if it were not true, then its contradictory, i.e., 'Some C is A', would be true. We combine it with the major premise thus: 'Some C is A' and 'Every A is B' produces 'Some C is B', which converts to 'Some B is C'. But the ruling of the minor premise was that 'No B is C'. Contradiction. As for the last two (moods), it is permissible to work with both methods: either by the first, so their minors are not taken as universal, and the universal of the contradictories...
Source pages p. 355

Commentary
This passage describes the method of reductio ad absurdum (خلف) to verify the conclusions of three groups of moods: the first two moods, the third mood, and the last two moods (probably the fourth and fifth or sixth and seventh). The commentator explains the procedure step by step. For the first two moods (which produce particular affirmative conclusions), assume the contradictory of the conclusion (i.e., a universal negative), combine it with the original minor premise to form a syllogism in the first figure, which yields a conclusion that, when converted, contradicts the original major premise. For example, if the original moods had premises 'Every B is C' and 'Every A is B' (or 'Some A is B'), the conclusion is 'Some C is A'. Its contradictory 'No C is A' is assumed. With the minor 'Every B is C', we derive 'No B is A' (Cesare?), which converts to 'No A is B', contradicting the major premise that 'Every A is B' (or that some A is B). For the third mood (universal negative conclusion), the contradictory (particular affirmative) is assumed as the minor, combined with the original major premise (universal affirmative), yielding a conclusion that converts to contradict the original minor premise. For example, if the original premises are 'No B is C' and 'Every A is B', conclusion 'No C is A'. Its contradictory 'Some C is A' with the major 'Every A is B' gives 'Some C is B', which converts to 'Some B is C', contradicting the minor 'No B is C'. For the last two moods (probably the ones producing particular negative), either method works. This is a standard logical technique to demonstrate validity by showing that denying the conclusion leads to inconsistency with the premises.

Key Terms
خلف
نقيض
انعكاس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The contradictory of the major premise
B) The contradictory of the conclusion
C) The contradictory of the minor premise
D) The original conclusion

Key Takeaway
Reductio ad absurdum is a reliable method to verify categorical syllogisms by assuming the contradictory of the conclusion and deriving a contradiction with the premises.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the syllogism: 'All intoxicants are forbidden' (major), 'All wine is an intoxicant' (minor), therefore 'All wine is forbidden'. Apply reductio by assuming the contradictory of the conclusion ('Some wine is not forbidden') and combine with the minor premise to derive a contradiction with the major premise. Write the steps.
?
